201 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Texas Eastern Transmission Corp. v. Tax Appeals Tribunal


No. 98


COURT OF APPEALS OF NEW YORK


95 N.Y.2d 323; 740 N.E.2d 214; 717 N.Y.S.2d 69; 2000 N.Y. LEXIS 2908; 146 Oil & Gas Rep. 425


September 6, 2000, Argued  
 October 19, 2000, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff company was taxed as a merchant and appealed the decision of the Supreme Court, Appellate Division (New York) which rejected plaintiff's claim that N.Y. Tax Law § 186 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff company, owner and operator of a natural gas pipeline system which spanned numerous states, sought a tax refund for three specific years it had been taxed. Plaintiff challenged the tax as violative of the Commerce Clause, U.S. Const. Art. I, § 8, cl. 3. The trial court held that because N.Y. Tax Law § 186 taxed gross earnings from New York sources only, plaintiff had not demonstrated that the tax was unconstitutional. Plaintiff contended that because, nationwide, it transported a higher volume of natural gas as a common carrier than it sold as a merchant, it should have been taxed as a transportation business under N.Y. Tax Law §§ 183, 184 rather than as a merchant under § 186. The court affirmed the decision because the record failed to demonstrate how the taxed income was grossly distorted or out of all proportion to the company's business in New York.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed because plaintiff failed to demonstrate that taxation under the statute was not fairly apportioned, and therefore failed to show that tax violated the Commerce Clause.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law - Validity of Statute - Tax on Natural Gas Suppliers - Commerce Clause

Petitioner, an out-of-State corporation with a natural gas pipeline system extending over several States which was required to pay franchise taxes as a gas supplier pursuant to Tax Law § 186 because it derived greater than 50% of its gross receipts from the sale of gas, has failed to demonstrate how taxation under Tax Law § 186 violates the Commerce Clause of the United States Constitution (US Const, art I, § 8, cl [3]). In determining whether a State tax unduly burdens interstate commerce, it must be determined whether the tax is fairly apportioned so as to prevent each State from taxing any more than its fair share of an interstate transaction. Petitioner challenges the tax insofar as it applied to gross earnings from sales of natural gas delivered in New York, without apportioning a segment of those gross earnings to activities outside the State that contributed to the company's New York sales. Although the record establishes that Tax Law § 186 taxed only gross earnings from New York sources, it does not demonstrate the extent to which gross earnings from New York sources came from sales, transportation, or other sources. Even assuming, arguendo, that the "overwhelming amount" of gross earnings came from sales, the record still fails to demonstrate how the income attributed to New York is grossly distorted or out of all proportion to petitioner's business in New York.

 

202 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Bros. v. Florence


No. 112, No. 113, No. 118, No. 141


COURT OF APPEALS OF NEW YORK


95 N.Y.2d 290; 739 N.E.2d 733; 716 N.Y.S.2d 367; 2000 N.Y. LEXIS 2910


September 13, 2000, Argued and Submitted  
October 19, 2000, Decided



DISPOSITION: Case No. 112: Order affirmed, with costs. Case No. 113: Order, insofar as appealed from, reversed, with costs, and the motion of the Rossback defendants for summary judgment dismissing the complaint as against them denied. Case No. 118: Order affirmed, with costs. Case No. 141: Order reversed, with costs, and defendants' motion to dismiss the complaint denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants challenged, in a consolidated appeal, New York trial court rulings finding that their nonmedical malpractice actions were time-barred by an amendment to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 214(6) shortening the limitations period applicable to such actions.


OVERVIEW: Appellants brought various nonmedical malpractice claims. The limitations period applicable to such claims was shortened by an amendment to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 214(6), and the trial courts found that appellants' claims were time-barred. Appellants sought review. The statutory amendment was intended to apply both retrospectively and prospectively. Due process required that a party bringing such a claim had to be given a reasonable period to bring their action before it was time-barred. Since the legislature did not establish such a grace period, it was the proper function of the courts to do so. A case-by-case approach to this determination did not provide adequate notice to potential litigants or trial courts. Balancing the legislative intent of the amendment, the reasonable expectations of potential claimants, and the importance of the subject matter affected, an outside one-year grace period was appropriate.


OUTCOME: Some of the trial court rulings were affirmed and some were reversed by the appellate court's determination that nonmedical malpractice claims barred as of the effective date of the amendment to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 214(6), shortening the limitations period, had the shorter of the remaining time under the former six-year limitations period or one year from the amendment's effective date to sue.


HEADNOTES

Statutes - Retroactive Application of Statute - Three-Year Statute of Limitations for Nonmedical Malpractice Actions - Remedial Nature of Statutory Amendment

1. It was the intention of the Legislature that an amendment to CPLR 214 (6), which shortened the limitations period in nonmedical malpractice claims to three years regardless of whether the underlying theory is based in contract or tort, and which was to "take effect immediately," was to apply to claims which had accrued before its effective date but were not commenced until after that date. The history of the amendment indicates that its purpose was to rewrite a judicial interpretation of the nonmedical malpractice Statute of Limitations and to "reaffirm" the original legislative intent for a universally applied three-year limitations period in all malpractice cases other than those brought against professional health care providers. The Legislature determined that a six-year period was unduly prolonged, and the amendment was seen as necessary to remediate the impact of judicial decisions which had held that nonmedical malpractice claims, when based on a contract theory, were governed by the six-year limitations period applicable to contract actions. The remedial purpose of the amendment would be undermined if it were applied only prospectively.

Statutes - Retroactive Application of Statute - Three-Year Statute of Limitations for Nonmedical Malpractice Actions - Procedural Due Process - Reasonable Time Requirement

2. It was the intention of the Legislature that an amendment to CPLR 214 (6), which shortened the limitations period in nonmedical malpractice claims to three years regardless of whether the underlying theory is based in contract or tort, was to apply to claims which had accrued before its effective date but were not commenced until after that date. Although a potential litigant has no vested interest in or right to a specific limitations period, when such a period is statutorily shortened or created, due process requires that potential litigants be afforded a reasonable time to commence their actions before the bar takes effect. Where an amendment does not contain a legislatively prescribed grace period, courts may uphold the constitutional validity of a retrospective application of a new statute by interpreting it as authorizing suits upon otherwise time-barred claims within a reasonable time after the statute's effective date.

Statutes - Retroactive Application of Statute - Three-Year Statute of Limitations for Nonmedical Malpractice Actions - Procedural Due Process - Reasonable Time Requirement - General Rule

3. It was the intention of the Legislature that an amendment to CPLR 214 (6), which shortened the limitations period in nonmedical malpractice claims to three years regardless of whether the underlying theory is based in contract or tort, was to apply to claims which had accrued before its effective date but were not commenced until after that date. Where an amendment does not contain a legislatively prescribed grace period, courts may uphold the constitutional validity of a retrospective application of a new statute by interpreting it as authorizing suits upon otherwise time-barred claims within a reasonable time after the statute's effective date. In determining what constitutes a reasonable time, a general rule should be applied to all cases in preference to a case-by-case analysis. The shortcomings of a case-by-case approach are that it fails to provide adequate and clear notice and guidance to potential litigants, as well as to lower courts, of what might constitute a reasonable time and all but inevitably results in uneven application. Accordingly, the Court shall make a balanced determination of what definite time period would fairly be necessary to afford a reasonable opportunity to interpose nonmedical malpractice claims, and then apply that period generally to all otherwise time-barred suits.

Statutes - Retroactive Application of Statute - Three-Year Statute of Limitations for Nonmedical Malpractice Actions - Procedural Due Process - Reasonable Time Requirement - One Year or Time Remaining under Previous Statute 

4. An amendment to CPLR 214 (6), which shortened the limitations period in all nonmedical malpractice claims to three years, applies to claims which had accrued before its effective date but were not commenced until after that date. Given the absence of a legislatively prescribed grace period, the Court shall determine what constitutes a reasonable time after the statute's effective date within which affected litigants may commence an action. The goal of the Legislature in amending subdivision (6) was immediately to curtail application of a six-year period of limitations in nonmedical malpractice cases resulting from two judicial decisions. Giving accrued nonmedical malpractice claims the shorter of the time remaining under the former six-year period or three full years from the 1996 amendment's effective date fails to accomplish the intended result. Moreover, fairness to litigants whose claims would otherwise be time-barred upon the effective date does not require extending the new limitations period to the shorter of either six years from accrual or three years from the amendment's effective date. However, giving nonmedical malpractice plaintiffs immediately barred as of the effective date of the amendment the shorter of either the remaining time under the former six-year limitations period or one year from the amendment's effective date in which to commence their actions, strikes the appropriate balance between State and litigants' personal interests for procedural due process purposes, comports with the legislative goal and the reasonable expectations of potential claimants, and gives due consideration to the importance of the "subject matter" of the claims affected.

Statutes - Retroactive Application of Statute - Three-Year Statute of Limitations for Nonmedical Malpractice Actions - Procedural Due Process - Reasonable Time Requirement - Claims Not Immediately Barred

5. An amendment to CPLR 214 (6), which shortened the limitations period in all nonmedical malpractice claims to three years, applies to claims which had accrued before its effective date but were not commenced until after that date. Due process requires that litigants affected by the amendment be given a reasonable time within which to commence their actions. Cases not immediately barred by the amendment need to be examined to see if the time remaining provides a reasonable opportunity to interpose a claim. Four months is unreasonably brief; such a drastic curtailment of a plaintiff's right to sue is not dictated in order to fulfill the legislative objective. Time barring a plaintiff for a four-month delay presents an element of unfairness given that other plaintiffs whose actions would have been immediately barred by the application of the amendment are to be given up to one year from the effective date to sue, thereby treating a diligent litigant more harshly than a less diligent one. Accordingly, the same bright-line rule developed for litigants whose claims were immediately extinguished by the amendment shall apply to those whose claims temporarily survived the amendment; they shall have no less than one year after the amendment's effective date.

 

203 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hansen


No. 115


COURT OF APPEALS OF NEW YORK


95 N.Y.2d 227; 738 N.E.2d 773; 715 N.Y.S.2d 369; 2000 N.Y. LEXIS 2911


September 14, 2000, Argued  
October 19, 2000, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant requested review of a judgment by the Appellate Division (New York) which affirmed his conviction of burglary and related charges.


OVERVIEW: The victim reported he heard noise and called 911, saw defendant kick in victim's sliding glass door while holding a snow shovel, and that he struggled with defendant, prior to defendant running away. Appellant was apprehended after a chase near the victim's home. Appellant claimed he was strung out and shoveling victim's snow. Portions of video tape of remarks of a news reporter covering the burglary were presented to the grand jury. The grand jury returned an indictment. Defendant pled guilty to burglary and related charges and was convicted. The court affirmed his conviction on appeal. While defendant's constitutional right to be prosecuted on a jurisdictionally valid indictment survived the guilty plea, his right to challenge evidence in the fact-finding process engaged in by the grand jurors did not. Inadmissible evidence, inadvertently introduced into criminal proceedings, did not necessarily alter the validity of the proceedings; rather, such a defect rendered the indictment dismissible when the remaining evidence was insufficient to sustain the indictment. After a guilty plea, however, the sufficiency of the evidence before the grand jury could not be challenged.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed. While defendant's constitutional right to be prosecuted on a jurisdictionally valid indictment survived his guilty plea, his right to challenge evidence in the fact-finding process engaged in by the grand jurors did not. The challenged evidence was a video tape of remarks of a news reporter covering the burglary.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Plea of Guilty - Forfeiture of Right to Raise Issues on Appeal - Impairment of Grand Jury Fact-Finding Process

Defendant, by pleading guilty, forfeited the right to contend that the fact-finding process of the Grand Jury was impaired by the prosecutor's introduction of inadmissible videotaped hearsay, since defendant's claim does not activate a question of jurisdiction or a constitutional defect implicating the integrity of the process. While defendant's constitutional right to be prosecuted on a jurisdictionally valid indictment survived the guilty plea, his right to challenge this evidence did not. To allow such a right to survive would be fundamentally inconsistent with the plea of guilty, because the claim essentially relates to the quantum of proof required to satisfy the factual elements of the crimes considered by the Grand Jury.

 

204 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Barr v. Crosson


No. 75


COURT OF APPEALS OF NEW YORK


95 N.Y.2d 164; 733 N.E.2d 217; 711 N.Y.S.2d 145; 2000 N.Y. LEXIS 1374


May 4, 2000, Argued  
June 20, 2000, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, without costs, plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment denied, defendants' motion for summary judgment granted and judgment granted declaring the subject salary disparity constitutional.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant state officials appealed a decision of the Supreme Court, Appellate Division, Fourth Department (New York), that granted plaintiff Monroe County Court judges' summary judgment motion and declared unconstitutional salary disparities between plaintiffs and Albany County judges. Plaintiffs cross-appealed denial of pre-judgment interest.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff Monroe County judges challenged disparities in New York's judicial salary schedules that resulted in their being paid five percent less than judges in nearby Albany County. In response to their equal protection challenge, defendant state officials argued significant cost of living differences. The court, applying a rational basis level of scrutiny, held that if economic differentials in median home values and per capita income supported a salary disparity, it was constitutional. The intermediate appellate court had improperly looked to a totality of economic indicators test. Looking only to home values and per capita income, the court found large cost of living differentials between the two counties justified a five percent salary differential.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order, denied summary judgment to plaintiffs, and granted defendants' summary judgment motion. The court held the disparity constitutional, because there was a rational basis for the disparate schedules.


HEADNOTES

Judges - Judicial Salaries - Pay Disparity - Equal Protection - Rational Basis - Albany and Monroe Counties

The disparity between the salaries of County Court Judges in Albany and Monroe Counties survives equal protection constitutional scrutiny, since sufficient record evidence of reliable statistics indicating a rational economic basis for the disparate salaries exists to support the geographical pay differential. Economic differentials in median home values and per capita income can alone provide a rational basis for a salary disparity. Here, data indicating that the median home value was between 34% and 50% higher in Albany County than in Monroe County, and that Albany County Court handled 46% more filings and 45% more dispositions per Judge than Monroe County Court provide a rational basis for the less than 5% pay differential. Plaintiffs have therefore failed to satisfy their heavy burden of proving that there is no reasonably conceivable state of facts which rationally supports the distinction. Moreover, a finding of unconstitutionality may not be based on an application of the "totality of economic indicators" test, since that test has been expressly rejected as contrary to the essential governing rational basis test.

 

205 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Francois v. Dolan


No. 46


COURT OF APPEALS OF NEW YORK


95 N.Y.2d 33; 731 N.E.2d 614; 709 N.Y.S.2d 898; 2000 N.Y. LEXIS 908


March 29, 2000, Argued  
May 18, 2000, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner appealed the judgment of the Supreme Court, Appellate Division (New York), which dismissed his petition in a N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceeding for an order directing the county court to entertain his plea of guilty to all counts of the indictment charging him with capital murder.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner sought an order directing the county court to entertain his plea of guilty to all counts of the indictment charging him with capital murder. The appellate court dismissed the petition, holding that mandamus did not lie because petitioner failed to demonstrate a clear legal right to the relief sought. On appeal, the court found that the legislative scheme did not support the theory that a person indicted for capital murder had an unqualified right, by pleading guilty to the indictment, to thwart the statutory authority of a district attorney to make a fully deliberative decision whether to seek the death penalty, within the 120-day period after arraignment prescribed by N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 250.40(2). Until the completion of the statutorily provided deliberative process, either by the filing of a death penalty notice, announcement of an intention not to seek that sanction, or by the expiration of the statutory period to make that decision, a capital defendant did not have an unqualified right to plead guilty to the entire indictment.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed; petitioner had no unqualified statutory right to plead guilty to all counts of the indictment charging him with capital murder, before the filing of a notice of intent to seek the death penalty and prior to the expiration of the statutory period within which such notice may have been filed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Plea of Guilty - Capital Murder - Mandamus to Compel Acceptance of Plea

Mandamus does not lie to compel a County Court to entertain petitioner's offer to plead guilty to all counts of the indictment charging him with capital murder, before the filing by the District Attorney of a notice of intent to seek the death penalty and prior to the expiration of the 120-day statutory period within which such notice may be filed (CPL 250.40 [2]), notwithstanding that CPL 220.10 (2) and 220.60 (2) generally permit defendants to plead guilty to an entire indictment upon arraignment and at any time before verdict. Until the completion of the statutorily provided deliberative process, either by the filing of a death penalty notice, announcement of an intention not to seek that sanction, or by the expiration of the statutory period to make that decision, a capital defendant does not have an unqualified right to plead guilty to the entire indictment. If petitioner had an unqualified right to plead guilty to an entire capital crime indictment, two critical powers conferred on the District Attorney in the 1995 death penalty legislation could be preempted. The defendant could thereby prevent the prosecution from pursuing the death penalty even after a notice of intent to seek the death penalty was filed under CPL 250.40 (1), and could preclude the District Attorney from even exercising the statutory right to consider, over time, whether to seek the ultimate sanction in a capital murder case. Moreover, giving precedence to the subsequently enacted, specific provision of section 250.40 over the earlier enacted, more general provisions of CPL 220.10 (2) and 220.60 (2) is consistent with the canon of statutory interpretation that what is special or particular in the later of two statutes supersedes as an exception whatever in the earlier statute is unlimited or general.

 

206 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Bluebird Partners, L.P. v. First Fid. Bank, N.A.


No. 40


COURT OF APPEALS OF NEW YORK


94 N.Y.2d 726; 731 N.E.2d 581; 709 N.Y.S.2d 865; 2000 N.Y. LEXIS 897


March 28, 2000, Argued  
 May 11, 2000, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order, insofar as appealed from, reversed, with costs, and motion by defendant United Jersey Bank to dismiss the complaint denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged order of Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, which found that the primary purpose of appellant's purchase of second series certificates of an airline company was champertous and dismissed appellant's complaint of a breach of fiduciary duty against respondent bank.


OVERVIEW: The lower court dismissed appellant's breach of fiduciary action against respondent bank on an appeal of respondent's motion to dismiss, finding that appellant had purchased second series certificates of an airline company in bankruptcy in order to bring a suit against respondent. The appellate court concluded that the record did not support a finding of champerty under N.Y. Jud. Law § 489 as a matter of law for summary resolution because it could not be determined on the record and in the procedural posture of the case that champerty was appellant's primary motivation or sole basis for acquiring the second series certificates. Thus, dismissal of the complaint was improper at this stage. The lower court's order was reversed and respondent's motion to dismiss on champerty grounds was denied.


OUTCOME: Order reversed because it could not be determined on the record and in the procedural posture of the case that champerty was appellant's primary motivation or sole basis for acquiring the second series certificates; respondent's motion to dismiss was denied.


HEADNOTES

Champerty and Maintenance - Purchase of Investment Certificates - Intent to Sue - Primary Purpose - Summary Disposition

1. In an action by the holder of a series of certificates of corporate indebtedness, alleging a breach of fiduciary duty against the trustee of that series of certificates, it cannot be said, as a matter of law, that plaintiff's purchases of the certificates were in violation of the prohibition against champerty contained in Judiciary Law § 489. Section 489 requires that the acquisition of a claim be made with the intent and for the purpose of bringing an action or proceeding. It is impossible to determine whether the acquisition of the claims against defendant was an incidental part of, or the driving force behind, plaintiff's purchases of the certificates where plaintiff has offered various reasons for its purchases: to gain leverage to settle a related action; to profit on the certificates, which were purchased at a greatly reduced cost; and to at least be economically indifferent as to the result of the related action. By purchasing the certificates, plaintiff could have simply been covering varying risks and potentialities, and its reasons qualify as a sufficient business motivation for the purchases. Although plaintiff admitted that a lawsuit against defendant was a consideration at the time plaintiff began to purchase the certificates, the decision to sue cannot automatically become synonymous with champerty, for litigation can be an appropriate and commonly used strategy. Moreover, the Appellate Division found evidence of a litigation-oriented "primary purpose" on the basis of a plainly mistaken fact and an inappropriate credibility determination. A finding of champerty as a matter of law might engender uncertainties in the free market system in connection with untold numbers of sophisticated business transactions.

Champerty and Maintenance - Purchase of Investment Certificates - Intent to Sue - Primary Purpose

2. In order to constitute champertous conduct in the acquisition of rights that would then be nullified in violation of Judiciary Law § 489, the foundational intent to sue on that claim must at least have been the primary purpose for, if not the sole motivation behind, entering into the transaction. The words, "sole" and "primary," are not synonymous generally or in law. A purpose that is the sole purpose is, by necessity, the primary purpose. However, a purpose that is primary is not necessarily the sole purpose. Yet, the distinction is one without a legal difference when the "primary" element is present. Section 489 requires that the acquisition be made with the intent and for the purpose of bringing an action or proceeding.

 

207 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Couser


No. 62


COURT OF APPEALS OF NEW YORK


94 N.Y.2d 631; 730 N.E.2d 953; 709 N.Y.S.2d 155; 2000 N.Y. LEXIS 887


April 6, 2000, Argued  
May 4, 2000, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at, Remitted by People v. Couser, 2003 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2993 (N.Y. App. Div. 4th Dep't, Mar. 21, 2003)



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, indicted for murder, claimed the Appellate Division (New York) erred by reversing a county court's finding that the definition of the term "command" contained in N.Y. Penal Law § 125.27(1)(a)(vii) was speculative and unconstitutionally vague.


OVERVIEW: The appeal required a determination of whether the statutory meaning of the word "command" contained in N.Y. Penal Law § 125.27(1)(a)(vii) was impermissibly vague. The case evolved out of plaintiff People's theory that defendant, from his jail cell, commanded the murder of a material witness. The lower court found that the term "command" had a commonly understood meaning that met standard due process strictures. Defendant's claim that the term "commanded," as transposed over into N.Y. Penal Law § 125.27, was unconstitutionally vague was rejected. The term had a common-sense core of meaning that criminal juries would have been capable of understanding. Evidence presented to the grand jury was legally sufficient to support the indictment.


OUTCOME: Judgment holding that the murder count against defendant should proceed to trial was affirmed. The term "command" survived the standard due process constitutional attack and was not unconstitutionally vague. Sufficient evidence was adduced to support the indictment.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law - Validity of Statute - Commander Portion of First Degree Murder Statute - To "Command" Death of Victim Not Unconstitutionally Vague

1. The word "command," as used to define felony first degree murder in Penal Law § 125.27 (1) (a) (vii), has a commonly accepted meaning, is not unconstitutionally vague, and survives the standard due process constitutional attack. The commonly accepted meaning of "command" for ordinary legal and constitutional purposes is "to direct authoritatively," and it is significant that a "command" is the only type of accomplice liability activity for which a confederate may become liable for first degree murder. That selective precision manifests the Legislature's confidence and understanding that "command" is a distinctive term which is different from "solicits," "requests," or "importunes." Moreover, "commands" has been retained throughout the development of the accomplice liability statute, while other terms have been recast.

Crimes - Murder - To "Command" Death of Victim - Standing to Make Constitutional Vagueness Challenge

2. Although defendant, who has been charged with first degree felony murder, has not yet been convicted of anything and is not subject to a death sentence in this case, he has standing to make a standard due process vagueness assertion that the word "command" as used in Penal Law § 125.27 (1) (a) (vii) is unconstitutionally vague.

Crimes - Indictment - Sufficiency of Evidence Before Grand Jury - Murder - To "Command" Death of Victim

3. The evidence presented to the Grand Jury was legally sufficient to support the count of an indictment charging defendant with felony first degree murder for allegedly commanding the death of another, where a witness, who was involved in the murder, testified that the actual killer told him that defendant had ordered the killing from his jail cell, that he had learned that it was defendant who had ordered others to travel to another city to kill witnesses against defendant, that he was subsequently incarcerated in the same jail as defendant, and that defendant had made incriminating statements to him about the murder.

 

208 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Foley


No. 17


COURT OF APPEALS OF NEW YORK


94 N.Y.2d 668; 731 N.E.2d 123; 709 N.Y.S.2d 467; 2000 N.Y. LEXIS 521


February 16, 2000, Argued and Submitted  
April 11, 2000, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Certiorari Denied October 2, 2000, Reported at: 2000 U.S. LEXIS 5898.
Writ of certiorari denied Foley v. New York, 531 U.S. 875, 121 S. Ct. 181, 148 L. Ed. 2d 124, 2000 U.S. LEXIS 5898 (2000)
Post-conviction proceeding at People v. Foley, 2006 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 15478 (N.Y. App. Div. 4th Dep't, Dec. 22, 2006)



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed New York appellate division's affirmance of convictions for promoting a sexual performance by children and attempted disseminating of indecent material contending N.Y. Penal Law §§235.22, 263.15 proscribing predatory pedophile activity utilizing internet technology was unconstitutionally overbroad and vague, and content-based restriction of U.S. Const. amend. I.


OVERVIEW: The legitimate reach of N.Y. Penal Law §235.22 outweighed its arguably impermissible applications. A person of ordinary intelligence could reasonably know that the statute was meant to prevent the intentional luring of minors to engage in sexual conduct through the dissemination of harmful, sexual images. It was not unconstitutionally vague or overbroad. The court held the statute survived first amendment strict scrutiny, because it curtailed the use of speech in a way which did not merit first amendment protection. Although the statute could incidentally burden some protected expression, it served the compelling interest of preventing the sexual abuse of children and was no broader than necessary. N.Y. Penal Law §235.22 did not discriminate against or burden interstate trade, it regulated the conduct of individuals who intended to use the internet to endanger the welfare of children. The statute was a valid exercise of the state's general police powers.


OUTCOME: Order affirmed. Statute prohibiting endangerment of children through dissemination of sexually graphic material over internet and by intentionally luring minors to engage in sexual conduct did not merit first amendment protection, was not unconstitutionally overbroad or vague, was valid exercise of state's police power, and did not burden interstate commerce.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Disseminating Indecent Material to Minors - Constitutionality - Overbreadth

1. Penal Law § 235.22, which criminalizes the use of sexually explicit communications, via a computer communications system, designed to lure children into harmful conduct, is not overbroad on the ground that it exposes individuals to criminal liability who unintentionally address a minor through sexually-oriented communication. Under the First Amendment overbreadth doctrine, statutes attempting to restrict or burden the exercise of First Amendment rights must be narrowly drawn, but a statute is subjected to less scrutiny where the behavior sought to be prohibited by the State moves from pure speech toward conduct and that conduct--even if expressive--falls within the scope of otherwise valid criminal laws that reflect legitimate State interests. Where conduct and not merely speech is involved, the overbreadth doctrine can be invoked only where the overbreadth is substantial. Section 235.22 is not directed at the mere transmission of certain types of communication over the Internet. The terms "importune," "invite" or "induce" used in the second part of the statute--the "luring prong"--describe acts of communication, not the content of one's views, and they identify forms of conduct which may provide a predicate for criminal liability. Moreover, the statute should be read as requiring that an individual intend to initiate this kind of communication with a minor and thereby further intend to "importune, invite or induce" the minor to engage in sexual conduct for the sender's benefit. The term "harmful to minors" found in Penal Law § 235.22 (1) is not overbroad on the ground that it allows New York to impose its community standards nationwide. Thus, the legitimate reach of Penal Law § 235.22 outweighs its arguably impermissible applications.

Crimes - Disseminating Indecent Material to Minors - Constitutionality - Overbreadth of Term "Harmful to Minors"

2. With respect to Penal Law § 235.22, entitled "Disseminating indecent material to minors in the first degree," which was enacted to address the convergence of predatory pedophile activity with Internet technology, the term "harmful to minors," used to describe the type of communication deemed indecent, is not overbroad on the ground that it allows New York to impose its community standards nationwide. Rather, that term was specifically defined according to the guidelines enunciated by the Supreme Court in Miller v California (413 US 15), and was further limited to "actual or simulated nudity, sexual conduct or sado-masochistic abuse" (see also, Penal Law § 235.20 [2], [3], [5]). It is difficult to envision a situation where that conduct would not be considered harmful to minors outside New York when the statute seeks specifically to prohibit the intentional dissemination of this type of material to a minor in conjunction with the sender's enticement or invitation to the child to engage in sexual activity.

Crimes - Disseminating Indecent Material to Minors - Constitutionality - Predatory Pedophile Activity on Internet - Vagueness

3. Penal Law § 235.22, which is entitled "Disseminating indecent material to minors in the first degree," and was enacted to address the convergence of predatory pedophile activity with Internet technology, is not unconstitutional on the ground of vagueness. The phrases "importunes, invites or induces," and "sexual conduct for [the defendant's] benefit," used to describe how and why a defendant influences the conduct of his minor victim, provide adequate notice of what is prohibited and permit evenhanded application of the law both by law enforcement authorities and fact finders called to interpret the speech at issue. Each and every term in the statute is either defined in the Penal Law (see, § 235.20) or has a plain and ordinary meaning. A person of ordinary intelligence would reasonably know that the statute is meant to prevent the intentional luring of minors to engage in sexual conduct through the dissemination of harmful, sexual images. There is no possibility of arbitrary or discriminatory enforcement: the combination of precise terms and the clearly pronounced elements adequately define the criminal conduct. The statute employs objective, ascertainable standards which do not provide room for law enforcement officials to apply the statute based upon their own personal ideas of right and wrong.

Crimes - Disseminating Indecent Material to Minors - Constitutionality - Predatory Pedophile Activity on Internet - Content-Based Restriction - Strict Scrutiny

4. Penal Law § 235.22, which is entitled "Disseminating indecent material to minors in the first degree," and was enacted to address the convergence of predatory pedophile activity with Internet technology, is not an unconstitutional content-based restriction. Although the statute is content-based, since it affects sexually explicit communication, it survives First Amendment strict scrutiny because it curtails the use of speech in a way which does not merit First Amendment protection, and is a carefully tailored means of serving a compelling State interest of protecting children from sexual exploitation. The speech conduct sought to be prohibited by the statute--the endangerment of children through the dissemination of sexually graphic material over the Internet--does not merit First Amendment protection, and, in any event, the statute does not effectuate a total ban on the dissemination of sexual content communication, but merely limits its use. While the statute may incidentally burden some protected expression in carrying out its objective, it serves the compelling interest of preventing the sexual abuse of children and is no broader than necessary to achieve its purpose.

Crimes - Disseminating Indecent Material to Minors - Constitutionality - Predatory Pedophile Activity on Internet - Commerce Clause - Burden on Interstate Trade

5. Penal Law § 235.22, which is entitled "Disseminating indecent material to minors in the first degree," and was enacted to address the convergence of predatory pedophile activity with Internet technology, does not unduly burden interstate trade in violation of the Commerce Clause. The statute, which prohibits individuals from luring children into sexual conduct, does not discriminate against or burden interstate trade; it regulates the conduct of individuals who intend to use the Internet to endanger the welfare of children. There is no legitimate commerce that is derived from the intentional transmission of sexually graphic images to minors for the purpose of luring them into sexual activity. The conduct sought to be sanctioned by the statute is of the sort that deserves no "economic" protection. Thus, section 235.22 is a valid exercise of the State's general police powers.

Crimes - Promoting Sexual Performance by Child - Constitutionality - Overbreadth

6. Penal Law § 263.15, which prohibits the promotion of any performance which includes sexual conduct by a child under the age of 16, is not unconstitutional on the ground of overbreadth. The statutory scheme allows the fact finder to make a determination on the evidence submitted whether the performance involves an individual under 16, and defendant was permitted to introduce expert testimony challenging whether the images he transmitted to the undercover police officer posing as a 15-year-old girl actually depicted children or had been digitally spliced to "manufacture" the picture of a child performing a sexual act. Of the many images transmitted by defendant, defendant's expert could point to only one that may have been digitally altered, and defendant did not object to the court's charge concerning the proof of age of the children in the pictures. The jury was thus instructed to consider, from the evidence before it, if a child who participated in the performance was under 16. Accordingly, the statute is not unconstitutionally overbroad as applied to defendant.

 

209 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


City of New York v. State


No. 16


COURT OF APPEALS OF NEW YORK


94 N.Y.2d 577; 730 N.E.2d 920; 709 N.Y.S.2d 122; 2000 N.Y. LEXIS 512


February 15, 2000, Argued  
 April 4, 2000, Decided



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the order of the Appellate Division should be modified, without costs, by declaring that Chapter 5 of the Laws of 1999 does not violate the home rule provisions of the State Constitution, and as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city challenged a decision in favor of appellee state allowing it to make a legislative amendment to N.Y. Gen. City Law § 25-m et seq. (1966), asserting that home rule privileges allowed taxation of commuting workers even if they were from different counties.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to its legislative rights under N.Y. Gen. City Law § 25-m et seq. (1966), appellant city imposed a commuter tax on all workers within its boundaries that were non-residents. The statute was eventually amended by N.Y. Tax Law § 1305(b) (1999), whereupon counties no longer could tax workers coming from a different county within the city. Appellant challenged the constitutionality of this amendment, arguing that common-law provisions under the home rule doctrine allowed it to tax workers from other counties. The supreme court denied the legislative challenge, holding that the amendment passed the requirements of the Adler test. Upon final determination, the Court of Appeals affirmed, finding a reasonable relationship present between a substantial state concern to remove unnecessary taxes and a reasonable relationship to that concern in the form of commercial freedom.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed on the grounds that the lower court did not err in finding a reasonable relationship between an amendment removing appellant's city imposed tax where the goal behind the amendment was to provide for the cessation of a commercial burden.


HEADNOTES

Taxation - Personal Income Tax - Repeal of New York City Commuter Tax on State Residents Who Work in New York City - Delegation of Legislative Responsibility - New York City Administrative Code

1. Chapter 5 of the Laws of 1999, which rescinded the personal income tax imposed by the City of New York on State resident commuters who work in the City while retaining the tax on out-of-State commuters who work in the City, and further provided that if a court declared the new law void, the entire tax authorization statute would be repealed, is not invalid on the ground that since it did not repeal the sections of the Administrative Code of the City of New York which implemented the tax, those provisions still have life, since chapter 5 repealed the authority on which the Code provisions are based; therefore, they are no longer effective. Moreover, chapter 5 does not unlawfully delegate the Legislature's power by allowing a court to effectuate a repeal of the tax. Legislative anticipation of judicial interpretation of a statute is nothing new. Although the anticipation is ordinarily reflected in severability clauses that call for a legislative act to continue if one section is stricken, the direction that the entire statute falls if certain sections are invalidated was an appropriate legislative choice, and cannot be construed as delegating legislative power to the judiciary.

Taxation - Personal Income Tax - Repeal of New York City Commuter Tax on State Residents Who Work in New York City - Home Rule Message Not Required for State Legislative Action

2. Chapter 5 of the Laws of 1999, which rescinded the personal income tax imposed by the City of New York on State resident commuters who work in the City while retaining the tax on out-of-State commuters who work in the City, and further provided that if a court declared the new law void, the entire tax authorization statute would be repealed, does not violate the home rule provisions of the State Constitution (art IX, § 2 [b] [2]). While chapter 5 is concededly a special law applying only to the City, it did not require a home rule message since it is supported by a substantial and legitimate State interest: to provide tax relief to State residents who live outside the City and to make the City more attractive for investment and growth, both goals with obvious implications for the State. The power to tax rests solely with the Legislature, and although the City alleges that the true motivation of chapter 5 was political, a court of law cannot inquire into the motives by which law was produced, since speculation on the "political" motivation of the Legislature as a judicial construct for statutory analysis would be a slippery and dangerous slope. Rather, the court shall rely on the stated purpose and legislative history of an act to find or reject a substantial State concern.

Taxation - Personal Income Tax - Repeal of New York City Commuter Tax on State Residents Who Work in New York City - Home Rule Message Not Required for State Legislative Action - Enactment of Tax Pursuant to Home Rule Message Not Determinative of Need for Message on Repeal

3. Chapter 5 of the Laws of 1999, which rescinded the personal income tax imposed by the City of New York on State resident commuters who work in the City while retaining the tax on out-of-State commuters who work in the City, and further provided that if a court declared the new law void, the entire tax authorization statute would be repealed, does not violate the home rule provisions of the State Constitution (art IX, § 2 [b] [2]). Although the Legislature requested a home rule message when the enabling law was initially enacted in 1966 (and on several occasions extending the tax since), a home rule message was not required prior to passage of chapter 5, since prior requests for home rule messages are not determinative of whether such messages are constitutionally required. Although the State appropriately authorized the City to implement the commuter tax, it never ceded its taxing authority to the City, nor pursuant to article XVI, § 1 of the State Constitution could it. Accordingly, a home rule message was not required. The statute addresses a subject of substantial State concern, bears a reasonable relationship to that concern, and accomplishes the clearly expressed legislative objective of easing the burden on those State residents working in New York City, but living outside the City limits by repealing the commuter tax.

Taxation - Personal Income Tax - Unconstitutionality of Repeal of New York City Commuter Tax on State Residents Who Work in New York City - Privileges and Immunities - Substantial Equality of Treatment

4. Chapter 5 of the Laws of 1999, which rescinded the personal income tax imposed by the City of New York on State resident commuters who work in the City while retaining the tax on out-of-State commuters who work in the City, violates the Privileges and Immunities Clause of the United States Constitution (art IV, § 2) which requires a "substantial equality of treatment" for the citizens of a taxing State and nonresident taxpayers. In assessing whether "substantial equality" exists between residents and nonresidents, the commuter tax may not be deemed an offset to an aggregation of general revenue taxes. The "substantial equality" of the tax is measured by the impact of the tax in question on nonresident and residents, and does not employ a determination of each group's relative tax burdens, which would relegate constitutional analysis to a difficult, if not impossible, tax calculation that might vary between neighboring States and the lifestyles of individual commuters. The State, which asserts that balancing the tax burden between in-State and out-of-State resident commuters constitutes a "substantial reason" for chapter 5, has failed to satisfy the test of "substantiality": the legislative history of chapter 5 reveals no "tax equalization" rationale for in-State and out-of-State commuters; rather, the State was concerned with providing a tax break to New York State resident commuters who work in the City, as well as attracting business to New York City.

Taxation - Personal Income Tax - Unconstitutionality of Repeal of New York City Commuter Tax on State Residents Who Work in New York City - Privileges and Immunities - Differential Tax Burden

5. Chapter 5 of the Laws of 1999, which rescinded the personal income tax imposed by the City of New York on State resident commuters who work in the City while retaining the tax on out-of-State commuters who work in the City, violates the Privileges and Immunities Clause of the United States Constitution (art IV, § 2) which requires a "substantial equality of treatment" for the citizens of a taxing State and nonresident taxpayers. There has been no showing that any differential tax burden exists such that repeal of the tax for New York residents but not for out-of-State residents yields "substantial equality." Many of the "offsetting" taxes burdening State residents, such as on sales, unearned income, liquor and gasoline, are paid by all New York residents whether or not they work in New York City, and are also paid by out-of-State commuters who purchase goods and services while in New York. Moreover, the State's position is contradictory: it alleges that City residents pay more to the State in revenue than they receive from the State in expenditures. Thus, the City is not a net drain on the financial strength of the rest of the State, and resident commuters are not supporting the City through the numerous taxes. Nor is it of any consequence that nonresident commuters may benefit from City services. The tax on nonresident commuters is not a "fee" for those services; the tax is not predicated on a "cost" for the City services commuters use, nor is it correlated to State subsidies of those services.

Taxation - Personal Income Tax - Unconstitutionality of Repeal of New York City Commuter Tax on State Residents Who Work in New York City - Privileges and Immunities - Exclusionary Discrimination

6. Chapter 5 of the Laws of 1999, which rescinded the personal income tax imposed by the City of New York on State resident commuters who work in the City while retaining the tax on out-of-State commuters who work in the City, violates the Privileges and Immunities Clause of the United States Constitution (art IV, § 2) which requires a "substantial equality of treatment" for the citizens of a taxing State and nonresident taxpayers. The State has made no showing that nonresidents are a "peculiar source of evil" at which chapter 5 is aimed. There is no evidence that nonresident commuters pay less in taxes than their New York State counterparts, and there is no reasonable relationship between the "danger" of a disparate tax burden between New Yorkers and nonresident commuters as a class to justify the discrimination practiced against them. Commuting into the City is not a taxable event substantially equivalent to the events on which the other taxes are based. The language of the statute plainly and frankly discriminates against nonresidents, and the discrimination is so great that both its purpose and effect are exclusionary. Chapter 5 operates as a general rule and intentionally creates inequality between resident and nonresident commuters in violation of the Privileges and Immunities Clause, which plainly and unmistakably protects the right of the citizens of one State to be exempt from any higher taxes or excises than those imposed by another State upon its own citizens.

Taxation - Personal Income Tax - Unconstitutionality of Repeal of New York City Commuter Tax on State Residents Who Work in New York City - Commerce Clause - Discriminatory Treatment of Out-of-State Residents - Facial Invalidity

7. Chapter 5 of the Laws of 1999, which rescinded the personal income tax imposed by the City of New York on State resident commuters who work in the City while retaining the tax on out-of-State commuters who work in the City, violates the Commerce Clause of the United States Constitution (art I, § 8, cl [3]) since it discriminates against interstate commerce by imposing a tax on out-of-State commuters, but not on in-State commuters. A taxing scheme that taxes only nonresidents who work in New York City favors resident commuters. It treats in-State and out-of-State economic interests differently and taxes nonresidents simply because they cross State lines. Thus, the tax scheme benefits residents of New York State and burdens residents of other States who commute to New York City on a daily basis. The law promotes economic protectionism and is incompatible with a single national economic unit. It therefore is facially invalid, and may survive Commerce Clause scrutiny only if the State can show that the tax advances a local purpose that cannot be adequately served by reasonable nondiscriminatory alternatives; however, tax relief for residents does not justify a discriminatory tax on nonresident workers. The movement of persons across State lines is a form of commerce; the commuter tax impacts interstate commerce, and the Commerce Clause protects commuters.

Taxation - Personal Income Tax - Challenge to Tax Statute - Attorneys' Fees

8. In an action successfully challenging the constitutionality of chapter 5 of the Laws of 1999, which rescinded the personal income tax imposed by the City of New York on State resident commuters who work in the City while retaining the tax on out-of-State commuters who work in the City, and further provided that if a court declared the new law void, the entire tax authorization statute would be repealed, plaintiffs' request for an award of attorneys' fees pursuant to Tax Law § 3030, which permits a discretionary award of such fees to the prevailing party in a proceeding in which the Commissioner is a party and which involves the determination, collection or refund of any tax, was properly denied, since the State has demonstrated that the Commissioner's position was substantially justified. The litigation represented the State's obligation to defend the constitutionality of a tax law that was passed by the State Legislature and signed by the Governor. Accordingly, an award of attorneys' fees is not appropriate.

Injunctions - When Injunctive Relief Appropriate - Enforcement of Tax Statute 

9. In an action successfully challenging the constitutionality of chapter 5 of the Laws of 1999, which rescinded the personal income tax imposed by the City of New York on State resident commuters who work in the City while retaining the tax on out-of-State commuters who work in the City, and further provided that if a court declared the new law void, the entire tax authorization statute would be repealed, the courts below did not err in not issuing a permanent injunction enjoining New York from enforcing the tax, since the availability of a tax refund renders injunctive relief inappropriate; the refund process provides an adequate remedy at law.

 

210 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Jeanty


No. 36, No. 37, No. 65 SSM 1


COURT OF APPEALS OF NEW YORK


94 N.Y.2d 507; 727 N.E.2d 1237; 706 N.Y.S.2d 683; 2000 N.Y. LEXIS 513


February 24, 2000, Argued and Submitted  
April 4, 2000, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reargument denied by, Sub nomine at People v. Jones, 95 N.Y.2d 849, 735 N.E.2d 1289, 2000 N.Y. LEXIS 1820, 713 N.Y.S.2d 524 (2000)
Writ of habeas corpus denied Jones v. Greiner, 2003 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 16935 (E.D.N.Y., Aug. 18, 2003)



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the orders of the Appellate Division in these three appeals should be affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal defendants appealed orders of Appellate Division (New York) affirming defendants' separate convictions in the supreme court and holding that the supreme court properly applied N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 270.35[2][a] to discharge and replace sworn jurors with alternates.


OVERVIEW: Criminal defendants challenged the replacement of jurors under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 270.35[2][a]. In the first case, juror did not appear for the continuation of the trial and was replaced with an alternate. In the second case, two jurors, sworn and seated for trial, called in and stated that they could not be in court that day. The jurors were replaced with alternates. In the final case, during the court's final charge to the jury, a sick juror was unable to return for the afternoon session and was replaced with an alternate. All defendants were convicted, and the appellate division affirmed. The court of appeals affirmed all three cases holding that having determined the jurors would not be within two hours after the time set for the trial to resume, the trial courts complied with the statute and were well within their discretion in replacing the jurors with alternates.


OUTCOME: Orders affirmed; appellate did not err in finding supreme court properly applied the statute to discharge and replace sworn jurors with alternates.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Jurors - Discharge of Juror - Replacement by Alternate of Juror Who Will be More than Two Hours Late

1. Once a trial court has conducted a reasonably thorough inquiry into an absent juror's whereabouts and possible time of his or her return, CPL 270.35 (2) permits the court, in the exercise of its discretion, to replace the juror with an alternate, if it determines that the juror will not appear within two hours after the time the trial is scheduled to resume. The two-hour rule is a bright-line rule and does not apply only to unreachable, missing jurors since, by its terms, it applies not only "if such juror fails to appear"--the missing juror scenario--but also "if the court determines that there is no reasonable likelihood such juror will be appearing"--an ill or otherwise indisposed juror. The statute makes no distinction between a missing and a sick juror that would warrant applying the two-hour rule to one but not the other. There is no reason to treat differently missing jurors who remain out of contact from those who explain their absence. Both situations delay a trial, and the legislative history of the statute clearly evinces an intent to avoid delays and provide precise guidelines to trial courts deciding whether to discharge jurors who fail to appear.

Crimes - Jurors - Discharge of Juror - Replacement by Alternate of Juror Who Will be More than Two Hours Late - Two-Hour Time Period Neither Arbitrary nor Unconstitutional 

2. Once a trial court has conducted a reasonably thorough inquiry into an absent juror's whereabouts and possible time of his or her return, if it is determined that the juror will not appear within two hours after the time the trial is scheduled to resume, CPL 270.35 (2) permits the court, in the exercise of its discretion, to replace the juror with an alternate, and the two-hour time period is not an arbitrary time limit that interferes with a defendant's right to trial by jury guaranteed by article I, § 2 of the State Constitution. The dictates of CPL 270.35 (2) provide good cause for discharging and replacing a juror. The "reasonably thorough inquiry" required by the statute insures against arbitrary decision-making regarding the substitution of alternates. Moreover, the two-hour rule is not an arbitrary cut-off point, but strikes a constitutionally acceptable balance between the need to avoid uncertainty and delay, and the defendant's right to an orderly jury trial. Accordingly, where a juror became ill before the Trial Judge's eyes and was sent home, and the court contacted the juror at home after the lunch recess and determined that she was still not feeling well and was too ill to return for the afternoon session, replacing that juror was clearly proper and did not offend constitutional principles.

Crimes - Jurors - Discharge of Juror - Replacement by Alternate of Juror Who Will be More than Two Hours Late - Conclusive Presumption

3. Once a trial court has conducted a reasonably thorough inquiry into an absent juror's whereabouts and possible time of his or her return, CPL 270.35 (2) permits the court, in the exercise of its discretion, to replace the juror with an alternate, if it determines that the juror will not appear within two hours after the time the trial is scheduled to resume. Although the statute states that "the court may presume such juror is unavailable," the incorporation of a rebuttable presumption into the statute is at direct odds with the legislative purpose of providing clear guidelines for trial courts to follow as to how long a Trial Judge must wait for an absent juror. Given the clear indication of purpose, it is illogical to view the two-hour period as creating only a presumption that could be dispelled by showing that an absent juror could be available some time after the two-hour period. Rather, the juror who is or who the court reasonably believes will be more than two hours late can be conclusively presumed to be unavailable and is subject, in the court's discretion, to discharge.

Crimes - Jurors - Discharge of Juror - Replacement by Alternate of Juror Who Will be More than Two Hours Late - Court's Duty to Make Reasonably Thorough Inquiry

4. Once a trial court has conducted a reasonably thorough inquiry into an absent juror's whereabouts and possible time of his or her return, CPL 270.35 (2) permits the court, in the exercise of its discretion, to replace the juror with an alternate, if it determines that the juror will not appear within two hours after the time the trial is scheduled to resume. A rule permitting discharge after two hours does not render that part of the statute requiring the court to conduct a "reasonably thorough inquiry" unnecessary, since holding that a trial juror who is absent for more than two hours could be discharged and replaced does not jettison a trial court's statutory duty to conduct a reasonably thorough inquiry into a juror's availability. To the contrary, this inquiry, which the statute mandates, is a prerequisite to obtaining the presumption that a juror is unavailable. Without a reasonably thorough inquiry, the exercise of the court's discretion on the ultimate issue of whether or not to replace the juror would be uninformed. A trial court has significant discretion to reach a balanced determination on replacing a juror based on the entirety of the circumstances, which can include the ability of an absent juror to return, the stage of the trial, the need to avoid delay, the possibility of prejudice, and the effect on the many other participants involved.

 

211 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Brukhman v. Giuliani


No. 2


COURT OF APPEALS OF NEW YORK


94 N.Y.2d 387; 727 N.E.2d 116; 705 N.Y.S.2d 558; 2000 N.Y. LEXIS 77


January 4, 2000, Argued  
 February 22, 2000, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff public assistance recipients appealed the order of the Supreme Court, Appellate Division (New York) dismissing their action to require defendant city to calculate the number of participation hours required by a work experience program according to prevailing wage rates rather than the Federal minimum wage rate.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs were public assistance recipients required by defendant New York City Department of Social Services, under welfare reform legislation, to participate in a Work Experience Program. They were assigned to various not-for-profit organizations and New York City agencies. Defendant calculated the required hours of participation by dividing the amount of benefits received by the Federal minimum wage. Plaintiffs sought judicial relief to require defendant to re-figure the calculations based on the pertinent prevailing wage rate pursuant to N.Y. Const. art. I, § 17. The court held that: (1) plaintiffs were not "in the employ of" anyone because they did not receive wages; (2) the city agencies and not-for-profit organizations to which plaintiffs were assigned were not "contractors or sub-contractors" within the meaning of the constitutional provision; and (3) plaintiffs were not engaged in "public work" within the envisioned scope of that constitutional term of art.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed. Because they did not receive wages, plaintiffs were not "in the employ of" anyone; the city agencies and not-for-profit organizations to which plaintiffs were assigned were not "contractors or sub-contractors" within the meaning of the constitutional provision; neither were plaintiffs not engaged in "public work".


HEADNOTES

Social Services - Public Assistance - Work Experience Program - Prevailing Wage Rate

The prevailing wage provision of NY Constitution, article I, § 17 does not apply to public assistance beneficiaries who are statutorily required to participate in the Work Experience Program as a condition of continued receipt of monetary grants. Therefore, using the typically lower minimum wage rate to compute the number of hours a beneficiary must work in order to satisfy his requirement does not facially violate the Constitution, since such beneficiaries are not "in the employ of" anyone within the intendment of the Constitution. Further, municipal agencies and not-for-profit organizations to which beneficiaries are assigned as a part of the Program are not "contractors or subcontractors" within the meaning of the constitutional provision, and such beneficiaries are not engaged in "public work" within the envisioned scope of that constitutional term of art. The expectations of "public work" reflected in the record of the debate on the constitutional provision in question are specifically and intentionally related to construction projects, rather than general services.

CMSG Rest. Group, LLC v State of New York


153539/14, 1214


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


145 A.D.3d 136; 40 N.Y.S.3d 412; 2016 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7160; 2016 NY Slip Op 07280



November 3, 2016, Decided 
November 3, 2016, Entered

CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The amusement and cabaret taxes, Tax Law § 1105(f)(1) and (3), respectively, did not violate the constitutional free speech rights of plaintiffs, owners of a men's entertainment club, as they were laws of general application that did not enforce any differential treatment based on the content of ideas or viewpoints expressed in the entertainment the club provided; [2]-As exemptions for dramatic, musical, and artistic performances were rationally related to the legitimate purpose of promoting cultural and artistic performances, they did not deprive any class of businesses of equal protection; [3]-Tax exemptions for live dramatic, choreographic, or musical performances were not unconstitutionally vague, and the opportunity to obtain an official clarification of whether the exemptions applied to a business was sufficient to allay any concerns about unpredictable enforcement.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed.

225 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


New York State Assn. for Affordable Hous. v Council of the City of N.Y.


158093/13, 269


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


141 A.D.3d 208; 33 N.Y.S.3d 202; 2016 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4145; 2016 NY Slip Op 04320



June 2, 2016 
June 2, 2016, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The court held that a local law relating to disclosure on a public website of information regarding affordable housing projects that defendant New York City Department of Housing Preservation and Development (HPD) initiates, funds or otherwise participates in was not unconstitutional and did not unlawfully interfere or frustrate the HPD's authority; [2]-The court noted that although one may reasonably argue that the disclosure requirements imposed by the law are costly, difficult or cumbersome, or that the law will not remedy corruption in the developer selection process or further the flexible and economical implementation of publicly funded housing, the wisdom, necessity or efficacy of the law was not the province of the courts.


OUTCOME: Order modified to declare the local law constitutional, and, as so modified, affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Local Laws--Preemption by State--Field Preemption--Regulation of Affordable Housing Projects

1. Local Law No. 44 (2012) of City of New York, which relates to the disclosure on a public website of information regarding "affordable housing" projects that defendant Department of Housing Preservation and Development initiates, funds, or otherwise participates in, was not preempted by articles of the Private Housing Finance Law and General Municipal Law that delegate powers to defendant to make loans or grants to develop both private and public properties for affordable housing under the doctrine of field preemption. The statements of legislative findings and purposes contained in the Private Housing Finance Law and General Municipal Law contained no statement of general preemption, nor could any intent to preempt the entire field of affordable housing be implied from the policy and purposes underpinning the relevant articles. The purpose of those articles is to improve the quantity, quality and affordability of housing in the City of New York, allow the City to assist the private sector in the development of affordable housing by authorizing it to expend funds to do so, and allow the City to build affordable housing and dispose of property. The legislative materials merely reflected the legislature's efforts to create a framework for the financing of affordable housing projects and to defer operational functions to local municipalities.

Local Laws--Preemption by State--Conflict Preemption--Regulation of Affordable Housing Projects

2. Local Law No. 44 (2012) of City of New York, which relates to the disclosure on a public website of information regarding "affordable housing" projects that defendant Department of Housing Preservation and Development initiates, funds, or otherwise participates in, was not preempted by articles of the Private Housing Finance Law and General Municipal Law that delegate powers to defendant to make loans or grants to develop both private and public properties for affordable housing under the doctrine of conflict preemption. Local Law No. 44 does not directly conflict with the state statutory scheme for affordable housing or wage reporting and does not improperly contravene the state legislature's specific statutory delegations of responsibility to defendant or restrict the manner in which the agency exercises its delegated authority. Nor does the law prohibit what would be permissible for defendant under the General Municipal Law or the Private Housing Finance Law or impose prerequisite additional restrictions on defendant's rights to provide loans or grants under those laws. Compliance with the local law is not a condition for access to housing finance funding and, thus, the law did not interfere in any way with defendant's financing, loan, and land disposition processes. While the City Charter provides that, where the City is providing grants or loans related to affordable housing, the programs must be run by defendant, defendant was subject to the oversight of the City Council and certain powers were delegated to the Mayor and City Council as well.

Constitutional Law--Due Process of Law--"Stigma Plus" Rule

3. Plaintiffs, a trade association for the affordable housing industry and contractors or subcontractors that have worked on affordable housing projects, failed to establish that Local Law No. 44 (2012) of City of New York, which relates to the disclosure on a public website of information regarding "affordable housing" projects that defendant Department of Housing Preservation and Development initiates, funds, or otherwise participates in, was unconstitutional because it violated the Due Process Clause of the New York State Constitution under the "stigma plus" rule. Plaintiffs did not show that they had been stigmatized, since defendant had not published any consumer complaints and had not yet issued regulations under the local law setting forth a protocol for vetting consumer complaints before their publication on its website. Nor did plaintiffs show that they had been deprived of any future employment opportunities, and it was speculation to say that they might ever be thus deprived. Further, the local law does not provide that, in the event consumer complaints about contractors are received, the contractors would not be considered for affordable housing projects.

Constitutional Law--Equal Protection of Laws--Discrimination against Corporations

4. Plaintiffs, a trade association for the affordable housing industry and contractors or subcontractors that have worked on affordable housing projects, failed to establish that Local Law No. 44 (2012) of City of New York, which relates to the disclosure on a public website of information regarding "affordable housing" projects that defendant Department of Housing Preservation and Development initiates, funds, or otherwise participates in, was unconstitutional because it violated the Equal Protection Clause of the New York State Constitution on the basis that it discriminated against corporations in favor of individuals. The rational basis for making a distinction between corporations and individuals was to protect small businesses from the costs of reporting. The local law was not invalid under rational-basis review simply because it may be underinclusive or fail to address all aspects of the problem.
