227 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


New York State United Teachers v State of New York


521358


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


140 A.D.3d 90; 31 N.Y.S.3d 618; 2016 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3432; 2016 NY Slip Op 03572



May 5, 2016 
May 5, 2016, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal dismissed by N.Y. State v State, 28 NY3d 978, 2016 N.Y. LEXIS 3197, 39 NYS3d 852, 62 NE3d 564 (2016)
Leave to appeal denied by N.Y. State United Teachers v State, 28 NY3d 915, 2017 N.Y. LEXIS 125 (N.Y., Feb. 14, 2017)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Plaintiffs' equal protection challenges to Education Law § 2023-a and the tax freeze legislation of Education Law § 2023-b and Tax Law § 606(bbb) failed under a rational basis test, as the differences in the services offered by various school districts resulted from a permissible consequence of local control over schools, namely, the variable willingness of the taxpayers of different districts to pay for and to provide enriched educational services and facilities beyond what the basic per pupil expenditure figures would permit; [2]-The legislation did not violate substantive due process under the Fifth and Fourteenth Amendments and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 6, as no fundamental rights were implicated and the legislation was rationally related to the legitimate government interest of restraining crippling property tax increases.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Parties -- Standing -- Common-Law Taxpayer Standing -- Challenge to Property Tax Cap

1. Plaintiff taxpayers had common-law standing to challenge the constitutionality of New York's "tax cap" legislation (Education Law § 2023-a) which imposes a tax levy cap on school districts unless the levy is approved by a supermajority of 60% of school budget voters. Plaintiffs established that they voted for budgets requiring levies that exceeded the cap which failed to achieve the required supermajority approval, and alleged that the tax cap impaired their constitutional rights, including the right to participate in the governance of their own schools, and degraded their ability to provide school funding as they saw fit. These allegations met the common-law standard for standing.

Parties -- Standing -- Citizen Taxpayer Standing -- Challenge to Property Tax Freeze Credit

2. Plaintiff taxpayers had standing under State Finance Law § 123-b (1) to challenge the constitutionality of New York's property "tax freeze" credit (Education Law § 2023-b; Tax Law § 606 [bbb]), which provided a two-year income tax credit to eligible homeowners who had paid real property taxes to schools that abided by New York's property "tax cap" (Education Law § 2023-a), where they alleged that the legislation authorized a wrongful expenditure, misappropriation, misapplication, or illegal or unconstitutional disbursement of state funds due to its allocation of those funds for a tax credit.

Schools -- State Aid to School Districts -- Constitutional Challenge to Property Tax Cap and Property Tax Freeze Credit -- Education Article Claim

3. Plaintiff taxpayers' complaint challenging the constitutionality of New York's "tax cap" legislation (Education Law § 2023-a), which imposes a tax levy cap on school districts unless the levy is approved by a supermajority of 60% of school budget voters, and property "tax freeze" credit (Education Law § 2023-b; Tax Law § 606 [bbb]), which provided a two-year income tax credit to eligible homeowners who had paid real property taxes to schools that abided by New York's property tax cap, failed to state a cause of action under the Education Article of the New York Constitution (NY Const, art XI, § 1). The Education Article enshrines in the Constitution a state-local partnership in which people with a community of interest and a tradition of acting together to govern themselves make the basic decisions on funding and operating their own schools. The provision is intended to ensure that education is a responsibility of the State, and fundamentally protects against failures by the State to provide adequate instruction and facilities commensurate with that responsibility. Here, plaintiffs' claims were not viable because they failed to allege that the challenged legislation so impaired local financing ability that it implicated the State's duty to provide funding sufficient to bring the educational inputs locally available up to a minimum standard.

Schools -- State Aid to School Districts -- Constitutional Challenge to Property Tax Cap and Property Tax Freeze Credit -- Equal Protection

4. Plaintiff taxpayers' claims that New York's "tax cap" legislation (Education Law § 2023-a), which imposes a tax levy cap on school districts unless the levy is approved by a supermajority of 60% of school budget voters, and property "tax freeze" credit (Education Law § 2023-b; Tax Law § 606 [bbb]), which provided a two-year income tax credit to eligible homeowners who had paid real property taxes to schools that abided by New York's property tax cap, failed to state a cause of action for deprivation of equal protection under the law. Education is not a fundamental right under either the Federal or New York Constitutions and, absent any allegation that the challenged legislation discriminates against a suspect class, the rational basis test is the appropriate standard. The rational basis test is not demanding, and a statute will pass muster under it unless a challenger meets the tremendous burden of demonstrating that no facts can reasonably be conceived to show the existence of a rational basis in support of some legitimate state interest in drawing the distinction. Here, the tax cap and the tax freeze were designed with the legitimate goal in mind of restraining onerous property tax increases that were believed to be depressing economic activity in the state. While these enactments incentivized districts and their residents to avoid property tax increases over the tax cap, neither prevented such increases if sufficient community support existed for them. The differences in the services offered by various school districts accordingly resulted from a permissible consequence of local control over schools, namely, the variable willingness of the taxpayers of different districts to pay for and to provide enriched educational services and facilities beyond what the basic per pupil expenditure figures would permit. Inasmuch as there was nothing irrational in this, plaintiffs' equal protection claims failed.

Schools -- State Aid to School Districts -- Constitutional Challenge to Property Tax Cap and Property Tax Freeze Credit -- Due Process

5. Plaintiff taxpayers' claims that New York's "tax cap" legislation (Education Law § 2023-a), which imposes a tax levy cap on school districts unless the levy is approved by a supermajority of 60% of school budget voters, and property "tax freeze" credit (Education Law § 2023-b; Tax Law § 606 [bbb]), which provided a two-year income tax credit to eligible homeowners who had paid real property taxes to schools that abided by New York's property tax cap, failed to state a cause of action for a deprivation of due process. A law that impinges upon a fundamental right is subject to strict scrutiny, whereas one that does not burden a fundamental right is valid if it bears a rational relationship to a legitimate governmental interest. The tax cap did not impair the fundamental right to vote because a supermajority requirement passes constitutional muster unless it discriminates against or authorizes discrimination against any identifiable class, and here, nothing of the sort was alleged. While the denial of a tax freeze credit to an individual who engages in certain forms of speech may penalize them for such speech, here, taxpayers remained free to vote as they wished in school budget elections, and their individual votes (or nonvotes) had no direct role in deciding their access to the tax freeze credit. Taxpayers in school districts where budgets with tax levies exceeding the property tax cap were proposed and adopted were not denied a tax credit because they engaged in constitutionally protected activities, but were simply required to pay for school property taxes entirely out of their own pockets rather than receiving a subsidy to offset those costs. Given that no fundamental rights were implicated, and that both the tax cap and the tax freeze were rationally related to the legitimate government interest of restraining crippling property tax increases, plaintiffs' substantive due process claims were properly dismissed.

Myers v Schneiderman


151162/15, 310


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


140 A.D.3d 51; 31 N.Y.S.3d 45; 2016 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3319; 2016 NY Slip Op 03457



May 3, 2016 
May 3, 2016, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion granted by Myers v. Schneiderman, 28 N.Y.3d 1131, 2017 N.Y. LEXIS 48, 46 N.Y.S.3d 835, 69 N.E.3d 623 (Jan. 12, 2017)

Motion granted by, in part Sara Myers v. Eric Schneiderman, etc., 29 N.Y.3d 988, 2017 N.Y. LEXIS 1277, 53 N.Y.S.3d 254, 75 N.E.3d 672 (May 4, 2017)
Motion granted by Sara Myers v. Eric Schneiderman, etc., 29 N.Y.3d 987, 2017 N.Y. LEXIS 1265, 53 N.Y.S.3d 254, 75 N.E.3d 671 (May 4, 2017)
Motion granted by, in part Sara Myers v. Eric Schneiderman, etc., 29 N.Y.3d 988, 2017 N.Y. LEXIS 1267, 53 N.Y.S.3d 255, 75 N.E.3d 672 (May 4, 2017)
Motion granted by, in part Sara Myers v. Eric Schneiderman, etc., 29 N.Y.3d 987, 2017 N.Y. LEXIS 1272, 53 N.Y.S.3d 253, 75 N.E.3d 671 (May 4, 2017)
Motion granted by, in part Sara Myers v. Eric Schneiderman, etc., 29 N.Y.3d 988, 2017 N.Y. LEXIS 1276, 53 N.Y.S.3d 254, 75 N.E.3d 671 (May 4, 2017)
Motion granted by, in part Sara Myers v. Eric Schneiderman, etc., 29 N.Y.3d 989, 2017 N.Y. LEXIS 1279, 53 N.Y.S.3d 255, 75 N.E.3d 672 (May 4, 2017)
Affirmed by Myers v. Schneiderman, 2017 N.Y. LEXIS 2557 (N.Y., Sept. 7, 2017)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The trial court properly granted the New York State Attorney General's pre-answer motion to dismiss the plaintiffs' complaint because Penal Law §§ 120.30 and 125.15 provided a valid statutory basis to prosecute licensed physicians who provided aid-in-dying to their patients, the word "suicide" had a straightforward meaning, and whatever label one put on the act that the plaintiffs were asking to be permitted, it unquestionably fit that literal description, since there was a direct causative link between the medication proposed to be administered by physicians and their patients' demise; [2]-The plaintiffs failed to show that a ban on aid-in-dying violated their state constitutional equal protection and due process rights because, even giving plaintiffs the benefit of every reasonable inference, aid-in-dying was not an issue where a legitimate consensus had formed.


OUTCOME: Order affirmed as modified.


HEADNOTES

Statutes--Construction--Penal Law §§ 120.30, 125.15 (3)--Aid-in-Dying

1. Penal Law §§ 120.30, which provides that "[a] person is guilty of promoting a suicide attempt when he [or she] intentionally causes or aids another person to attempt suicide," and 125.15 (3), which provides that "[a] person is guilty of manslaughter in the second degree when [he or she] intentionally causes or aids another person to commit suicide," prohibit physician-assisted suicide (aid-in-dying) and provide a valid statutory basis to prosecute licensed physicians who provide aid-in-dying. The word "suicide," which has a straightforward meaning, is traditionally defined as the act or instance of taking one's own life voluntarily and intentionally, especially by a person of years of discretion and of sound mind. Aid-in-dying unquestionably fits this literal description since there is a direct causative link between the medication proposed to be administered by plaintiff physicians and their patients' demise. While plaintiffs argued that aid-in-dying reflected a starkly different choice than suicide--that of death by a quick and painless means over death that is equally certain to happen in reasonably close temporal proximity but in a manner that is sure to be unbearably painful--no rule of statutory construction allowed the plain language to be ignored simply because there was a possibility that the legislature, writing on a blank slate, would have chosen to carve out of that definition a particular application of the term. Further, legislative history revealed that section 125.15 (3) was intended to apply even where the defendant was motivated by sympathetic concerns, such as the desire to relieve a terminally ill person from the agony of a painful disease.

Constitutional Law--State Constitutional Law--Equal Protection--Aid-in-Dying

2. New York's prohibition on physician-assisted suicide (aid-in-dying) (see Penal Law §§ 120.30, 125.15 [3]) does not violate the Equal Protection Clause of the New York Constitution (NY Const, art I, § 11). New York's Equal Protection Clause is no broader in coverage than the federal provision, and New York's distinction between the right to refuse medical treatment and the prohibition on aid-in-dying is permissible under the United States Constitution. New York's prohibition on aid-in-dying does not violate equal protection as the refusal of treatment and the affirmative administration of life-ending medication are fundamentally different things. New York does not treat anyone differently from anyone else or draw any distinctions between persons. Everyone, regardless of physical condition, is entitled, if competent, to refuse unwanted lifesaving medical treatment; no one is permitted to assist a suicide. Plaintiffs failed to establish that the Court should reconsider whether aid-in-dying was functionally indistinct from the refusal to be kept alive as they failed to sufficiently demonstrate a societal evolution on the question of aid-in-dying such that, if the ban was upheld, the Court would be paying blind adherence to outmoded thinking.

Constitutional Law--State Constitutional Law--Due Process--Aid-in-Dying

3. New York's prohibition on physician-assisted suicide (aid-in-dying) (see Penal Law §§ 120.30, 125.15 [3]) does not violate the Due Process Clause of the New York Constitution (NY Const, art I, § 6). A ban on aid-in-dying does not implicate a fundamental liberty interest under the United States Constitution, and is rationally related to a state's interests in preserving human life, protecting the integrity and ethics of the medical profession, and ensuring the welfare of vulnerable groups. New York has, at times, found that the State Due Process Clause may be more protective of rights than its federal counterpart, and has treated a person's freedom of choice with respect to his or her own body and medical treatment as a vitally important right to be subordinated to the State's prerogatives under only compelling circumstances. However, such circumstances all involved a patient's right to refuse medical treatment, and were rooted in the same concepts that gave rise to a cause of action for medical malpractice based on the lack of informed consent--that every person of adult years and sound mind has a right to determine what should be done with his or her own body, and a surgeon who performs an operation without the patient's consent commits an assault for which the surgeon is liable in damages. Here, plaintiffs failed to establish that the same principles applied to the affirmative act of taking one's own life. Plaintiffs offered nothing other than conclusory arguments for why, unlike the United States Constitution, the New York State Constitution should be construed to extend the right to refuse treatment and let nature take its course to a fundamental right to receive treatment that does the opposite. Plaintiffs also failed to establish that the Court should reconsider whether the ban on aid-in-dying was rationally related to a legitimate government interest as they failed to sufficiently demonstrate a societal evolution on the question of aid-in-dying such that, if the ban was upheld, the Court would be paying blind adherence to outmoded thinking.

 

229 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Hawkins v New York State Dept. of Corr. & Community Supervision


521536


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


140 A.D.3d 34; 30 N.Y.S.3d 397; 2016 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3147; 2016 NY Slip Op 03236



April 28, 2016 
April 28, 2016, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Counsel Amended April 29, 2016.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-In a CPLR art. 78 proceeding, the trial court properly annulled a determination of the Board of Parole denying an inmate's request for parole release because the inmate was 16 years old when he was convicted of murder in the second degree and sentenced, in 1979, to a prison term of 22 years to life, but the Board failed to consider the significance of the inmate's youth and its attendant circumstances at the time of the commission of the crime before making a parole determination; the inmate was entitled to a de novo parole release hearing; [2]-The trial court erred in precluding one of the Board's commissioners from participating in any future parole proceeding because the inmate did not request such relief, and the record provided no basis for a conclusion that the commissioner at issue was either unqualified or biased.


OUTCOME: Judgment modified, and, as so modified, judgment affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Parole -- Denial -- Juvenile Homicide Offender -- Board's Failure to Provide Meaningful Opportunity for Release

Petitioner, a juvenile homicide offender who was sentenced to a term of 22 years to life and had been denied parole release nine times since becoming eligible, was entitled to a de novo parole release hearing where the parole board failed to consider the significance of petitioner's youth and its attendant circumstances at the time of the commission of the crime before making its parole determination and thus denied him a meaningful opportunity for release. As a person serving a sentence for a crime committed as a juvenile, petitioner had a substantive constitutional right not to be punished with a life sentence if the crime reflected transient immaturity. The procedural requirement necessary to implement this substantive guarantee at the sentencing stage is a hearing where youth and its attendant characteristics are considered in order to separate out those who can be punished by a life in prison from those who cannot. Moreover, a constitutional life sentence for a juvenile homicide offender--for all but the rare case of an irreparably corrupt juvenile--guarantees, at some point, a meaningful opportunity to obtain release. A parole board is no more entitled to subject an offender to the penalty of life in prison in contravention of this rule than is a legislature or a sentencing court, and an analogous procedural requirement is necessary at the parole release hearing stage. Thus, for those persons--like petitioner--convicted of crimes committed as juveniles who, but for a favorable parole determination will be punished by life in prison, the parole board must provide a meaningful opportunity for release by considering youth and its attendant characteristics in relationship to the commission of the crime at issue before making its parole determination. Here, neither the hearing transcript nor the parole board's written determination reflected that the parole board met its constitutional obligation to consider these factors.

 

230 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


American Economy Ins. Co. v State of New York


456, 16096


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


139 A.D.3d 138; 31 N.Y.S.3d 456; 2016 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2801; 2016 NY Slip Op 02924



April 14, 2016 
April 14, 2016, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion granted by American Economy Ins. Co. v. State of New York, 28 N.Y.3d 1049, 2016 N.Y. LEXIS 3525, 43 N.Y.S.3d 246, 65 N.E.3d 1281 (Nov. 17, 2016)
Motion granted by, in part American Economy Ins. Co. v. State, 28 N.Y.3d 1162, 2017 N.Y. LEXIS 182, 49 N.Y.S.3d 90, 71 N.E.3d 582 (Feb. 16, 2017)
Reversed by, Judgment entered by American Economy Ins. Co. v. State of New York, 2017 N.Y. LEXIS 3195 (N.Y., Oct. 24, 2017)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The court held that plaintiffs established that the amendment to Workers' Compensation Law § 25-a, as applied retroactively to policies issued before October 1, 2013, was unconstitutional because the New York legislature amended the statute without an understanding of the impact it would have on policies issued before October 1, 2013; [2]-The court further held that the amendment violated the Contract Clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 10, cl. 1, because it retroactively impaired an existing contractual obligation to provide insurance coverage where the insurer does not have the right to terminate the policy or change the premium rate; [3]-The court further held that the retroactive application also constituted a regulatory taking.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed; complaint reinstated; judgment directed in plaintiffs' favor.


HEADNOTES

Statutes -- Retroactive Application of Statute -- Workers' Compensation Law § 25-a (1-a)

1. A 2013 amendment to Workers' Compensation Law § 25-a (1-a), which closed the reopened case fund to newly reopened claims as of January 1, 2014 and provided that any reopened claims that would have been transferred to the fund under the former law would become the obligation of the carrier, impermissibly imposed on plaintiff private insurance companies that underwrite workers' compensation insurance policies significant additional liability retroactively with respect to contracts issued before October 1, 2013. The reopened case fund provided for the payment of workers' compensation benefits to employees whose cases were closed and later reopened and was financed by an annual assessment from workers' compensation insurers. Its existence meant that reopened claims were not included when insurers' premium rates for their occurrence-based policies were calculated and approved. Premiums charged for policies approved prior to October 1, 2013 took into account the transfer to the fund of reopened claims under former Workers' Compensation Law § 25-a, and did not account for potential future liability relating to such claims, which were expected to qualify for a transfer to the fund. While policies approved on or after October 1, 2013 included premium increases to cover the additional liability resulting from the closure of the fund, a benefit payable on a reopened claim made after January 1, 2014 but arising out of an accident that occurred before October 1, 2013 would impose on the insurer a liability that was not contemplated when the premium for the pre-October 1, 2013 policy was calculated. The fund's closure failed to provide for this unfunded liability. Even though the statute mandated only the payment of future benefits, it nonetheless impermissibly attached new legal consequences to a relationship completed before its enactment.

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Workers' Compensation Law § 25-a

2. A 2013 amendment to Workers' Compensation Law § 25-a (1-a) which closed the reopened case fund to newly reopened claims as of January 1, 2014 and provided that any reopened claims that would have been transferred to the fund under the former law would become the obligation of the carrier violated the Contract Clause of the US Constitution and constituted a regulatory taking in violation of the Takings Clause. The amendment impermissibly imposed on plaintiff private insurance companies that underwrite workers' compensation insurance policies significant additional liability retroactively with respect to contracts issued before October 1, 2013. The amendment violated the Contract Clause because it retroactively impaired an existing contractual obligation to provide insurance coverage where the insurer did not have the right to terminate the policy or change the premium rate. Defendants failed to show that the impairment was reasonable and necessary to serve a significant and legitimate public purpose. The legislation's stated purpose of preventing a windfall to insurance carriers was based upon the erroneous premise that premiums already covered this new liability. Retroactive application also constituted a regulatory taking as it imposed severe retroactive liability on a limited class of parties that could not have anticipated the liability, and the extent of that liability was substantially disproportionate to the parties' experience.

Schulz v State of New York Exec.

235 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Gifford v Mccarthy


520410


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


137 A.D.3d 30; 23 N.Y.S.3d 422; 2016 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 238; 2016 NY Slip Op 00230



January 14, 2016 
January 14, 2016, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The State Division of Human Rights properly determined that the owners of a farm were subject to the Human Rights Law because the farm's wedding facilities fell within the broad definition of "place of public accommodation," Executive Law § 292(9); [2]-The owners opened the farm to the public as a venue for wedding ceremonies and receptions and offered several wedding-related event services; [3]-The owners discriminated against a couple on the basis of sexual orientation in violation of Executive Law § 296(2)(a) when they refused to host the couple's wedding on the farm; [4]-The owners displayed no unwillingness to allow the couple to marry at the farm until one of them referred to her fiancée as a "she"; [5]-Their purported willingness to offer some services to the couple did does not cure their refusal to provide a service that was offered to the general public.


OUTCOME: Determination confirmed.


HEADNOTES

Civil Rights--Discrimination Based on Sexual Orientation--Refusal to Host Same-Sex Wedding

1. In a proceeding pursuant to Executive Law § 298, the determination of respondent State Division of Human Rights (SDHR) finding that petitioners, the owners and operators of facilities rented to the public as a venue for wedding ceremonies and receptions, were subject to the Human Rights Law and engaged in unlawful discrimination on the basis of sexual orientation when they refused to host a same-sex wedding for respondents, a same-sex couple, was confirmed. SDHR properly found that petitioners' wedding facilities fell within the broad definition of "place of public accommodation" under Executive Law § 292 (9). That the wedding ceremonies occurred on private property and pursuant to written contracts did not remove petitioners' facilities from the reach of the Human Rights Law, the critical factor being that the facilities were made available to the public at large. Further, the finding that petitioners engaged in unlawful discrimination was supported by substantial evidence. Petitioners displayed no unwillingness to allow respondents to marry at their facility until it was revealed that respondents were a same-sex couple. Petitioners could not distinguish their conduct from that prohibited under Executive Law § 296 (2) (a) by claiming that their decision not to host same-sex marriages was based solely upon their religious beliefs, as the act of entering into a same-sex marriage is conduct that is inextricably tied to sexual orientation. There is no basis for distinguishing between discrimination based on sexual orientation and discrimination based on someone's conduct of publicly committing to a person of the same sex. Further, petitioners' purported willingness to offer some services to respondents did not cure their refusal to provide a wedding ceremony. The Human Rights Law does not permit businesses to offer a limited menu of goods or services to customers on the basis of a status that fits within one of the protected categories.

Constitutional Law--Freedom of Religion--Mandated Hosting of Same-Sex Wedding--Free Exercise

2. In a proceeding pursuant to Executive Law § 298, the remedy imposed by respondent State Division of Human Rights requiring petitioners, the owners and operators of wedding facilities who were found to have illegally discriminated against respondents, a same-sex couple, by refusing to host their wedding, to cease and desist from engaging in discriminatory practices and establish anti-discrimination training and procedures did not violate petitioners' rights under the Free Exercise Clause of the Federal or State Constitutions. The Human Rights Law (Executive Law art 15) does not target religious beliefs, nor is its objective to infringe upon or restrict practices because of their religious motivation. Rather, the Human Rights Law generally forbids all discrimination against a protected class in places of public accommodation regardless of the motivation. The fact that some religious organizations and educational facilities are exempt from the statute's public accommodation provision did not demonstrate that it was not neutral or generally applicable so as to violate petitioners' First Amendment free exercise rights. Moreover, balancing the competing interests involved, petitioners failed to show that respondent's determination constituted an unreasonable interference with their religious freedom under New York's Free Exercise Clause. While the burden placed on petitioners' right to freely exercise their religion was not inconsequential, it did not require them to participate in the marriage of a same-sex couple and left them free to adhere to and profess their religious beliefs that same-sex couples should not marry. To be weighed against petitioners' interests in adhering to the tenets of their faith was New York's long-recognized, substantial interest in eradicating discrimination.

Constitutional Law--Freedom of Speech--Mandated Hosting of Same-Sex Wedding at Venue Open to Public

3. In a proceeding pursuant to Executive Law § 298, respondent State Division of Human Rights' directive that petitioners, the owners and operators of facilities open to the public as a venue for weddings, cease and desist from their practice of prohibiting same-sex marriage ceremonies did not violate the free speech rights guaranteed to them by the Federal and State Constitutions. The conduct allegedly compelled was not sufficiently expressive as to trigger First Amendment protections in that there was no real likelihood that petitioners would be perceived as endorsing the values or lifestyle of the individuals renting their facilities as opposed to merely complying with anti-discrimination laws. Respondent's determination did not compel petitioners to endorse, espouse or promote same-sex marriages, and instead simply required them to abide by the law and offer the same goods and services to same-sex couples that they offered to other couples. Further, there was nothing in the record to indicate that petitioners' wedding business was organized for specific expressive purposes, rather than for the purpose of making a profit through service contracts with customers, so as to invoke the First Amendment's protection for expressive association. Even if petitioners were deemed to be an expressive enterprise, a customer's association with a business for the limited purpose of obtaining goods and services, as opposed to becoming part of the business itself, does not trigger the First Amendment's protection for expressive association.

Civil Rights--Discrimination Based on Sexual Orientation--Compensatory Damages Award and Civil Penalty

4. In a proceeding pursuant to Executive Law § 298, there was no basis upon which to disturb either the compensatory damages award or the civil fine assessed by respondent State Division of Human Rights against petitioners, the owners and operators of wedding facilities who were found to have illegally discriminated against respondents, a same-sex couple, by refusing to host their wedding. Respondents were the victims of discrimination based on their sexual orientation, and the record reflected the hurt, humiliation and mental anguish that they suffered as a result of petitioners' conduct. The compensatory damages awards to each of them were well in accord with those sustained in similar cases. Mindful that respondent State Division of Human Rights has been empowered to take appropriate action to fulfill the extremely strong statutory policy of eliminating discrimination, the $10,000 civil fine imposed upon petitioners did not constitute an abuse of discretion as a matter of law (Executive Law § 297 [4] [c] [vi]).

 

236 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Williams v Department of Corr. & Community Supervision


400638/13, 15259


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


136 A.D.3d 147; 24 N.Y.S.3d 18; 2016 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 129; 2016 NY Slip Op 00135



January 12, 2016 
January 12, 2016, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion denied by Williams v. Dep't of Corr. & Cmty. Supervision, 28 N.Y.3d 982, 2016 N.Y. LEXIS 3194, 39 N.Y.S.3d 856, 62 N.E.3d 568 (Oct. 20, 2016)
Appeal dismissed by, in part, Motion granted by, in part, Motion denied by, in part Matter of Foster Williams v. Department of Corr.& Community Supervision, 2017 N.Y. LEXIS 1288 (N.Y., May 4, 2017)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The court affirmed the judgment of trial court declaring New York's Sexual Assault Reform Act, Executive Law § 259-c(14), was not unconstitutional on its face or as applied to a parolee because SARA imposed no restrictions, punishment, or penalty over and above that to which parolees were already subject to, was not enacted with a punitive intent, and did not violate the Ex Post Facto Clause, US Const. art. I, § 10, cl. 1.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes--Sex Offenders--Sexual Assault Reform Act--Retrospective Application of Residence and Travel Restrictions--Ex Post Facto Clause

1. The mandatory 1,000-foot buffer zone contained in New York's Sexual Assault Reform Act (Executive Law § 259-c [14]) (SARA), which prohibited petitioner, a sex offender parolee who had been convicted before SARA was passed and amended, from residing or traveling near schools or other institutions where minor children congregate, did not violate the Ex Post Facto Clause of the United States Constitution (art I, § 10). Both SARA, as originally enacted, and the 2005 amendments, were intended to be civil measures designed to protect children from victimization by sexual predators by limiting access that certain previously adjudicated sex offenders could have to defined public areas where children regularly congregate and travel. The child-focused language in the statute itself strongly supports the legislative intent to protect children as opposed to further punishing sex offenders. Although the residency and travel restrictions SARA imposes constitute affirmative restraints, bear some resemblance to historical criminal punishment and serve the goal of deterrence, SARA only applies to parolees, who otherwise have restricted liberty. Because the actual restraints SARA places on parolees are no greater than the restrictions or conditions to which they are otherwise subject, they do not transform SARA's civil regulatory scheme into criminal punishment. Moreover, there is a sufficient rational connection between SARA's nonpunitive intent of deterring recidivism and its effect of limiting access to potential victims.

Crimes--Sex Offenders--Sexual Assault Reform Act--Residence and Travel Restrictions--Substantive Due Process

2. New York's Sexual Assault Reform Act (Executive Law § 259-c [14]) (SARA), which prohibited petitioner sex offender parolee from residing or traveling near schools or other institutions where minor children congregate, did not deprive petitioner of his liberty or the right to intrastate travel in violation of the Due Process Clauses of the United States and New York Constitutions (US Const Amends V, XIV; NY Const, art I, § 6). Even assuming there is a fundamental right to intrastate travel, SARA does not violate any such right. Parolees subject to SARA have only conditional liberty. They have no liberty interest, let alone a fundamental right, to be free from special conditions of parole. Even if SARA were to implicate a deprivation of petitioner's liberty right, that right would not be a fundamental one. Therefore, the inquiry is whether SARA bears a rational relationship to the legitimate government interest it seeks to advance. On a rational basis review, a statute will be upheld unless the disparate treatment is so unrelated to the achievement of any combination of legitimate purposes that it is irrational. Under such a highly deferential standard, SARA did not violate any of petitioner's substantive due process rights.

244 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Schulz v. State of N.Y. Exec.


520540


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


134 A.D.3d 52; 19 N.Y.S.3d 92; 2015 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7825; 2015 NY Slip Op 07728



October 22, 2015, Decided 
October 22, 2015, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal dismissed by Schulz v State of N.Y. Exec., 26 NY3d 1139, 2016 N.Y. LEXIS 249, 27 NYS3d 502 (2016)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Heightened review of the sufficiency of the Governor's message of necessity for the New York Secure Ammunition and Firearms Enforcement Act (SAFE Act), L 2013, ch. 1, was not required under N.Y. Const. art. III, § 14, even though the SAFE Act impacted upon fundamental rights; [2]-Assuming that the SAFE Act substantially burdened the right to keep and bear arms under the Second Amendment, US Const. amend. II, and that the weapons and feeding devices addressed were not the type of dangerous and exotic weaponry that merited no Second Amendment protection, the provisions bore a substantial relation to the achievement of an important governmental objective in public safety by reducing access to weapons designed to quickly fire significant amounts of ammunition and the ammunition feeding devices required to hold that ammunition.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Messages of Necessity -- Factual Justification 

1. In an action challenging the constitutionality of the New York Secure Ammunition and Firearms Enforcement Act (SAFE Act), heightened review of the facts justifying the message of necessity was not required, even though the SAFE Act impacted upon fundamental rights. NY Constitution, article III, § 14 requires that a bill be printed and placed upon the desks of legislators "at least three calendar legislative days prior to its final passage," unless the governor "certifies the facts which in his or her opinion necessitate an immediate vote thereon," but the sufficiency of those facts is not subject to judicial review. Inasmuch as the message of necessity provided a factual justification, no further judicial review was warranted.

Constitutional Law -- Right to Bear Arms -- Prohibition of Assault Weapons and Large-Capacity Magazines 

2. In an action challenging the constitutionality of the New York Secure Ammunition and Firearms Enforcement Act (SAFE Act), the core prohibitions of assault weapons and large-capacity magazines contained in the SAFE Act did not violate the Second Amendment of the US Constitution. Accepting for purposes of discussion that the SAFE Act substantially burdened the right to keep and bear arms so as to subject it to Second Amendment scrutiny, and assuming that the weapons and feeding devices addressed were not the type of dangerous and exotic weaponry that merited no Second Amendment protection, the challenged provisions were subject to intermediate scrutiny, surviving if they bear a substantial relationship to the achievement of an important governmental objective. With regard to the SAFE Act's objective, New York has compelling governmental interests in public safety and crime prevention. Plaintiff challengers provided no proof to call the well-established premise behind the challenged provisions into question, namely, that the governmental interest in public safety is substantially furthered by reducing access to weapons designed to quickly fire significant amounts of ammunition and the ammunition feeding devices required to hold that ammunition.

People v Bradberry


643 KA 13-02191


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


131 A.D.3d 800; 16 N.Y.S.3d 97; 2015 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6491; 2015 NY Slip Op 06609



August 19, 2015, Decided 
August 19, 2015, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Leave to appeal denied by People v Bradberry, 26 NY3d 1086, 2015 N.Y. LEXIS 4114, 23 NYS3d 643, 44 NE3d 941 (N.Y., Dec. 4, 2015)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-In a case where defendant was convicted after a nonjury trial of two counts of course of sexual conduct against a child in the first degree under Penal Law § 130.75, as a lesser included offense of predatory sexual assault against a child under Penal Law § 130.96, and one count of incest in the second degree under Penal Law § 255.26, because the crime of predatory sexual assault against a child was a continuing offense to which the usual requirements of specificity with respect to time did not apply, the time periods of 7.5 months and one year alleged in the indictment were sufficient to give defendant adequate notice of the charges to enable him to prepare a defense, to ensure that the crimes for which he was tried were in fact the crimes with which he was charged, and to protect his right not to be twice placed in jeopardy for the same conduct.

Matter of Acevedo v New York State Dept. of Motor Vehs.


520060


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


132 A.D.3d 112; 14 N.Y.S.3d 790; 2015 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6332; 2015 NY Slip Op 06467



August 6, 2015, Decided 
August 6, 2015, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed by Matter of Acevedo v. New York State Dept. of Motor Vehs., 2017 N.Y. LEXIS 1299 (N.Y., May 9, 2017)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Was a motorist's application for an order, granting him a new driver's license and declaring emergency regulations under 15 NYCRR 136.5 unconstitutional, properly dismissed. HOLDINGS: [1]-The motorist's challenges to regulatory provisions imposing a lifetime license revocation and requiring the issuance of a restricted license and the installation of an ignition interlock device were nonjusticiable; [2]-The New York Legislature did not preempt the field of driving while under the influence law by enacting Vehicle and Traffic Law art. 31 because the Legislature vested the New York State Commissioner of Motor Vehicles with broad authority to promulgate regulations to regulate and control the exercise of the New York State Department of Motor Vehicle's powers (Vehicle and Traffic Law § 215(a)); [3]-15 NYCRR 136.5(b)(3) did not conflict with Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1193(2)(b).


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Courts -- Justiciable Questions -- Regulations Affecting Relicensing of Recidivist Drivers by Department of Motor Vehicles

1. In a combined CPLR article 78 proceeding and declaratory judgment action, Supreme Court properly dismissed, as nonjusticiable, petitioner's challenges to regulations adopted by respondent Department of Motor Vehicles that impose a lifetime license revocation upon certain recidivist drivers and require the issuance of a restricted license and the installation of an ignition interlock device. A controversy is justiciable when the plaintiff in an action for a declaratory judgment has an interest sufficient to constitute standing to maintain the action. As to the challenge to the lifetime license revocation, petitioner did not have five or more alcohol- or drug-related driving convictions or incidents during his lifetime, or three or four alcohol- or drug-related driving convictions or incidents and a "serious driving offense" (15 NYCRR 136.5 [a] [2]) within a 25-year look-back period; thus, he was not subject to a lifetime license revocation under the regulatory scheme (15 NYCRR 136.5 [b] [1], [2]). As for his challenge to the imposition of a restricted license and the installation of an ignition interlock device (15 NYCRR 136.5 [b] [3] [ii]), such harm could not eventuate until petitioner's revocation period had expired and, even then, only if a relicensing application was subsequently approved by the Commissioner of Motor Vehicles. Alternatively, petitioner could apply for and be granted an exemption from the restricted license and ignition interlock device requirements (15 NYCRR 136.5 [d]). As the harm sought to be enjoined was contingent upon events which might not come to pass, the claim too was nonjusticiable as speculative and abstract.

Administrative Law -- Delegation of Legislative Power -- Relicensing of Recidivist Drivers by Department of Motor Vehicles

2. The Legislature granted the Department of Motor Vehicles regulatory authority over the relicensing of persons with multiple alcohol- and/or drug-related driving offenses, as evidenced by the scope and language of relevant provisions of the Vehicle and Traffic Law. Within the broad regulatory authority vested in the Commissioner of Motor Vehicles, he or she has the sole authority to approve or deny relicensing applications, including those submitted by persons whose licenses were revoked for alcohol- or drug-related driving offenses (Vehicle and Traffic Law §§ 215 [a]; 510 [5], [6] [a]; 1193 [2] [c] [1]). Furthermore, while the Vehicle and Traffic Law establishes minimum periods of revocation for alcohol- or drug-related driving offenses, it also provides that revoked licenses may only be restored in the discretion of the Commissioner (Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1193 [2] [c] [1]) and that the Commissioner may, on a case-by-case basis, refuse to restore a license which otherwise would be restored under the statute, in the interest of the public safety and welfare (Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1193 [2] [b] [12]).

Constitutional Law -- Separation of Powers -- Promulgation of Regulations Affecting Relicensing of Recidivist Drivers by Department of Motor Vehicles

3. The Department of Motor Vehicles (DMV) did not run afoul of the constitutional separation of powers doctrine by promulgating regulations that impose a lifetime license revocation upon certain recidivist drivers and require the issuance of a restricted license and the installation of an ignition interlock device (15 NYCRR 136.5 [b] [1], [2], [3] [ii]). DMV did not act on its own ideas of public policy, but rather implemented the Legislature's policies of promoting highway safety and reducing instances of impaired and intoxicated driving. Inasmuch as the Commissioner of Motor Vehicles has the discretion to continue license revocations beyond the statutory minimum period (Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1193 [2] [c] [1]) and recidivist drivers pose a heightened risk to the safety of public roadways, DMV promulgated 15 NYCRR 136.5 (b) (3) in furtherance of legislatively-defined policy and thus engaged in permissible, interstitial rulemaking. Furthermore, since the early demise of proposed legislation to impose stricter penalties upon recidivist drivers could be an expression of the Legislature's intent to keep intact the Commissioner's broad discretionary authority over relicensing, it could not be concluded based on that factor alone that DMV exceeded its authority. Moreover, inasmuch as DMV relied on its own empirical data regarding the number of statewide personal injuries and deaths caused by recidivist drivers, a fair amount of expertise was required to promulgate the regulations.

Administrative Law -- Validity of Regulation -- No Conflict with Statute -- Relicensing of Recidivist Drivers by Department of Motor Vehicles

4. 15 NYCRR 136.5 (b) (3), which allows the Commissioner of Motor Vehicles to impose, in addition to the statutory minimum, a five-year license revocation period upon anyone who has committed three or four alcohol- or drug-related driving offenses within a 25-year look-back period, does not conflict with the Vehicle and Traffic Law. Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1193 (2) (b) sets "minimum periods" during which the Commissioner cannot reissue a revoked license, but also vests the Commissioner with complete discretion to determine whether relicensing is appropriate once the statutory minimum revocation period expires.

Constitutional Law -- Ex Post Facto Law -- Regulations Affecting Relicensing of Recidivist Drivers by Department of Motor Vehicles

5. The Department of Motor Vehicles' denial of petitioner recidivist driver's request for a new license did not constitute an impermissible retroactive application of 15 NYCRR 136.5 (b) (3) or a violation of the Ex Post Facto Clause of the US Constitution. Legislation is retroactive when it takes away or impairs vested rights or alters past transactions or considerations. However, a driver's license is a personal privilege subject to reasonable restrictions and revocation by the Commissioner of Motor Vehicles in his or her discretionary powers, not a vested right. Furthermore, regulations affecting the relicensing of recidivist drivers are not retroactive merely because they permit the consideration of an alcohol- or drug-related driving conviction that occurred before their promulgation to determine a person's eligibility to enjoy the privilege of possessing a driver's license. Moreover, the prohibition contained in the Ex Post Facto Clause of the US Constitution applies only to penal statutes and not to regulations such as those here.

249 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v Cesar


2013-02141


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


131 A.D.3d 223; 14 N.Y.S.3d 100; 2015 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6128; 2015 NY Slip Op 06252



July 22, 2015, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-While the trial court could consider defendant's undocumented immigration status in deciding whether he should be incarcerated or placed on probation, in ordering his incarceration, it erred in relying solely on his immigration status to the exclusion of all other relevant factors, as doing so violated the Due Process and Equal Protection clauses of the U.S. and N.Y. Constitutions.


OUTCOME: The sentence was vacated.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Appeal -- Waiver of Right to Appeal -- Constitutional Claims

1. Defendant's otherwise valid waiver of the right to appeal did not foreclose his appeal from a judgment convicting him of driving while intoxicated, for which County Court sentenced him to a period of incarceration rather than to a term of probation based solely on his undocumented immigration status. A defendant may not waive his right to appellate review of certain claims, such as constitutional claims that implicate a larger societal interest in their correct resolution which embraces the reality of fairness in the process itself.

Crimes -- Appeal -- Preservation of Issue for Review -- Appellate Division -- Interest of Justice Jurisdiction

2. The Appellate Division exercised its interest of justice jurisdiction to reach unpreserved issues raised by defendant on his appeal concerning whether County Court's denial of probation in favor of incarceration based solely on defendant's undocumented immigration status violated his constitutional rights. Defendant's appeal raised a novel issue in New York that was likely to reappear in the future, and provided the Court with an opportunity to discuss how and to what extent a defendant's undocumented immigration status may be considered by a sentencing court.

Crimes -- Sentence -- Probation -- Undocumented Immigration Status

3. A defendant's undocumented immigration status may be considered by a sentencing court in determining whether a sentence should include probation, but courts may not rely solely upon a defendant's undocumented immigration status in imposing a sentence of incarceration to the exclusion of all other relevant factors. Accordingly, County Court violated defendant's constitutional rights by denying him probation solely on the basis of his status as an undocumented immigrant under the flawed belief that defendant's continuing violation of federal immigration laws would constitute an automatic violation of the standard conditions of probation. A court's consideration of a defendant's status as an undocumented immigrant is not, in and of itself, a violation of the constitutional separation of powers doctrine. Such consideration is proper given that, pursuant to Penal Law § 65.00 (1) (a), a sentencing court must consider the "history, character and condition of the defendant." However, a sentencing court's refusal to consider a sentence of probation for an undocumented defendant solely on the basis of his or her immigration status violates the Due Process and Equal Protection Clauses of the Federal and New York Constitutions by treating certain defendants differently from others based upon their undocumented presence in this state. Nothing would have prevented County Court from modifying the standard conditions of probation to provide that, while defendant must obey all laws, his undocumented immigration status would not be a basis, in and of itself, for the filing of a violation of probation petition. A sentencing court may consider, in a proper case, potential ways to modify the standard conditions if the defendant might otherwise be an appropriate candidate for probation.

381 Search Warrants Directed to Facebook, Inc. v New York County Dist. Attorney's Off.


30207/13, 30178/14


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


132 A.D.3d 11; 14 N.Y.S.3d 23; 2015 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6067; 2015 NY Slip Op 06201

Motion granted by 381 Search Warrants Directed to Facebook, Inc. v. N.Y. Cnty. Dist. Attorney's Office, 28 N.Y.3d 1134, 2017 N.Y. LEXIS 52 (2017)
Motion granted by 381 Search Warrants Directed to Facebook, Inc. v. N.Y. Cnty. Dist. Attorney's Office, 28 N.Y.3d 1134, 2017 N.Y. LEXIS 49, 69 N.E.3d 626 (2017)
Affirmed by Matter of 381 Search Warrants Directed to Facebook, Inc., 2017 N.Y. LEXIS 767 (N.Y., Apr. 4, 2017)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The trial court properly denied an online social networking service's motion to quash search warrants requiring it to produce user information, because there was no constitutional or statutory right to challenge an alleged defective warrant before it was executed.


OUTCOME: The appeals were dismissed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Search Warrant -- Warrant Served on Online Social Networking Service -- Pre-Enforcement Challenge on Behalf of Target

Petitioner, an online social networking service, could not litigate on its customers' behalf the constitutionality of digital search warrants for customer accounts obtained in connection with a large-scale investigation into the fraudulent filing of Social Security disability claims prior to enforcement. There is no constitutional or statutory right to challenge an alleged defective warrant before it is executed and there was no basis for providing petitioner a greater right than the targets of the warrants are afforded. The sole remedy for challenging the legality of a warrant under the Criminal Procedure Law is by a pretrial suppression motion as there is no mechanism for a motion to quash a search warrant or for taking an appeal from a denial of such a motion. Petitioner's motion to quash the search warrants could not be considered as analogous to a motion to quash a subpoena. While the warrants were issued prior to any pending criminal proceeding, the relief petitioner sought would necessarily impact the subsequent criminal actions. Further, petitioner did not have the right to contest the warrants under the Stored Communications Act (18 USC §§ 2701-2712) (SCA), which provides an Internet Service Provider (ISP) with the right to contest any order or subpoena served upon it. Section 2703 (d) gives the ISP the right to object to court orders and subpoenas issued under section 2703 (b) or (c). The SCA is not a catchall statute designed to protect the privacy of stored Internet communications and it distinguishes disclosure devices under subsections (b) and (c) from warrants which are governed by subsection (a) and require the government to make the traditional and more stringent showing of probable cause. As a finding of probable cause was made by the reviewing judge, the warrants, which required petitioner to disclose electronic content in its electronic storage, were akin to SCA section 2703 (a) warrants.

For The People Theaters of N.Y. Inc. v City of New York


121197/02, 121080/02, 12158


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


131 A.D.3d 279; 14 N.Y.S.3d 338; 2015 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6068; 2015 NY Slip Op 06200



July 21, 2015, Decided 
July 21, 2015, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The trial court properly declared that the 2001 amendments to a city's adult use zoning regulation violated the First Amendment, because, while the amendments were justified as a measure to eradicate the potential for sham compliance with the original ordinance and the signage evidence in the adult video and book stores had been changed to reduce the adult focus and there was no evidence in the record that the floor configurations made the non-adult sections difficult to access, most of the book and video stores and the adult eating and drinking establishments retained a predominant, ongoing focus on sexually explicit activities that included peep shows, topless dancing, and lap dancing.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Freedom of Speech -- Zoning Amendments Restricting Adult Establishments

The 2001 amendments to New York City Zoning Resolution § 12-10, enacted to address concern that certain adult establishments (60/40 establishments), while superficially complying with 1995 zoning amendments requiring that at least 60% of their business be devoted to nonadult uses, retained a predominant, ongoing focus on sexually explicit materials or activities, were an unconstitutional violation of the First Amendment. To prove its theory that the 60/40 establishments' technical compliance with the 1995 amendments was merely a sham, defendant municipality bore the burden of establishing that the essential characteristics or features of the 60/40 uses were similar to those adult uses that were previously found to cause negative secondary effects in the community, based upon criteria such as the presence of large signs advertising adult content, a significant emphasis on the promotion of materials exhibiting "specified sexual activities" or "specified anatomical areas," the exclusion of minors from the premises on the basis of age, and difficulties in accessing nonadult materials. Most of the signage of the adult bookstores and video stores was not graphic, and there was no evidence that any of the stores had adult signs that were larger than those of nearby nonadult businesses, or that the signs advertising adult content were large. The signs had been significantly modified, and signs advertising nonadult stock had been added so as to limit, if not eliminate, any emphasis on adult material. Although the stores still placed a significant emphasis on promoting sexually focused adult materials and activities, most of the stores kept the nonadult materials in the front of the stores. With respect to adult eating and drinking establishments, although the evidence clearly demonstrated an emphasis on the promotion of materials exhibiting "specified sexual activities" or "specified anatomical areas," indicating a predominant sexual focus, there was little to no evidence presented as to the nature of the outward signage and the difficulty in accessing the nonadult material or the nonadult section. There was also no evidence presented as to the nature of the pre-1994 or 100% clubs. That the 60/40 clubs regularly featured topless dancing did not automatically mean that they retained a predominant sexual focus.

 

252 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Constellation Nuclear Power Plants LLC v Tax Appeals Trib. of the State of N.Y.


519639


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


131 A.D.3d 185; 14 N.Y.S.3d 538; 2015 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6052; 2015 NY Slip Op 06183



July 16, 2015, Decided 
July 16, 2015, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal dismissed by, Leave to appeal denied by Constellation Nuclear Power Plants LLC v Tax Appeals Trib., 26 NY3d 996, 2015 N.Y. LEXIS 3581, 19 NYS3d 217, 41 NE3d 75 (N.Y., Oct. 27, 2015)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The Tax Appeals Tribunal properly denied an application by an owner of nuclear power stations for a refund of investment tax credits, as the Tribunal properly analyzed the disputed assets for purposes of former Tax Law § 210(12)(b)(i)(A) (now at Tax Law § 210-B) as a part of a larger system that generated electricity as an end product, and electricity was specifically not included in the term "goods" under the statute; [2]-The owner failed to establish its entitlement to pollution tax credits available to certain industrial waste treatment facilities or air pollution control facilities, used in the taxpayer's trade or business pursuant to former § 210[12][b][i][B], because its assets were admittedly not certified pursuant to former § 210(12)(b)(iii) and the fact that it satisfied federal license procedures and specifications did not excuse its compliance.


OUTCOME: Order affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- Franchise Tax on Business Corporations -- Investment Tax Credits 

1. In a proceeding challenging respondent Tax Appeals Tribunal's determination sustaining the denial of certain investment tax credits under Tax Law former § 210 (12) (b) (i) (A) for assets used at nuclear power plants owned by petitioner, petitioner established neither that its interpretation of the governing statute was the only reasonable construction, nor that the Tribunal's interpretation was irrational or unreasonable. Tax Law former § 210 (12) (b) (i) (A) created tax credits for assets principally used in the production of goods by manufacturing or processing but provided that the term "goods" did not include electricity (§ 210 [12] [b] [i]). The disputed assets converted water to steam and steam to water and were continuously engaged, even when the turbines were shut down and no electricity was being produced. As all of the disputed assets were necessary to the power plants' ultimate purpose of producing electricity and the separate components created water and steam only to serve that purpose, it was not irrational for the Tribunal to analyze the disputed assets as part of the larger system and conclude that the claimed assets were principally used in the production of electricity rather than water or steam. Even when analyzed in isolation, there was a rational basis for the Tribunal's determination that the assets were not engaged in manufacturing or processing. The ongoing conversion of water to steam and then back to its original form as water made no permanent change in the water and yielded no final product, making it more akin to recycling.

Taxation -- Franchise Tax on Business Corporations -- Investment Tax Credits 

2. Petitioner, the owner and operator of nuclear power plants, was not entitled to investment tax credits available to certain "industrial waste treatment facilities or air pollution control facilities, used in the taxpayer's trade or business" (Tax Law former § 210 [12] [b] [i] [B]) where it conceded that it did not have the certifications from the Department of Environmental Conservation (DEC) required by the statute. Tax Law former § 210 (12) (b) (iii) specifically provided that the credits were available only to facilities that had been certified by the DEC as compliant with applicable state environmental, public health, and sanitary statutes, rules and other requirements. Petitioner could not be excused from the certification requirement on the ground that its facilities satisfied federal licensing procedures and specifications that met or exceeded those imposed by the State as courts do not essentially rewrite unambiguous provisions of a statute by ignoring explicit language, no matter how equitable such a result may appear.

Taxation -- Franchise Tax on Business Corporations -- Investment Tax Credits -- Constitutionality of Eligibility Criteria 

3. In a proceeding challenging respondent Tax Appeals Tribunal's determination sustaining the denial of certain investment tax credits for nuclear power plant facilities owned by petitioner, petitioner failed to show that application of Tax Law former § 210 (12) (b) (iii), which provided that certain investment tax credits are available only to facilities that have been certified by the Department of Environmental Conservation (DEC) as compliant with applicable state environmental, public health and sanitary statutes, rules and other requirements, to petitioner violated the Supremacy Clause of the US Constitution. While the federal government has exclusive authority under the doctrine of preemption to regulate the construction and operation of nuclear power plants, making a tax credit available to an eligible taxpayer does not constitute controlling the taxpayer's activities or processes. Tax Law former § 210 (12) (b) (iii) merely set out the criteria for eligibility for pollution tax credits and charged DEC with certifying that those criteria were met. If the preemption doctrine prevented state governments from establishing eligibility criteria for tax credits for nuclear power plants, it would necessarily also prevent them from allowing tax credits to such facilities at all. Petitioner failed to show that, in charging the Nuclear Regulatory Commission with the duty to regulate the operation and construction of nuclear power plants, it was also the clear and manifest purpose of Congress to preempt the allowance of state tax credits to such facilities or the establishment of criteria for such credits.

Taxation -- Franchise Tax on Business Corporations -- Investment Tax Credits -- Constitutionality of Eligibility Criteria 

4. In a proceeding challenging respondent Tax Appeals Tribunal's determination that sustained the denial of certain investment tax credits for nuclear power plant facilities owned by petitioner, petitioner failed to show that it was denied equal protection of the law through application of Tax Law former § 210 (12) (b) (iii), which provides that certain investment tax credits are available only to facilities that have been certified by the Department of Environmental Conservation (DEC) as compliant with applicable state environmental, public health and sanitary statutes, rules and other requirements. Petitioner neither showed that it met the requirements for certification nor that it was treated differently from similarly situated taxpayers and petitioner did not negate every conceivable basis which might support the certification requirement. DEC's denial of the requisite certifications was based on the ground that the assets for which petitioner sought tax credits were not within the categories of equipment eligible to receive DEC certification for such credits under the Tax Law and Environmental Conservation Law.

Taxation -- Franchise Tax on Business Corporations -- Employment Incentive Credits 

5. In a proceeding challenging respondent Tax Appeals Tribunal's determination that sustained the denial of certain investment tax credits under Tax Law former § 210 (12) (b) (i) (A) and (B) for assets used at nuclear power plants owned by petitioner, the Tribunal properly determined that petitioner was not eligible for employment incentive credits pursuant to Tax Law former § 210 (12-D) as petitioner's eligibility for the employment incentive credits depended solely on its eligibility for the investment tax credits.

257 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Loehr v Administrative Bd. of the Cts. of the State of N.Y.


519568


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


130 A.D.3d 89; 13 N.Y.S.3d 260; 2015 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5149; 2015 NY Slip Op 05243



June 18, 2015, Decided 
June 18, 2015, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Leave to appeal denied by Matter of Loehr v. Administrative Bd. of Cts. of State of New York, 26 N.Y.3d 1058, 2015 N.Y. LEXIS 3728, 22 N.Y.S.3d 414, 43 N.E.3d 771 (Nov. 24, 2015)
Reversed by Matter of Loehr v. Administrative Bd. of the Cts. of the State of New York, 2017 N.Y. LEXIS 1245 (N.Y., May 4, 2017)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Respondent's policy prohibiting certificated justices of the supreme court from receiving pension benefits during their employment violated N.Y. Const. art. V, § 7, Judiciary Law § 115(3) and Retirement and Social Security Law § 212 as § 212 allowed the practice; [2]-Respondent did not have discretion to condition recertification upon suspension of a statutory right; [3]-The policy treated certificated justices of the supreme court differently and effectively created two classes of justices; [4]-Judiciary Law § 115 did not empower respondent to make a certificated judgeship a lesser class of employment than a noncertificated judgeship; [5]-It was not rational to claim that double-dipping within the court system created a problem with the public and the other branches of government that related to necessity under N.Y. Const. art. VI, § 25(b).


OUTCOME: Order and judgment reversed. Motion to dismiss denied. Amended petition granted. Declaratory judgment entered.


HEADNOTES

Judges -- Certification of Retired Justice to Remain in Office -- Continued Receipt of State Pension

The policy of respondent Administrative Board of the Courts of New York State that "no judge henceforth certificated for service as a Justice of the Supreme Court pursuant to Judiciary Law § 115 may receive, concurrent with receipt of a salary for such service, a retirement allowance for prior judicial service within the Unified Court System" violated Retirement and Social Security Law § 212, Judiciary Law § 115 (3) and NY Constitution, article V, § 7. Retirement and Social Security Law § 212 (1) explicitly allows New York public employees, including Supreme Court Justices, to retire in place and continue to work while collecting their state pension, without mention of employers or an employer's discretion to condition recertification upon suspension of a statutory right. Moreover, the statute's history supports the conclusion that the legislature intended to give such a right to public employees, not subject to diminishment based on an employer's interests. Respondent's policy effectively created two classes of justices: those who could receive either private retirement benefits or solely nonjudicial public service retirement benefits, and those who were eligible for judicial public service retirement benefits but were not allowed to receive them. Judiciary Law § 115 does not empower respondent to make a certificated judgeship a lesser class of employment than a noncertificated judgeship. A certificated judge is a justice of the Supreme Court in the district in which he or she resides for purposes that include "salary" and "rights" (Judiciary Law § 115 [3]; NY Const, art VI, § 25 [b]). Nor does a Supreme Court Justice's pension-taking fall under the category of necessity when determining a justice's eligibility for certification (NY Const, art VI, § 25 [b]).

261 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Application of Cesar Adrian Vargas for Admission to the Bar of the State of New York.


2013-10725


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


131 A.D.3d 4; 10 N.Y.S.3d 579; 2015 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4587; 2015 NY Slip Op 04657



June 3, 2015, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether applicant, an undocumented immigrant authorized to be present in the U.S. under the Deferred Action for Childhood Arrivals policy and otherwise eligible for admission to the bar, could satisfy the standard of good character and general fitness. [1]-To the extent 8 USC.S. § 1621(d) required a state law to be the sole mechanism by which New York exercised its authority under § 1621(d) to opt out of § 1621(a)'s restrictions on the issuance of law licenses, it unconstitutionally infringed on the State's authority to divide power among its three branches of government; [2]-The judiciary's authority under Judiciary Law § 53(1) to regulate the granting of law licenses included opting out of § 1621(a)'s restrictions to the extent they applied to the admission of attorneys to the bar; [3]-Applicant's immigration status did not reflect adversely on his competence to practice law.


OUTCOME: The immigrant's application was granted, subject to certain conditions.


HEADNOTES

Attorney and Client -- Admission to Practice -- Undocumented Immigrants -- Admission Eligibility

An undocumented immigrant, who is authorized to be present in the United States under the auspices of the Deferred Action for Childhood Arrivals (DACA) policy of the federal government, and who meets the statutory eligibility requirements and the rules of court governing admission to the practice of law in the State of New York, may satisfy the standard of good character and general fitness necessary for admission. Nor is such an individual barred from admission to the practice of law by 8 USC § 1621, which generally prohibits the issuance of state professional licenses to undocumented immigrants unless an individual state has enacted legislation affirmatively authorizing the issuance of such licenses. The undocumented status of an individual applicant does not, alone, suggest that the applicant is not possessed of the qualities that enable attorneys to vigorously defend their client's interests within the bounds of the law, nor does it suggest that the applicant cannot protect, as an officer of the court, the rule of law and the administration of justice. Further, a narrow reading of 8 USC § 1621 (d), so as to require a state legislative enactment to be the sole mechanism by which the State of New York exercises its authority granted in 8 USC § 1621 (d) to opt out of the restrictions on the issuance of licenses imposed by 8 USC § 1621 (a), unconstitutionally infringes under the Tenth Amendment of the United States Constitution on the sovereign authority of the State to divide power among its three coequal branches of government. In light of New York's allocation of authority to the judiciary to regulate the granting of professional licenses to practice law (see Judiciary Law § 53 [1]), the judiciary may exercise its authority as the state sovereign to opt out of the restrictions imposed by section 1621 (a) to the limited extent that those restrictions apply to the admission of attorneys to the practice of law in the State of New York.
