265 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of ELT Harriman, LLC v. Assessor of Town of Woodbury


2012-09618


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


128 A.D.3d 201; 7 N.Y.S.3d 422; 2015 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3317; 2015 NY Slip Op 03356



April 22, 2015, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion granted by Matter of ELT Harriman, LLC v. Assessor of Town of Woodbury, 2016 N.Y. LEXIS 157 (N.Y., Feb. 18, 2016)
Leave to appeal denied by Matter of ELT Harriman, LLC v. Assessor of Town of Woodbury, 2016 N.Y. LEXIS 165 (N.Y., Feb. 18, 2016)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The successor property owner's (SPO) tax proceedings could not qualify as the final order for the moratorium under RPTL 727(1) to be measured against; [2]-A change in the property's ownership was not an exception to the three-year moratorium; [3]-The SPO was not a noncomplicit transferee such that the moratorium violated N.Y. Const. art. XVI, § 2 as it was actually aware of the prior owner's proceedings and settlement negotiations and knew that those proceedings could affect its pecuniary interests, but took no action to intervene; [4]-The reduced assessment of $ 2,618,551 did not exceed the property's fair market value (FMV) such that the assessment violated art. XVI, § 2 as it equated to an $ 8,250,000 value, and the SPO had listed the property for sale for $ 9,750,000; [5]-The sale price between the prior owner and the SPO was a distress sale and did not reflect the FMV.


OUTCOME: Order affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- Assessment -- Three-Year Moratorium on Tax Certiorari Proceedings -- Applicability to Successor Property Owner 

1. In related RPTL article 7 proceedings challenging respondents' 2008, 2009, and 2010 assessments of petitioner's real property, the three-year moratorium of RPTL 727 (1) was applicable, based upon a consent judgment reducing the assessment for the years 2006 and 2007 entered in tax certiorari proceedings commenced by the prior owner of the property, warranting dismissal of the petitions. RPTL 727 (1), which provides that when an assessment is found to be unlawful, unequal, excessive or misclassified by a final court order or judgment, "the assessed valuation so determined shall not be changed for such property for the next three succeeding [annual] assessment rolls," applies, even where, as here, the final order or judgment is entered upon stipulation of the parties. The plain language of RPTL 727 (1) imposes a moratorium upon further challenges to the property's tax assessment for a three-year period which, in this instance, comprised the years 2008, 2009, and 2010. Petitioner's own tax proceedings could not qualify as the "final order or judgment" for the moratorium to be measured against, as petitioner's proceedings resulted in no judicial findings that the assessed valuations for 2008 through 2010 were "unlawful, unequal, excessive or misclassified," as expressly required by RPTL 727 (1).

Taxation -- Assessment -- Three-Year Moratorium on Tax Certiorari Proceedings -- No Exception for Change in Ownership 

2. In related RPTL article 7 proceedings challenging respondents' 2008, 2009, and 2010 assessments of petitioner's real property, the three-year moratorium on tax certiorari proceedings provided in RPTL 727 (1) was applicable, based upon a consent judgment reducing the assessment for the years 2006 and 2007 entered in tax certiorari proceedings commenced by the prior owner of the property, despite the change in the property's ownership. The recognized exceptions to the three-year moratorium address situations where changes in circumstances defeat the statute's purpose of locking in judicially settled assessments to maintain the status quo, because the changed circumstances themselves upset the status quo (RPTL 727 [2]). A change in a property's ownership is not listed in the statute as an exception, and under the doctrine of expressio unius est exclusio alterius, the presence of exceptions in RPTL 727 (2), without inclusion of a property sale exception among them, must be inferentially construed as the Legislature's intent not to except circumstances where property is sold to a new owner. Logically, the sale of property to a purchaser would not be an appropriate exception to include within the enumerated exceptions of RPTL 727 (2), as such an exception might encourage sales to "straw buyers" as a means of circumventing the three-year moratorium of RPTL 727 (1) and thereby defeat the purpose of the statute.

Taxation -- Assessment -- Three-Year Moratorium on Tax Certiorari Proceedings -- Constitutionality of Application to Successor Property Owner

3. In related RPTL article 7 proceedings challenging respondents' 2008, 2009, and 2010 assessments of petitioner's real property, the application of the three-year moratorium on tax certiorari proceedings provided in RPTL 727 (1), based upon a consent judgment reducing the assessment for the years 2006 and 2007 entered in tax certiorari proceedings commenced by the prior owner of the property, was not unconstitutional. Petitioner could have undertaken procedural steps to participate in the negotiations that resulted in the assessment reduction, but did not do so. Despite being aware of the prior owner's proceedings and of settlement negotiations that lasted for approximately a year, and aware that those proceedings could affect its pecuniary interests, petitioner made no timely effort to intervene in those proceedings or to join or consolidate them with its own tax certiorari proceedings. Thus, petitioner was not a "noncomplicit transferee," as it had negotiated the purchase of the property fully cognizant of the prior owner's tax proceedings, as demonstrated by the contract of sale, without negotiating a mechanism for adjusting the purchase price for the property to take into account those ongoing proceedings. Furthermore, the record failed to establish that the reduced $2,618,551 assessment exceeded the property's fair market value so as to render the assessment unconstitutional under New York Constitution, article XVI, § 2. The assessment equated to a market value of $8,250,000, and petitioner had listed the property with a commercial real estate broker for $ 9,750,000. With regard to the property's sale price between the prior owner and petitioner, the sale had all the indicia of a distress sale and was not reflective of a fair market value.

268 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of The Torok Trust v Town Bd. of Town of Alexandria


375 CA 14-01460


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


128 A.D.3d 97; 7 N.Y.S.3d 748; 2015 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2645; 2015 NY Slip Op 02632



March 27, 2015, Decided 
March 27, 2015, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Leave to appeal dismissed by Matter of Torok Trust v Town Bd. of Town of Alexandria, 25 NY3d 1098, 2015 N.Y. LEXIS 1422, 14 NYS3d 323, 35 NE3d 491 (N.Y., June 11, 2015)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-A school district was properly directed to issue a tax refund to a taxpayer pursuant to RPTL 727(1), as a reduction of the property assessment was warranted because application of § 727(1), based on a stipulation between the parties agreeing to a lower assessment for a tax year, resulted in an automatic reduction in the assessment for the following school tax year without the need for any filing of a tax certiorari proceeding.


OUTCOME: Order affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- Assessment -- Settlement of Assessment Challenge -- Automatic Reduction in Succeeding Assessments

In a tax certiorari proceeding challenging the assessed valuation of petitioner's property for 2007, where the parties agreed to a reduced assessment for the 2007 tax year and the local school district issued petitioner a refund for the 2007-2008 school tax year pursuant to that agreement, petitioner was also entitled to a refund for the 2008-2009 school tax year, notwithstanding that it did not commence a tax certiorari proceeding for the 2008 tax year. RPTL 727 (1) imposes a three-year freeze to the "next three succeeding assessment rolls" from the "date of the most recent assessment under review" where an order or judgment is issued determining that the assessment is unlawful, unequal, excessive, or misclassified. A stipulation between the parties agreeing to a lower assessment has the same effect. The assessment under review here was for the 2007 tax year, and therefore the assessment rolls from 2008 through 2010 were required to have that same assessment. Thus, the application of RPTL 727 (1) resulted in an automatic reduction in the assessment for the 2008-2009 school tax year, without the need for any filing of a tax certiorari proceeding for the 2008 tax year. Requiring petitioner to commence a tax certiorari proceeding for the 2008-2009 school tax year would go against the interest in reduced litigation contemplated by the statutory respite period.

270 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of County of Chautauqua v Shah


109 CA 14-00923


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


126 A.D.3d 1317; 6 N.Y.S.3d 334; 2015 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2246; 2015 NY Slip Op 02245



March 20, 2015, Decided 
March 20, 2015, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The counties' claims that L 2012, ch. 56, pt. D, § 61 was unconstitutional as it impermissibly deprived them of vested rights to repayment under Social Services Law § 368-a in violation of their rights under the Due Process Clauses, N.Y. Const. art. I, § 6 and U.S. Const. amend. XIV, § 1, were rejected as counties were not persons within the meaning of the state and federal constitutions and could not raise a due process argument against the State; [2]-A municipality could not invoke the protections of the Fourteenth Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. XIV, against its own state as a municipal corporation, in its own right, received no protection from the Due Process Clause vis-a-vis its creating state; [3]-The state's highest court has concluded that the same principle applied equally under the Due Process Clause of the New York State Constitution.


OUTCOME: Judgment modified. Declaratory judgment entered declaring that § had not been shown to be unconstitutional. As modified, judgment affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law--Due Process of Law--Counties Seeking Reimbursement from State for Medicaid Overburden Expenditures Not Persons within Meaning of Due Process Clause

273 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of State of New York v Frank P.


30096/10, 13572


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


126 A.D.3d 150; 2 N.Y.S.3d 483; 2015 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1507; 2015 NY Slip Op 01551



February 19, 2015, Decided 
February 19, 2015, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-In a proceeding under Mental Hygiene Law art. 10, the order of civil commitment of an inmate who was deemed a dangerous sex offender was improperly imposed because the State failed to establish by clear and convincing evidence that the inmate had or would have serious difficulty controlling his behavior for purposes of Mental Hygiene Law §§ 10.03(e) and 10.07(f); [2]-The State's evidence was lacking, as it was based on the inmate's criminal history that occurred prior to his 33-year incarceration, and two expert witnesses who expressed their opinions in a conclusory fashion.


OUTCOME: Order reversed; Proceeding dismissed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Sex Offenders -- Civil Commitment or Supervision -- Requirement That Offender Has Serious Difficulty Controlling His Behavior

A proceeding pursuant to Mental Hygiene Law article 10 seeking civil commitment of respondent as a dangerous sex offender was dismissed on the ground that the State failed to establish by clear and convincing evidence that respondent has or will have serious difficulty controlling his behavior. The New York statutory structure requires the State to prove that the individual is dangerous and the dangerousness must be coupled with a mental abnormality, which incorporates the additional requirement that the offender have serious difficulty with behavioral control. Besides relying on respondent's criminal history, which occurred prior to his 33-year incarceration, the State relied primarily on two expert witnesses, both of whom diagnosed respondent as suffering from two abnormalities (paraphilia not otherwise specified [paraphilia NOS] and antisocial personality disorder [ASPD]). However, a diagnosis of ASPD cannot serve as the factual predicate of sexual abnormality under article 10. Moreover, as to paraphilia NOS, neither expert conducted a quantified analysis of the factors that led to their conclusion of lack of control, and concluding that a respondent has a volitional impairment from only a diagnosis of sexual abnormality violates the requirement that the State prove separately from the abnormality that a sex offender has serious difficulty controlling his behavior. The concept of predisposition and volition are separate and distinct. Respondent spent 33 consecutive years in prison and there was no evidence that he engaged in any inappropriate sexual behavior during that prolonged period to suggest that he had serious difficulty controlling his behavior in such an environment. Rather, defendant voluntarily attended anger management and sex offender treatment programs while in prison.

OUTCOME: Order affirmed and matter remitted.


HEADNOTES

279 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of NYC C.L.A.S.H., Inc. v New York State Off. of Parks, Recreation & Historic Preserv.


519023


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


125 A.D.3d 105; 2 N.Y.S.3d 231; 2014 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9001; 2014 NY Slip Op 09085



December 31, 2014, Decided 
December 31, 2014, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Leave to appeal denied by NYC C.L.A.S.H., Inc. v N.Y. State Office of Parks, Rec. & Historic Preserv., 25 NY3d 963, 2015 NY LEXIS 727, 8 NYS3d 259, 30 NE3d 903 (N.Y., Apr. 2, 2015)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The New York State Office of Parks, Recreation and Historic Preservation's (OPRHP) rule establishing smoke-free areas in certain outdoor locations, 9 NYCRR 386.1, which also prohibited smoking in each state park in New York City, with limited exceptions, did not violate the separation of powers doctrine as OPRHP did not improperly balance economic and social concerns against its stated goal in order to act on its own ideas of sound public policy; [2]-All aspects of the regulation were grounded in OPRHP's stated purpose and goals; [3]-The designation of no smoking areas was no longer a subject of great public debate; [4]-PRHPL 3.09(2) and (5) instructed OPRHP to operate and maintain its sites while providing for the health, safety and welfare of the public; [5]-The rule did not intrude upon legislative authority; [6]-9 NYCRR 386.1 was not arbitrary and capricious.


OUTCOME: Order and judgment modified. As modified, judgment affirmed. Petition dismissed. Declaratory judgment entered that 9 NYCRR 386.1 was not unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Administrative Law -- Validity of Regulation -- Smoke-Free Regulation Promulgated by Office of Parks, Recreation and Historic Preservation -- Separation of Powers 

1. Respondents, the Office of Parks, Recreation and Historic Preservation (OPRHP) and its Commissioner, did not usurp the legislature's prerogative by promulgating 9 NYCRR 386.1, which establishes smoke-free areas in certain limited outdoor locations under OPRHP's jurisdiction and prohibits smoking in each state park located in New York City, with limited exceptions (see 9 NYCRR 386.1 [a] [2]). OPRHP did not improperly balance economic and social concerns against its stated goal in order to act on its own ideas of sound public policy. All aspects of the regulation are grounded in OPRHP's stated purpose--to allow all patrons to enjoy the fresh air and natural beauty of its outdoor facilities; the choices made by OPRHP were not very difficult or complex; and the designation of no smoking areas is not a continued subject of great public debate. Furthermore, OPRHP did not write on a clean slate, creating its own comprehensive set of rules. The legislature has instructed OPRHP to "[o]perate and maintain" its sites while "[p]rovid[ing] for the health, safety and welfare of the public using [those sites]" (PRHPL 3.09 [2], [5]). Nor did OPRHP improperly intrude upon an area that is a matter of legislative debate. Finally, sufficient agency expertise from OPRHP was required to enact the rule such that it did not intrude upon legislative authority. OPRHP considered the nature of the properties under its jurisdiction in order to determine how best to locate the no smoking areas, as evidenced by the rule's treatment of parks in New York City, which differ in size and setting from the parks elsewhere in the state.

Administrative Law -- Validity of Regulation -- Smoke-Free Regulation Promulgated by Office of Parks, Recreation and Historic Preservation -- Applicability of Regulation to State Parks Located in New York City 

2. 9 NYCRR 386.1, which establishes smoke-free areas in certain limited outdoor locations under the jurisdiction of respondent Office of Parks, Recreation and Historic Preservation (OPRHP), was not arbitrary and capricious in that it treated state parks located in New York City differently from those throughout the rest of the state. Pursuant to 9 NYCRR 386.1, smoking is prohibited throughout state parks located in New York City, except that the Commissioner of OPRHP may allow smoking in limited areas, whereas, in parks throughout the rest of the state, smoking is permitted except in designated nonsmoking areas. As determined by respondents, the greater restriction in New York City parks was due to their smaller size, which permitted patrons to easily walk to an area outside of a park to smoke. Furthermore, since smoking was already prohibited in New York City's public parks, respondents determined that the nearby state parks should be managed in a consistent manner. OPRHP's different treatment of parks located in New York City was not unreasonable.

281 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of County of St. Lawrence v. Shah


518063


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


124 A.D.3d 88; 998 N.Y.S.2d 474; 2014 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8230; 2014 NY Slip Op 08278



November 26, 2014, Decided 
November 26, 2014, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected January 5, 2015.
Leave to appeal granted by Matter of County of St. Lawrence v. Shah, 25 N.Y.3d 903, 2015 N.Y. LEXIS 767, 7 N.Y.S.3d 275, 30 N.E.3d 166 (2015)
Stay denied by Cnty. of St. Lawrence v. Shah, 25 N.Y.3d 1058, 2015 N.Y. LEXIS 1315, 11 N.Y.S.3d 541, 33 N.E.3d 497 (2015)
Motion granted by Matter of County of St. Lawrence v. Shah, 27 N.Y.3d 1181, 2016 N.Y. LEXIS 2463, 59 N.E.3d 1202 (2016)
Motion granted by Matter of County of St. Lawrence v. Shah, 27 N.Y.3d 1181, 2016 N.Y. LEXIS 2454, 59 N.E.3d 1202 (2016)
Reversed by, in part, Petition dismissed by, Judgment entered by Matter of County of Chemung v. Shah, 2016 N.Y. LEXIS 3420 (N.Y., Oct. 27, 2016)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-An amendment barring reimbursement of petitioner county's Medicaid claims was not unconstitutional because it did not retroactively extinguish a vested right to reimbursement, under Social Services Law § 368-a(1)(h), but only imposed a statute of limitations; [3]-A failure to set a grace period was cured because the statute was interpreted to permit filing otherwise time-barred claims within six months of the reversal of the trial court's declaration that the amendment was unconstitutional; [4]-It was no error to order respondent Department of Health (DOH) to pay the county's claims because Social Services Law § 368-a(1) stated DOH "shall" reimburse such expenditures without requiring a claim, and the state could not avoid this duty, so the county was entitled to mandamus relief.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed as modified.


HEADNOTES

Health -- Medicaid Reimbursement Payments -- Reimbursement of Overburden Expenditures -- Timeliness of Claims 

1. The 2012 amendment (L 2012, ch 56, § 1, part D, § 61) to the Medicaid Cap Statute (L 2005, ch 58, § 1, part C, § 1), cited by respondent Department of Health as the basis for denying petitioner county's reimbursement claims for overburden expenditures incurred prior to 2006, is not unconstitutional since it did not retroactively extinguish petitioner's vested right to such reimbursement pursuant to Social Services Law § 368-a (1) (h). Under Social Services Law § 368-a (1) (h), petitioner's right to reimbursement of overburden expenditures accrued when it made payment to the State for those expenses for which no local share was owed, i.e., prior to January 1, 2006. The 2012 amendment did not specifically repeal any part of Social Services Law § 368-a or affect the counties' inherent right to reimbursement. Rather, the amendment simply imposed a statute of limitations for the payment of reimbursement claims by barring claims submitted after a certain date. A statute of limitations does not impair an underlying substantive right, but may deprive a litigant of any remedy.

Statutes -- Retroactive Application of Statute -- New Statute of Limitations for Submission of Certain Medicaid Reimbursement Claims to State -- Procedural Due Process -- Reasonable Grace Period 

2. Social services districts may submit claims to the Department of Health for the reimbursement of any pre-2006 overburden expenditures pursuant to Social Services Law § 368-a (1) (h) within six months of the Appellate Division's reversal of the Supreme Court's declaration that the 2012 amendment (L 2012, ch 56, § 1, part D, § 61) to the Medicaid Cap Statute (L 2005, ch 58, § 1, part C, § 1) was unconstitutional; any claims submitted thereafter are barred as untimely. Although potential litigants have no right or vested interest in any specific limitations period, when a limitations period is statutorily shortened, or created where none existed before, due process requires a reasonable grace period before the time bar takes effect. While the legislature did not expressly set a grace period in the 2012 amendment, the constitutional validity of the retrospective application of a new statute may be upheld by interpreting it as permitting the filing, within a reasonable time after the statute's effective date, of claims that would otherwise be time-barred. However, due to possible reliance by potential claimants on Supreme Court's declaration that the 2012 amendment was unconstitutional, it was reasonable to run the grace period from the date of the Appellate Division's decision reversing that declaration, rather than from the enactment of the statute.

Proceeding against Body or Officer -- Mandamus -- Department of Health's Reimbursement for Medicaid Overburden Expenditures Incurred by County 

3. Since respondent Department of Health's obligation to reimburse social services districts for Medicaid overburden expenditures is mandatory and ministerial and does not involve any discretion, petitioner county was entitled to a writ of mandamus requiring respondent to review its records to identify any individuals who may not have been properly coded as overburden patients, and all medical services that were provided to individuals who were or were not properly coded as overburden patients, then process all pre-2006 overburden expenditure reimbursements to petitioner. Social Services Law § 368-a (1) states that respondent "shall" reimburse petitioner for, among other things, overburden expenditures; the statute does not include any requirement that petitioner make a claim for those payments. Thus, respondent Commissioner was required to pay those reimbursements even without any claims being made, and should have done so by 2006.

Matter of Greenberg v. Assessor of Town of Scarsdale


2012-09046


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


121 A.D.3d 986; 996 N.Y.S.2d 48; 2014 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7140; 2014 NY Slip Op 07160



October 22, 2014, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Administrative determinations may only be challenged via CPLR art. 78 review after the determination is final (CPLR 7801(1)); [2]-The issuance of written recommendations by the Executive Director of the Westchester County Tax Commission was not a final approval or denial of appellant's tax refund applications; [3]-Accordingly, this issue was not ripe for review, and the Supreme Court (SC), on this basis, properly directed the dismissal of the tenth cause of action in Matter No. 2 and the causes of action in Matter No. 4 seeking this relief; [4]-The SC, erred in sua sponte directing the dismissal of the first through ninth causes of action in Matter No. 2; [5]-The SC properly granted that branch of a motion which was to dismiss, as time-barred, so much of the petition as asserted claims pursuant to RPTL art. 7 for tax years 2005 through 2008.


OUTCOME: The appeal from so much of the order as, sua sponte, directed the dismissal of the complaint/petition in Matter No. 2 and so much of the petition in Matter No. 4 as alleged causes of action pursuant to CPLR art. 78 related to certain tax refund applications is deemed to be an application for leave to appeal from those portions of the order, and leave to appeal was granted. Further, the order was modified. Respondents' motion to strike was denied.


HEADNOTES

Courts--Ripeness Doctrine--Proceeding Seeking Real Property Tax Refund--Administrative Action on Request for Tax Refund Not Final

Dismissal and Nonsuit--Premature Dismissal--Hybrid CPLR Article 78 Proceeding and Action for Damages and Declaratory Relief

Limitation of Actions--30-Day Statute of Limitations--Action Seeking Tax Refund

Actions--Joinder--Common Questions of Law and Fact in Hybrid Proceeding and Action

 

289 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Retired Pub. Empls. Assn., Inc. v Cuomo


517749


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


123 A.D.3d 92; 995 N.Y.S.2d 757; 2014 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7030; 2014 NY Slip Op 07044



October 16, 2014, Decided 
October 16, 2014, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Civil Service Law § 167(8) provided authority for the State of New York's actions in effectuating the reduced state contribution rates to the retirees' health premium and subscription costs under the New York State Health Insurance Plan; [2]-The retirees' rights under the Contracts Clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 10, cl. 1, and the Due Process Clause, N.Y. Const. art. I, § 1 were not violated as § 167(1)(a) did not employ terms that signalled an intent to create a contractual or vested right; [3]-Section 167(1)(a) reflected a policy determination as to the State's contribution rate towards retiree health insurance premiums that was subject to change at the will of the legislature; [4]-Section 167(8) was not an unconstitutional delegation of legislative authority as the standard provided in § 167(8) provided adequate guidance for the state agency's exercise of its discretion.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Civil Service -- Retirement and Pension Benefits -- Reduction in State's Percentage Contribution Toward Retirees' Health Insurance Premiums

1. In a hybrid CPLR article 78 proceeding and declaratory judgment action by petitioners, former New York State employees receiving a fixed percentage retirement allowance from the state pursuant to Civil Service Law §167 (1) (a), challenging the state's reduced contribution towards their health insurance benefits after the state and an employee organization entered into a collective bargaining agreement that reduced the state's contribution to current employees' health insurance premiums, respondents' extension of the reduced contribution rates to petitioners was lawful and in compliance with Civil Service Law §167. Nothing in the language of Civil Service Law §167 (1) (a) constituted clear and irresistible evidence that the legislature intended to create a contractual or vested right to the higher contribution rates set forth therein. In amending section 167, the legislature's use of the phrase "[n]otwithstanding any inconsistent provision of law" in subdivision (8) expressed its intent to preempt any other potentially conflicting statute, including the fixed contribution rates set forth in subdivision (1) (a). The legislature also expressly granted the Civil Service Commission the discretion to extend modifications in contribution rates to "employees or retirees" not covered by a collective bargaining agreement. Thus, the plain and unambiguous language of Civil Service Law §167 (8) permitted reductions in the state's contribution towards petitioners' health insurance premiums in the event that a collective bargaining agreement between the state and an employee organization so provided. Moreover, since the Commission's discretion was entirely dependent upon and limited by the terms of a collective bargaining agreement, reasonable safeguards and standards were in place such that the statute did not amount to an unconstitutional delegation of legislative authority.

301 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Caprio v. New York State Dept. of Taxation & Fin.


651176/11, 11231


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


117 A.D.3d 168; 987 N.Y.S.2d 4; 2014 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2335; 2014 NY Slip Op 2399



April 8, 2014, Decided 
April 8, 2014, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reversed by, Summary judgment granted by, Judgment entered by, Certified question answered by Caprio v. New York State Dept. of Taxation & Fin., 2015 N.Y. LEXIS 1530 (N.Y., July 1, 2015)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The retroactive application of the 2010 amendment to Tax Law § 632(a)(2) violated the taxpayers' federal and state due process rights as the taxpayers reasonably relied on the old law in structuring their transaction, and had no forewarning of the change; [2]-Before the 2010 amendment, the Tax Law contained no specific provision governing a nonresident's receipt of payments from an S-corporation's distribution of an installment obligation under 26 U.S.C.S. § 453(h)(1)(A). The taxpayers made a compelling argument that under the previous law, those payments were not taxable by New York; [3]-The three-and-one-half year length of the retroactive period favored the taxpayers; [4]-The public purpose for retroactive application factor favored the taxpayers as raising money for the state budget did not warrant retroactivity where the other factors militated against it.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed. Judgment entered for taxpayers.


HEADNOTES

Statutes -- Retroactive Application of Statute -- Change to Tax Law -- Due Process Violation 

1. An amendment to section 632 (a) (2) of the Tax Law was impermissibly applied retroactively to a transaction entered into by plaintiff nonresident taxpayers more than 3½ years earlier. Plaintiffs reasonably relied on the old law in structuring their transaction, and had no forewarning of the change in the legislation. In light of plaintiffs' reliance on the prior law, the excessive length of the retroactive period, and the absence of a compelling public purpose, a due process violation occurred by the retroactive application of the statute.

Taxation -- Personal Income Tax -- Retroactive Application of Statute Affecting Subchapter S Election -- Taxpayer's Lack of Warning of Change in Statute 

2. Plaintiff taxpayers satisfied the first prong of three-prong test to determine whether the retroactive application of a tax statute passes constitutional muster with regard to an amendment to section 632 (a) (2) of the Tax Law, which changed the manner of computing the tax on a taxpayer's sale of stock of a subchapter S corporation. Plaintiffs had no actual forewarning of the change made by the amendment, which was not even proposed to the legislature until long after plaintiffs had entered into the transaction which the state sought to tax retroactively. Thus, plaintiffs had no warning and no opportunity to alter their behavior in anticipation of the impact of the amendment. Plaintiffs made a compelling argument that under the previous law the payments for the sale of the stock of their subchapter S corporation were not taxable, and, hence, they reasonably relied on existing law to conclude that those payments were not taxable. While defendants asserted that there was a long-standing policy of taxing transactions similar to plaintiffs', the record contained no evidence that the New York State Department of Taxation and Finance took any steps to inform taxpayers of that policy. Plaintiffs were not required to seek an advisory opinion from the Department before entering into the transaction in question, inasmuch as a reasonable reading of the Tax Law as it then existed was that the transaction was not subject to New York tax. Moreover, the law does not require plaintiffs to show a specific proposed alternative course of action to demonstrate that they reasonably relied on the existing scheme of taxation.

Taxation -- Personal Income Tax -- Retroactive Application of Statute Affecting Subchapter S Election -- Length of Retroactive Period 

3. Plaintiff taxpayers satisfied the second prong of three-prong test to determine whether the retroactive application of a tax statute passes constitutional muster with regard to an amendment to section 632 (a) (2) of the Tax Law, which changed the manner of computing the tax on a taxpayer's sale of stock of a subchapter S corporation, where the amendment was enacted some 3½ years prior to the challenged transaction, nearly three times longer than a period that had been found excessive by the Court of Appeals in a prior case examining the retroactive applicability of statutes (see James Sq. Assoc. LP v Mullen, 21 NY3d 233, 993 NE2d 374, 970 NYS2d 888 [2013]). The excessive period of retroactivity was long enough so that plaintiffs gained a reasonable expectation that they would secure repose in the existing tax scheme. Moreover, no legislative history was revealed to indicate that the legislature was correcting any specific error in the existing law. Inasmuch as the transaction in question would unquestionably be subject to New York tax under the new law, it could fairly be considered a new tax and a lengthy period of retroactivity would not be warranted.

Taxation -- Personal Income Tax -- Retroactive Application of Statute Affecting Subchapter S Election -- Public Purpose of Amendment

4. Plaintiff taxpayers satisfied the third prong of three-prong test to determine whether the retroactive application of a tax statute passes constitutional muster with regard to an amendment to section 632 (a) (2) of the Tax Law, which changed the manner of computing the tax on a taxpayer's sale of stock of a subchapter S corporation, where the public purpose of the amendment was to implement an executive budget by raising tax revenues. Raising money for the state budget is not a particularly compelling justification and is insufficient to warrant retroactivity where, as here, other factors, such as the lack of any warning of the change to the taxpayers and the excessive length of the retroactive period, militated against it. Inasmuch as plaintiffs were merely following the law as it then existed, it could not be considered either a "windfall" or unfair for them to escape additional taxation resulting from the retroactive application of the amendment.

183 CA 13-00640


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT

307 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v Brown


2012-07905


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


115 A.D.3d 155; 979 N.Y.S.2d 367; 2014 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 520; 2014 NY Slip Op 524



January 29, 2014, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Amended March 13, 2014.
Leave to appeal granted by People v. Brown, 23 N.Y.3d 961, 2014 N.Y. LEXIS 1536, 988 N.Y.S.2d 568, 11 N.E.3d 718 (2014)
Motion granted by People v. Brown, 23 N.Y.3d 1007, 2014 N.Y. LEXIS 1412, 992 N.Y.S.2d 768, 16 N.E.3d 1247 (2014)
Affirmed by People v. Brown, 2015 N.Y. LEXIS 1087 (N.Y., May 14, 2015)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether a parolee was in New York State Department of Corrections and Community Supervision (DOCCS) custody for CPL 440.46(1) purposes. HOLDINGS: [1]-Defendant parolee was in DOCCS custody and was eligible for resentencing since art. 440.46(1), as amended in 2001, did not distinguish between defendants who were incarcerated and parolees for purposes of determining custodial eligibility; [2]-Art. 440.46(1) encompassed any person in DOCCS custody; under Executive Law § 259-i(2)(b), a non-incarcerated parolee was within the legal custody of DOCCS; a non-incarcerated parolee was eligible to apply for resentencing; [3]-The New York State legislature did not limit the term "custody" to persons actually incarcerated as Executive Law § 259-i(2)(b) stated that a parolee was in the legal custody of DOCCS until return to imprisonment in the custody of the department.


OUTCOME: Judgment was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Sentence -- Resentencing under Drug Law Reform Act -- Eligibility -- Parolees 

Nonincarcerated parolees are eligible for resentencing under CPL 440.46 (1), which, originally codified under the 2009 Drug Law Reform Act (DLRA), allows persons in the "custody" of the Department of Corrections and Community Supervision (DOCCS) and serving sentences for certain drug-related offenses to seek resentencing. Under Executive Law § 259-i (2) (b), parolees are within the "legal custody" of DOCCS, and CPL 440.46 (1) does not distinguish between incarcerated persons and parolees for purposes of determining custodial eligibility for resentencing. Previously, only persons in the "custody" of the Department of Correctional Services (DOCS) were allowed to apply for resentencing (see L 2009, ch 56, § 1, part AAA, § 9 [enacting CPL 440.46 (1)]), and, at that time, parolees were in the "legal custody" of the Division of Parole (see Executive Law § 259-i [former (2) (b)]). Thus, only incarcerated offenders were eligible for resentencing. Upon the subsequent merger of DOCS and the Division of Parole into DOCCS, however, CPL 440.46 (1) was amended to permit any otherwise qualified person in the "custody" of DOCCS to apply for resentencing. Moreover, the 2009 DLRA's purpose was to grant relief to low-level nonviolent drug offenders from inordinately harsh punishments, and, in effecting the DOCCS merger, the legislature sought to provide for a seamless network for care, custody, treatment and supervision from the commencement of a person's sentence of state imprisonment until his or her discharge from supervision in the community. It is entirely consistent with that legislative history that the legislature chose not to exclude parolees from resentencing relief.

 

308 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Pines v State of New York


2011-02821


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


115 A.D.3d 80; 979 N.Y.S.2d 142; 2014 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 378; 2014 NY Slip Op 335



January 22, 2014, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal dismissed by Pines v State, 23 NY3d 982, 2014 N.Y. LEXIS 1314, 989 NYS2d 13, 11 NE3d 1117 (N.Y., June 5, 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The supreme court erred in declaring that compensation of judges and justices of the Unified Court System of the State of New York was increased under L 2009, ch. 51, § 3 because the Legislature did not adjust judicial compensation through the enactment of L 2009, ch. 51, § 3; [2]-The statute merely appropriated a lump sum for compensation adjustments, and nothing in the plain language of the statute established, within that gross amount, what monies were to be specifically paid to the justices and what lesser or greater sums were to be paid to the jurists of the other courts of the State; [3]-The new salary schedules the supreme court directed to be implemented based on the plain language of the statute were not only stricken from the enacted version of the statute, but they directly conflicted with the duly-enacted salary schedules contained in Judiciary Law §§ 220-224.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed.


HEADNOTES

Judges -- Disqualification -- Rule of Necessity 

1. The Appellate Division, Second Department, was required, pursuant to the Rule of Necessity, to determine, in a declaratory judgment action commenced by plaintiffs, judges and justices of the Unified Court System, whether judicial compensation was increased by the legislature through the enactment of Laws of 2009, chapter 51, § 3. Although the members of the Court had a pecuniary interest in the case and would be affected by the outcome of the appeal, their recusal would leave plaintiffs without a legal remedy as the self-interest implicated by the issues raised on appeal provided grounds for disqualifying not only the members of the Court but also the members of any other judicial body that might have replaced them. The participation of an independent, unbiased adjudicator in the resolution of disputes is an essential element of due process of law. Thus, in order to ensure the dignity of the judiciary and maintain the integrity of the administration of justice, ordinarily, when a judge has an interest in litigation, recusal is warranted. Nevertheless, where all the members of the adjudicative body are disqualified and no other body exists to which the appeal might be referred for disposition, the Rule of Necessity ensures that neither the parties nor the legislature will be left without the remedy provided by law.

Judges -- Judicial Salaries -- Increases in Judicial Compensation under Laws of 2009, Chapter 51, § 3 -- Statutory Construction -- Plain Language

2. In a declaratory judgment action commenced by plaintiffs, judges and justices of the Unified Court System, Supreme Court erred in concluding that judicial compensation was increased by the legislature through the enactment of Laws of 2009, chapter 51, § 3 based upon the plain language of the statute. Laws of 2009, chapter 51, § 3 contained no language adjusting the salary levels for the various classes of judges and justices, and an interpretation of the statute that transferred the authority to set the salary levels, sub silentio, to the Chief Judge would violate the Compensation Clause of the New York State Constitution. The Compensation Clause requires judicial compensation to be set by the legislature through the enactment of affirmative legislation (NY Const, art VI, § 25 [a]), and, pursuant to that directive, the legislature enacted Judiciary Law article 7-B, which sets forth the salaries to be paid to the various classes of judges and justices. Although Laws of 2009, chapter 51, § 3 appropriated a gross amount of money "[f]or expenses necessary to fund adjustments in the compensation of state-paid judges and justices of the unified court system," nothing in the plain language of the statute established, within that gross amount, what monies were to be specifically paid to the various classes of judges and justices. Supreme Court's determination that the gross amount was to be disbursed by the Chief Judge pursuant to the salary schedule proposed by the judiciary in its budget request for the relevant year ran afoul of the Compensation Clause inasmuch as the judiciary's proposed salary schedule was not enacted as part of Laws of 2009, chapter 51, § 3.

Judges -- Judicial Salaries -- Increases in Judicial Compensation under Laws of 2009, chapter 51, § 3 -- Statutory Construction -- Legislative Intent

3. In a declaratory judgment action commenced by plaintiffs, judges and justices of the Unified Court System, consideration of extrinsic information evincing legislative intent failed to establish that the legislature intended to increase judicial compensation through the enactment of Laws of 2009, chapter 51, § 3. The legislature's decision not to explicitly repeal or revise Judiciary Law article 7-B, which sets forth the salaries to be paid to the various classes of judges and justices, constituted evidence that additional legislation was required before the funds appropriated by Laws of 2009, chapter 51, § 3 for adjustments in judicial compensation could be disbursed. Laws of 2009, chapter 51, § 3 appropriated funds for adjustments in judicial compensation but omitted proposed language indicating that the adjustments would be accomplished "[pursuant to a subsequent chapter of law specifying such salary levels]." Although, in previous years, similarly enacted appropriations included such references to future legislation, the omission thereof from Laws of 2009, chapter 51, § 3 did not evince a legislative intent to effect an increase in judicial compensation as the legislature did not amend Judiciary Law article 7-B or otherwise direct the disbursement of the appropriated funds. Moreover, during the floor debates, members of the legislature expressed the view that the adjustment of judicial salaries could not take place without repeal or amendment of Judiciary Law article 7-B, notwithstanding the elimination of the reference to future legislation. Finally, the legislature struck from Laws of 2009, chapter 51, § 3 the judiciary's proposed salary schedule that would have effectively repealed Judiciary Law article 7-B.

Judges -- Judicial Salaries -- Increases in Judicial Compensation under Laws of 2009, chapter 51, § 3 -- Statutory Construction -- Repeal by Implication

4. Laws of 2009, chapter 51, § 3, which appropriated funds for adjustments in judicial compensation, did not repeal by implication Judiciary Law article 7-B, which sets forth the salaries to be paid to the various classes of judges and justices of the Unified Court System and thereby effect an increase in judicial compensation. The funds appropriated by Laws of 2009, chapter 51, § 3 could be reconciled with Judiciary Law article 7-B inasmuch as, in prior years, the legislature had similarly directed that funds be set aside but had not effected an increase in judicial compensation by failing to repeal or revise Judiciary Law article 7-B. Repeal by implication results from an enactment, the terms and operation of which cannot be harmonized with the terms and necessary effect of an earlier statute. The repeal of a statute by implication is, however, not favored by the law because, when the legislature intends to repeal an act, it usually says so expressly. As laws are presumed to be passed with deliberation and full knowledge of all the existing ones on the same subjects, it is reasonable to conclude that the legislature, in passing a statute, did not intend to interfere with or abrogate any former law relating to the same matter, unless the repugnancy between the two is irreconcilable. Here, the legislature, in enacting Laws of 2009, chapter 51, § 3, was continuing upon the same course it had established in preceding years--appropriating funds necessary to implement a salary increase so that one could be effected if and when it was willing to accept and support such an increase by repealing or revising Judiciary Law article 7-B.

 

309 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Moulton v State of New York


515096


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


114 A.D.3d 115; 977 N.Y.S.2d 797; 2013 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8505; 2013 NY Slip Op 8557



December 26, 2013, Decided 
December 26, 2013, Entered


HEADNOTES

Torts -- False Imprisonment -- Wrongful Confinement -- Detention under Improperly Imposed Period of Postrelease Supervision 

1. Claimant's continued detention and reincarceration for a parole violation based upon a term of postrelease supervision (PRS) administratively imposed by the then Department of Correctional Services (DOCS) after DOCS-imposed PRS was judicially declared invalid in New York (Matter of Garner v New York State Dept. of Correctional Servs.,10 NY3d 358, 889 NE2d 467, 859 NYS2d 590 [2008]) was not privileged, thus supporting his claim for false imprisonment against defendant State of New York. Even if the arrest warrant based upon claimant's violation of PRS were lawful, the validity and lawfulness of that detention evaporated in the wake of Garner. When claimant's parole revocation hearing was held two weeks later, defendant did not have the "privilege" to ignore that mandate, or to find claimant to be in violation of any invalid PRS term or to resentence him to the remainder of his term. After Garner, claimant was held without justification. Furthermore, Garner's companion decision, People v Sparber (10 NY3d 457, 889 NE2d 459, 859 NYS2d 582 [2008]) clearly indicated that the sole remedy for a sentencing court's failure to pronounce a statutorily required period of PRS was to vacate the sentence and remit for a resentencing hearing so that the trial judge can make the required pronouncement. Here, there was no indication that any effort was made by DOCS, the Division of Parole or the District Attorney to bring claimant before a court for resentencing. Even after legislation was passed establishing a mechanism for judicial resentencing, defendant did not take measures to have claimant lawfully resentenced by a court to PRS even as he remained incarcerated on an invalid parole revocation (see Correction Law § 601-d).

State -- Claim against State -- Absolute Immunity -- Parole Decisions 

2. Defendant State of New York was not entitled to absolute immunity for the actions of its parole officers, who continued to detain claimant for a parole violation based upon a term of postrelease supervision (PRS) administratively imposed by the then Department of Correctional Services (DOCS) after DOCS-imposed PRS was judicially declared invalid in New York (Matter of Garner v New York State Dept. of Correctional Servs.,10 NY3d 358, 889 NE2d 467, 859 NYS2d 590 [2008]). Inherently discretionary parole decisions of government officials have been recognized to be quasi-judicial decisions entitled to absolute immunity. However, after Garner categorically declared DOCS-imposed PRS invalid in this state, defendant's officials no longer had a reasoned basis or jurisdiction to enforce DOCS-imposed PRS and, when they did so, they stepped outside the scope of their authority and acted in the clear absence of all jurisdiction and without a colorable claim of authority. Thus, defendant acted without jurisdiction over claimant when it conducted a parole revocation hearing, revoked parole and reincarcerated him, all based upon an alleged violation of a period of PRS that it knew or should have known was a nullity under the controlling rulings of Garner and its companion decision, People v Sparber (10 NY3d 457, 889 NE2d 459, 859 NYS2d 582 [2008]), as well as under federal law.

State -- Claim against State -- Qualified Immunity -- Parole Decisions 

3. Defendant State of New York was not entitled to qualified immunity for the actions of its parole officers, who continued to detain claimant for a parole violation based upon a term of postrelease supervision (PRS) administratively imposed by the then Department of Correctional Services (DOCS) after DOCS-imposed PRS was judicially declared invalid in New York (Matter of Garner v New York State Dept. of Correctional Servs.,10 NY3d 358, 889 NE2d 467, 859 NYS2d 590 [2008]). Claimant's parole revocation hearing, the revocation of his parole and his reincarceration were prohibited by law after Garner categorically declared DOCS-imposed PRS invalid in this state, and claimant's right not to be subjected to such action was clearly established under state and federal law at the time it was taken after Garner. As a reasonable official would have understood that what he or she was doing violated that right, defendant's actions were not supported by a reasonable basis under existing law.

Torts -- Malicious Prosecution -- Detention under Improperly Imposed Period of Postrelease Supervision 

4. Claimant stated a cause of action for malicious prosecution based upon his continued detention and reincarceration for a parole violation based upon an administratively-imposed term of postrelease supervision (PRS) after DOCS-imposed PRS was judicially declared invalid in New York (Matter of Garner v New York State Dept. of Correctional Servs.,10 NY3d 358, 889 NE2d 467, 859 NYS2d 590 [2008]). Defendant could not ever lawfully prosecute claimant on a parole violation where the PRS in effect at the time of the alleged violation was imposed by the then Department of Correctional Services and was, as such, a nullity under Garner and People v Sparber (10 NY3d 457, 889 NE2d 459, 859 NYS2d 582 [2008]). Even had claimant been brought before a court for resentencing and the imposition of PRS, he could not thereafter have been found to have violated parole based on an incident that occurred before PRS was lawfully imposed by a court. Claimant's release on a habeas corpus petition, based on the term of his PRS being a nullity, was not inconsistent with his innocence of the parole violation and, thus, it constituted a favorable termination. Furthermore, even if probable cause existed at the time of arrest, it did not exist after Garner so as to authorize defendant to prosecute claimant at a parole revocation hearing on an administratively-imposed period of PRS that was invalid and unconstitutional as a matter of federal due process. Moreover, claimant alleged that defendant had the knowledge and notice that the PRS was invalid or acted with reckless disregard of whether it was valid so as to sufficiently allege actual malice.

 

310 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Nicholson v Incorporated Vil. of Garden City


2012-05095


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


112 A.D.3d 893; 978 N.Y.S.2d 288; 2013 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8547; 2013 NY Slip Op 8600



December 26, 2013, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal dismissed by Nicholson v. Inc. Vill. of Garden City, 23 N.Y.3d 947, 2014 N.Y. LEXIS 1027, 987 N.Y.S.2d 600, 10 N.E.3d 1156 (2014)
Leave to appeal denied by Michael Nicholson, etc. v. Incorporated Vil. of Garden City, 2014 N.Y. LEXIS 2400 (N.Y., Sept. 11, 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-In a declaratory judgment action, the trial court erred by finding that Local Law 4-2009 of the Village of Garden City (New York) was unconstitutional as it was reasonably related to a legitimate purpose of preserving larger corner lots on boulevard-style streets within the Village; [2]-The record established that the local law was not inconsistent with the comprehensive plan of the Village under Town Law § 263 and Village Law § 7-704; [3]-Plaintiffs' property was not arbitrarily singled out for less favorable treatment than neighboring properties in a manner that would be required to sustain a finding of reverse spot zoning; [4]-Plaintiffs' contention that the local law violated the uniformity requirement of Village Law § 7-702 was without merit.


OUTCOME: Order reversed, and matter remitted.


HEADNOTES

Local Laws--Validity--Constitutionality of Regulation Rezoning Corner Lots on Four Avenues in Village

 

311 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Piznarski


105460


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


113 A.D.3d 166; 977 N.Y.S.2d 104; 2013 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8089; 2013 NY Slip Op 8157



December 5, 2013, Decided 
December 5, 2013, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected February 4, 2014.
Leave to appeal denied by People v. Piznarski, 23 N.Y.3d 1041, 2014 N.Y. LEXIS 2236, 993 N.Y.S.2d 255, 17 N.E.3d 510 (2014)
Related proceeding at People v Piznarski, 2014 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 5373 (N.Y. Sup. Ct., Dec. 15, 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether defendant was properly convicted of four counts of second-degree unlawful surveillance (Penal Law § 250.45) based on his secretly recording his sexual encounters with two women. HOLDINGS: [1]-As § 250.45 merely required that the recording be surreptitious, it provided defendant fair notice that his actions were prohibited; [2]-The phrase "reasonable expectation of privacy" in § 250.45 was not impermissibly vague; [3]-As he made the recordings for his later sexual gratification, and used them to coerce a victim to perform additional sexual acts, the evidence was sufficient to support a determination under § 250.45(3)(b) that he did not have a legitimate purpose for surreptitiously recording the women; [4]-Consolidation of two indictments did not deprive him of a fair trial, as the evidence in one case would have been admissible in the other to show intent and common plan.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Unlawful Surveillance -- Scope of Conduct Criminalized -- Recording of Consensual Sexual Encounter

1. Defendant's surreptitious video recording of women engaging in sexual acts with him in his bedroom constituted prosecutable conduct under the unlawful surveillance in the second degree statute (Penal Law § 250.45), regardless of the consensual nature of the sexual acts. Nothing in the plain language of Penal Law § 250.45 precludes its application to the surreptitious recording of a consensual sexual encounter by one of the participants. The statute's reach is not limited to the actions of a "Peeping Tom." Furthermore, legislative history indicated that the statute was intended to proscribe the type of conduct engaged in by defendant. One of the circumstances that necessitated the enactment of the statute was the videotaping of women engaged in sexual relations without their knowledge and their lack of a remedy.

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Vagueness -- Unlawful Surveillance Statute -- Element of Surreptitiousness 

2. The element of surreptitiousness in the unlawful surveillance in the second degree statute (Penal Law § 250.45) was not unconstitutionally vague as applied to defendant, who video recorded women engaging in sexual acts with him in his bedroom without their knowledge or consent. By its terms, the statute prohibits the use of a device to surreptitiously record, without limitation as to the location of the device (see Penal Law § 250.45 [1], [2], [3] [a]), and is not restricted to circumstances in which a defendant "spies" on the victim from another location while the victim is being recorded. Thus, the fact that the camera was in plain view and both victims were aware of defendant's presence in the room was immaterial, as defendant was using the camera surreptitiously. Inasmuch as the statute merely requires that the recording be surreptitious, it provided fair notice to defendant that his actions were prohibited.

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Every Word to be Given Effect -- Unlawful Surveillance Statute 

3. In the prosecution of defendant arising from his video recording of women engaging in sexual acts with him in his bedroom, the People's interpretation of the element of surreptitiousness in the unlawful surveillance in the second degree statute (Penal Law § 250.45) did not impermissibly render superfluous the statute's requirement that the recording be without a victim's knowledge or consent. The People established that neither victim was aware of or consented to defendant recording them while having sex, and also tendered proof that defendant used the camera surreptitiously. The videos showed that the victims were not watching defendant when he began to record. Nor did defendant call the victims' attention to the camera or to the fact that he was recording them. That evidence, which established the element of surreptitiousness, was distinguishable from the evidence that proved the victims' lack of knowledge or consent and gave it independent meaning and effect. Finally, legislative history confirmed that the statute was designed to proscribe the type of conduct engaged in by defendant.

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Vagueness -- Unlawful Surveillance Statute -- Privacy Element 

4. The phrase "reasonable expectation of privacy" in the unlawful surveillance in the second degree statute (Penal Law § 250.45 [1], [2]) was not unconstitutionally vague as applied to defendant, who surreptitiously video recorded women engaging in sexual acts with him in his bedroom. For purposes of Penal Law § 250.45 (1) and (2), such phrase encompasses circumstances in which "a reasonable person would believe that he or she could fully disrobe in privacy" (Penal Law § 250.40 [1]). When a person knowingly undresses and engages in sexual relations with another person, he or she should be able to do so with the reasonable expectation that his or her actions are limited to that particular time and place and that his or her naked body and/or sexual acts will not be memorialized and/or repeatedly viewed at any time by the other person present or by anyone else with whom that person decides to share the recordings. The privacy element, when considered in light of the clear and understandable elements of the criminal conduct proscribed by Penal Law § 250.45 (1) and (2), gave defendant adequate notice and law enforcement sufficient guidance.

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Vagueness -- Unlawful Surveillance Statute -- "No Legitimate Purpose" 

5. The phrase "no legitimate purpose" in the unlawful surveillance in the second degree statute (Penal Law § 250.45 [3] [a]) was not unconstitutionally vague as applied to defendant, who surreptitiously video recorded women engaging in sexual acts with him in his bedroom. Although the statute does not define "no legitimate purpose," it prohibits surreptitious recording for purposes generally involving personal gain, for harmful reasons and for salacious and lewd purposes (see Penal Law § 250.45 [1], [2]). Furthermore, where recordings occur in certain places, including bedrooms, it is presumed that there is no legitimate purpose (see Penal Law § 250.45 [3] [b]). Upon review of the statute in its entirety and considering the common understanding of the phrase "no legitimate purpose," defendant received adequate notice that his surreptitious recording of the victims while they were engaged in a sexual act was unlawful and the statute provided clear standards for enforcement.

Crimes -- Unlawful Surveillance -- One-Party Consent Rule Not Applicable 

6. New York's one-party consent rule applicable to, among other things, eavesdropping and audio recording (see Penal Law § 250.00 [1], [2], [6]; CPL 700.05 [9]) is not applicable to the unlawful surveillance in the second degree statute (Penal Law § 250.45), which by its plain language criminalizes the recording of an individual "without such person's knowledge or consent."

Crimes -- Unlawful Surveillance -- Privacy Element -- Sufficiency of Evidence 

7. In the prosecution of defendant for various crimes, including unlawful surveillance in the second degree (Penal Law § 250.45), arising from his surreptitious video recording of women engaging in sexual acts with him in his bedroom, the evidence was legally sufficient to establish the element of "reasonable expectation of privacy" (Penal Law § 250.45 [1], [2]) with respect to one of the victims. The victim's testimony and the video itself demonstrated that she was concerned about her privacy, notwithstanding the fact that she was naked in defendant's presence. Defendant and the victim were having sex behind closed doors and the victim testified that she was not aware that she was being recorded and would not have consented thereto. The victim never acknowledged the camera or suggested in any way that, by being naked in defendant's presence, she was forgoing her privacy and giving defendant permission to capture images of her naked body for his future use.

Crimes -- Unlawful Surveillance -- "No Legitimate Purpose" Element -- Sufficiency of Evidence 

8. In the prosecution of defendant for various crimes, including unlawful surveillance in the second degree (Penal Law § 250.45), arising from his surreptitious video recording of women engaging in sexual acts with him in his bedroom, the evidence was legally sufficient to establish that defendant lacked a legitimate purpose for recording the victims without their knowledge or consent (Penal Law § 250.45 [3] [a]). Because the recordings were made in defendant's bedroom, the statute established a rebuttable presumption that they were made with no legitimate purpose (Penal Law § 250.45 [3] [b]). Even assuming that the statute would permit a finding that defendant's later sexual gratification was a legitimate purpose, the evidence indicated a more nefarious purpose--defendant's threatening messages to one victim via social media notifying her that she had been recorded implied that he would upload the recording to the Internet. Defendant did more than merely record and later watch the video of that victim; he also created an edited video with a title that included the victim's name and still photographs of her wearing only undergarments.

Crimes -- Consolidation and Severance -- Different Criminal Transactions -- Intent and Common Plan and Scheme 

9. County Court properly consolidated the two indictments charging defendant with various crimes arising from his surreptitious video recording of women engaging in sexual acts with him in his bedroom, since proof regarding the events alleged in each of the indictments was relevant to the issues of intent and common plan and scheme (CPL 200.20 [2] [b]). While defendant admitted that he intended to record the victims, he disputed the People's claim that he intended to surreptitiously record them without their knowledge or consent. In that regard, a recording of one victim and defendant's threatening messages to her via social media, which indicated that she was unaware of the recording, were relevant to prove that defendant intended to surreptitiously record the other victim, and vice versa. Moreover, the recordings of both victims tended to show that defendant maintained a camera on the desk in his bedroom, which he would turn on during sexual activity, suggesting a common plan to secretly record women with whom he had sexual encounters, so that he could later use the videos for illegitimate purposes.

Grand Jury -- Defective Proceeding -- Impairment of Integrity of Proceedings -- Expert Witness Present during Victim's Testimony 

10. In the prosecution of defendant for various crimes, including unlawful surveillance in the second degree (Penal Law § 250.45), arising from his surreptitious video recording of women engaging in sexual acts with him in his bedroom, the forensics computer expert who performed the search of defendant's laptop and compiled the incriminating videos contained thereon should not have been present in the grand jury room after he completed his testimony and while one of the victims was testifying (CPL 190.25 [3]), but his presence did not impair the integrity of the grand jury proceeding. The expert was asked by the People to remain in the grand jury room in order to assist "as a technician" during the victim's testimony, specifically to play video clips for the grand jurors. By the time the victim testified, the expert had completed his testimony, which related to his role as a forensics computer expert and did not touch on any areas about which the victim testified. In addition, nothing indicated that the expert and the victim worked together during the investigation.

Crimes -- Sentence -- Factors Considered 

11. Defendant's sentence to 1 to 3 years on his conviction of unlawful surveillance, consecutive to two concurrent nine-month sentences on coercion convictions and three concurrent one-year sentences on unlawful surveillance convictions, arising from his surreptitious video recording of women engaging in sexual acts with him, was not excessive. The sentencing court carefully considered statements from the People, the victims, and defense counsel, as well as letters from defendant's father and sister, noted defendant's academic and personal accomplishments and his lack of prior criminal history, and reviewed information concerning his medical condition. In concluding that a term of imprisonment was warranted, the court found that there was a "gaping hole" in defendant's character and gave appropriate weight to the devastating and profound effect of defendant's actions upon the victims.

Crimes -- Sex Offenders -- Sex Offender Registration Act -- Unlawful Surveillance 

12. County Court properly determined to certify defendant as a sex offender upon his conviction of unlawful surveillance and coercion arising from his repeated surreptitious recording of women engaging in sexual acts and his use of coercion to convince one of those women to perform additional sexual acts. As relevant here, a conviction of unlawful surveillance in the second degree under Penal Law § 250.45 (2) and (3) is included as a sex offense "unless upon motion by the defendant, the trial court, having regard to the nature and circumstances of the crime and to the history and character of the defendant, is of the opinion that registration would be unduly harsh and inappropriate" (Correction Law § 168-a [2] [e]). Notwithstanding one expert's opinion that defendant posed a low risk to reoffend, given the nature and circumstances of his crimes, the requirement that defendant register as a sex offender was not unduly harsh and inappropriate.

 

312 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Distressed Holdings, LLC v Ehrler


2011-03673


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


113 A.D.3d 111; 976 N.Y.S.2d 517; 2013 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7974; 2013 NY Slip Op 8044



December 4, 2013, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-When plaintiff judgment creditor sent documents required by CPLR 5222-a to defendant judgment debtor's bank, which did not send the debtor the documents, causing the debtor's account to be restrained without a chance to claim exemptions, due process was violated because the debtor could not assert her rights before her property was taken; [2]-Executing a levy under CPLR 5232 did not make the issue academic because, under CPLR 5222-a, whether enforcing a judgment by a restraining notice or levy execution, the bank had to send proper notice; [3]-The proper remedy was to let the debtor claim exemptions before releasing funds, instead of terminating the execution, because the creditor met its statutory obligations, had no control over the bank, and had a strong interest in satisfying the judgment against the debtor, and the debtor had a strong interest in keeping exempt funds.


OUTCOME: Order modified.


HEADNOTES

Judgments -- Supplementary Proceedings -- Restraining Notice -- Bank Account -- Notice to Judgment Debtor

In an action by plaintiff to enforce a judgment against defendant by restraining defendant's bank account under CPLR 5222, the failure of the bank to comply with its obligation under CPLR 5222-a (b) (3) to provide defendant with the restraining notice, exemption notice and exemption claim forms within two business days after receipt of those documents from plaintiff constituted a violation of defendant's due process rights as her account was restrained without her having been afforded notice and an opportunity to claim that any or all of the funds in the account were exempt from debt collection. Upon the issuance of a restraining notice under CPLR 5222, the judgment creditor must serve the banking institution with the restraining notice; an exemption notice advising the judgment debtor that his or her bank account is being restrained, that certain funds are exempt from restraint and of the procedure by which an exemption is claimed; and two exemption claim forms (CPLR 5222-a [b] [1]). Thereafter, the banking institution must, within two business days after receipt of such documents, serve them upon the judgment debtor (CPLR 5222-a [b] [3]). Here, plaintiff properly sent the required documents to the bank, but the bank did not timely send them to defendant. Although plaintiff also executed a levy against the account under CPLR 5232, the bank was required to provide the requisite notice to defendant regardless of whether a restraining notice or levy was employed to enforce the judgment (see CPLR 5222-a [a], [b] [3]). Rather than terminate the restraint, however, defendant was afforded the opportunity to claim exemptions before any funds in her account were turned over.

People v. Brainard


105111


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


111 A.D.3d 1162; 975 N.Y.S.2d 498; 2013 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7809; 2013 NY Slip Op 7881



November 27, 2013, Decided 
November 27, 2013, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Defendant's fine, prison term, and consecutive probation with ignition interlock conditions did not violate the double jeopardy protection against multiple punishments because the legislature clearly intended, in Penal Law § 60.21, a period of probation or conditional discharge in addition to any fine or term of imprisonment; [2]-The trial court had authority to enforce the ignition interlock condition, under Penal Law § 65.10(2)(k-1), because, if it had reasonable cause to believe he violated that condition, CPL arts. 410.30, 410.40, 410.70 gave it the authority to file a declaration of delinquency, order defendant to appear and hold a hearing, and revoke his conditional discharge and impose another sentence, such as probation or a fine.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes--Double Jeopardy--Cumulative Punishments--Driving while Intoxicated

Motor Vehicles--Operating Vehicle while Under Influence of Alcohol or Drugs--Conditional Discharge--Installation of Ignition Interlock Device

315 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Bond, Schoeneck & King, PLLC v New York Dept. of Economic Dev.


514812


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT
