319 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v Salazar


SCI. 40154C/07, 9943


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


112 A.D.3d 5; 973 N.Y.S.2d 140; 2013 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6582; 2013 NY Slip Op 6629



October 10, 2013, Decided 
October 10, 2013, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Leave to appeal denied by People v Salazar, 22 NY3d 1090, 2014 N.Y. LEXIS 351, 981 NYS2d 676, 4 NE3d 978 (N.Y., Jan. 14, 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether the trial court erred in setting aside a verdict convicting a Hispanic, non-English speaking defendant of DWI on the grounds that the failure of police to administer a physical coordination test to him violated equal protection and due process. HOLDINGS: [1]-As non-English speakers were not a suspect class, and intentional discrimination based on defendant's ethnicity was not established, the rational basis test applied, and a rational basis for the policy existed--the impracticability of conducting coordination tests through interpreters; [2]-A DWI suspect had no due process right to compel the police to administer a coordination test simply because it might yield information that was helpful to him.


OUTCOME: The order of dismissal was reversed.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Equal Protection of Laws -- Failure to Administer Physical Coordination Tests to Non-English Speaking Person Charged with Driving while Intoxicated -- Standard of Review

1. In a prosecution for driving while intoxicated in which a non-English speaking defendant of Hispanic origin alleged that the failure of the police to administer physical coordination tests to him when those same tests were administered to English speakers deprived him of equal protection, the trial court erroneously determined that the challenged practice disadvantaged a suspect class, thereby triggering the strict scrutiny that requires the government to establish a compelling justification. Although Hispanics as an ethnic group constitute a suspect class under equal protection analysis, the practice at issue was facially neutral as to ethnicity, since the policy determination as to whether or not to perform physical coordination tests was based on a suspect's ability to speak and understand English, and was not based upon race, religion or national origin. Language, by itself, does not identify members of a suspect class. Moreover, there was nothing in the record to support the trial court's rejection of the police officer's explanation of the policy, or its conclusion that the police chose not to administer a coordination test on the basis of anti-Hispanic animus. The only evidence adduced showed that non-English-speaking suspects were not offered the option of taking a physical coordination test in order to avoid confusion and complications due to a language barrier. Accordingly, since the practice at issue did not burden a suspect class, and intentional discrimination based on ethnicity was not demonstrated, defendant's claim was evaluated under the rational basis analysis.

Constitutional Law -- Equal Protection of Laws -- Failure to Administer Physical Coordination Tests to Non-English Speaking Person Charged with Driving while Intoxicated -- Rational Basis

2. A rational basis existed for the failure of the police to administer physical coordination tests to a non-English speaking defendant of Hispanic origin suspected of driving while intoxicated when those same tests were routinely administered to English speakers, since the police clearly have an interest in the reliability of coordination tests, and the evidence supported the conclusion that conducting the test through a Spanish-speaking police officer who was not trained in conducting the test could compromise the reliability of the result. Furthermore, there was evidence in the record that it is inherently impracticable to conduct coordination tests through interpreters. While the failure to provide interpreters in judicial or administrative proceedings may give rise to equal protection and due process claims, sobriety coordination tests are merely investigative techniques and are neither judicial nor administrative actions. To require the police department to have qualified interpreters on call on a 24/7 basis would impose unrealistic and substantial financial and administrative burdens. Thus, the avoidance of those crushing obligations provided a rational basis for the policy. Additionally, the time it would take an interpreter to get to a testing site would serve to degrade evidence, as the passage of time impacts sobriety. Accordingly, the policy in question did not violate the equal protection clauses of the Federal and New York State Constitutions.

Constitutional Law -- Due Process of Law -- Failure to Administer Physical Coordination Tests to Non-English Speaking Person

3. It was not an unconstitutional denial of due process for the police to fail to administer physical coordination tests to a non-English speaking defendant of Hispanic origin who was arrested for driving while intoxicated where such tests are routinely administered to English-speaking defendants. While the failure to administer the tests may have deprived defendant of an opportunity to obtain potentially favorable evidence, the police have no affirmative duty to gather or help gather evidence for an accused. Defendant failed to show that the policy of the police department not to administer physical coordination tests to Spanish-speaking suspects presented a great risk that he would be erroneously deprived of his liberty, especially in light of the results of his breathalyzer test results and the arresting officer's observations of defendant at the scene of the arrest, which amply supported the conclusion that defendant was in fact intoxicated. Moreover, the enormous fiscal and administrative burdens associated with maintaining interpreters for non-English speakers on an around-the-clock basis were a legitimate governmental concern.

324 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v Beltran


2010-07150


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


110 A.D.3d 153; 970 N.Y.S.2d 289; 2013 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5555; 2013 NY Slip Op 5638



August 14, 2013, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Leave to appeal denied by People v. Beltran, 23 N.Y.3d 1018, 2014 N.Y. LEXIS 1780, 992 N.Y.S.2d 800, 16 N.E.3d 1280 (2014)
Post-conviction relief denied at People v. Beltran, 2014 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 5303 (N.Y. Sup. Ct., Nov. 25, 2014)
Related proceeding at, Request denied by People v. Beltran, 2015 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7667 (N.Y. App. Div. 2d Dep't, June 23, 2015)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The child was properly declared to be a vulnerable witness, CPL § 65.20, where she was particularly young and defendant occupied a position of authority, since he was the child's great uncle by marriage, the child regarded him as a family member, he was responsible for the care of the child, and he had frequent contact with her; [2]-Placing defendant and the child in the same room during the child's testimony would contribute to the likelihood that the child would suffer severe mental or emotional harm; [3]-Defendant's constitutional rights were not violated; [4]-The charges in the indictment were properly joined, CPL § 200.20(2)(c); [5]-The first and second counts of the indictment were multiplicitous, since those counts both alleged a course of sexual conduct against a child in the first degree.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed as modified.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Witnesses -- Use of Closed-Circuit Television for Child Witness

1. In defendant's prosecution for course of sexual conduct against a child, Supreme Court properly determined that one of the complainants--a seven-year-old child who testified in open court with regard to general background material about her family but became overwhelmed to the point where she was crying and could not respond when questioned about the incident--was a vulnerable witness within the meaning of CPL article 65. Clear and convincing evidence supported the finding that the child was a vulnerable witness. She was likely to suffer serious mental and emotional harm if required to testify in the physical presence of defendant given her "particularly young" age at the time of trial and that defendant--her great uncle by marriage whom she regarded as a family member--occupied a position of authority, was responsible for her care at the time the crime occurred and had frequent contact with her (see CPL 65.20 [10]). Moreover, the emotional trauma the child experienced when she attempted to testify in open court about the crime substantially impaired her ability to communicate with the jury. Furthermore, the child was properly permitted to testify through live two-way closed-circuit television while defendant remained in the courtroom. The record supported the requisite specific finding that placing defendant and the child in the same room during her testimony would contribute to the likelihood that she would suffer severe mental or emotional harm, inasmuch as Supreme Court's observations of the child when she was questioned in the courtroom, and the hearing testimony of a social worker who had met with the child, provided clear and convincing evidence that the cause of the child's severe emotional upset was defendant's presence in the room (see CPL 65.20 [11]).

Crimes -- Right of Confrontation -- Use of Closed-Circuit Television for Child Witness

2. In a prosecution for course of sexual conduct against a child, defendant was not deprived of his federal and state constitutional right to confront the witnesses against him where one of the complainants, a seven-year-old child who became overwhelmed to the point where she was crying and could not respond when questioned about the incident in open court, was determined to be a vulnerable witness within the meaning of CPL article 65 and permitted to testify via closed-circuit television outside defendant's physical presence. The evidence established the likelihood that the child would suffer severe mental or emotional harm if required to testify in defendant's physical presence as required by the statute (CPL 65.20 [12]). Moreover, the child testified by way of two-way closed-circuit television, so that she could see defendant during her testimony and defendant could see her, allowing for face-to-face confrontation. Furthermore, as the prosecutor and defense counsel were in the room with the child and provisions were made for defense counsel and defendant, who remained in the courtroom, to communicate with each other during the testimony, defendant's confrontation rights were not violated.

 

325 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Arroyo v Board of Educ. of City of New York


2011-04742


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


110 A.D.3d 17; 970 N.Y.S.2d 229; 2013 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5442; 2013 NY Slip Op 5507



July 31, 2013, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Since a personal injury action filed by the parents on behalf of their minor child was never properly dismissed, there was no need for a motion to restore, and the board of education's motion to dismiss based on laches was properly denied because it failed to comply with the 90-day written demand requirement in CPLR 3216(b), which was a condition precedent to dismissal of the action for general delay.


OUTCOME: Order affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Dismissal and Nonsuit -- Want of Prosecution

In an action to recover damages for personal injuries which was "marked off" the calendar and later marked "disposed" prior to the filing of a note of issue due to plaintiffs' failure to appear at a status conference, Supreme Court properly denied defendant's cross motion made more than 12 years later to dismiss the complaint based on the doctrine of laches since the action was never properly dismissed and defendant had not made a 90-day written demand on plaintiffs to serve and file a note of issue. Absent a 90-day demand pursuant to CPLR 3216 (b), a court has no power to dismiss an action for general delay. Moreover, given that the case was at a pre-note-of-issue stage, the acts of designating the case "marked off" and "disposed" were a nullity and meant nothing because there was no calendar from which to mark it off of and marking a case off a motion or conference calendar does not dispose of it. Laches does not provide an alternate route to dismissal where a defendant has not served the 90-day demand statutorily required to prompt resumption of the litigation.

 

326 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v Tohom


2011-07111


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


109 A.D.3d 253; 969 N.Y.S.2d 123; 2013 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5149; 2013 NY Slip Op 5234



July 10, 2013, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether a therapeutic "comfort dog" was allowed in a trial when a court determined that the dog could provide emotional support for a victim. HOLDINGS: [1]-Executive Law § 642-a encompassed a child victim who had to bear witness against an individual accused of his or her victimization; [2]-The victim came within the purview of § 642-a because the criminal acts took place when she was between the ages of 11 and 15 years, and the legislature desired to afford special protection to children under 16 years of age who were victimized by crime and who were called upon to testify; [3]-The dog's accompaniment of the victim to the witness stand did not adversely affect defendant's due process right to a fair trial or compromise his constitutional right of confrontation; [4]-The county court balanced the victim's need for the dog against the potential prejudice to defendant.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Witnesses -- Presence of Therapeutic Comfort Dog during Testimony of Child Witness

1. In a prosecution arising from allegations that defendant engaged in multiple acts of sexual misconduct against his daughter, County Court properly allowed the presence of a therapeutic comfort dog at the witness stand during the 15-year-old victim's testimony under Executive Law §642-a and as an exercise of its inherent power and discretion to control the trial proceedings. Executive Law §642-a, entitled "Fair treatment of child victims as witnesses," provides in subdivision (4) that a trial judge "should be sensitive to the psychological and emotional stress a child witness may undergo when testifying." The legislative intent behind the passage of section 642-a reveals that the statute was intended to cover child victims and witnesses who were under the age of 16 years. Given that the victim here was between the ages of 11 and 15 years when the criminal acts committed against her took place, the trial court properly concluded that she came within the purview of the statute. Since the language of subdivision (4) is so general that it can only be interpreted as authorizing a trial judge to utilize his or her discretion in fashioning an appropriate measure to address a testifying child witness's emotional or psychological stress, there was no rational reason why the court's exercise of sensitivity should not be extended to allow the use of a comfort dog where it was shown that such animal could ameliorate the stress of the testifying child witness. While subdivision (4) does not provide any criterion for a finding of necessity, the testimony of the social worker who had been working with the victim provided ample evidence that the dog's presence alleviated the victim's anxiety and allowed her to more easily discuss the conduct which was perpetrated against her. Furthermore, the court's decision to allow the dog at the witness stand during the victim's testimony was also a proper exercise of its inherent power and discretion to control the trial proceedings. Defendant made no showing that the dog's presence had any identifiable impact on the proceedings and the court specifically informed the jury that it was not to draw any inference in favor of or against either side because of the dog's presence.

Crimes -- Fair Trial -- Presence of Therapeutic Comfort Dog during Testimony of Child Witness

2. In a prosecution arising from allegations that defendant engaged in multiple acts of sexual misconduct against his minor daughter, her accompaniment by a therapeutic comfort dog to the witness stand did not violate defend ant's due process right to a fair trial. Defendant, who admitted that the dog was trained merely to respond to a person's stress level, offered no support for his contention that the dog's presence was inherently prejudicial because the dog allegedly conveyed the impression that the victim was being truthful as she testified. It is beyond dispute that a dog does not have the ability to discern truth from falsehood and thus cannot communicate such a distinction to a jury. Moreover, it could not be concluded that any actual prejudice resulted from the concededly unobtrusive presence of the dog in the courtroom. While the dog may have engendered some sympathy for the victim, there was no proof that such sympathy was significantly greater than the normal human response to a child's testimony about his or her sexual abuse at the hand of an adult. Furthermore, the court gave the jury specific instructions that it was not to permit sympathy to enter into its considerations, which instructions the jury was presumed to follow, and defense counsel did not suggest any further instruction regarding the dog's presence.

Crimes -- Right of Confrontation -- Presence of Therapeutic Comfort Dog during Testimony of Child Witness

3. In a prosecution arising from allegations that defendant engaged in multiple acts of sexual misconduct against his minor daughter, her accompaniment by a therapeutic comfort dog to the witness stand did not violate defendant's constitutional right to confront the witnesses against him. Defendant made no complaint or objection during trial that the dog physically impeded the jury's ability to observe the victim as she testified, but instead agreed that the dog sat peacefully and quietly on the stand. Nor did the dog's presence make it more likely that the jury would credit the victim's testimony as truthful. Defense counsel engaged in extended and thorough cross-examination of the victim and his right to cross-examine the central witness in the People's case against defendant was thus not impeded. There was also no indication that the jury deemed the dog's presence to be, in effect, a corroboration of the victim's testimony. Furthermore, the court specifically informed the jury that it was not to draw any inference in favor of or against either side because of the dog's presence and it must be presumed that the jury followed the legal instructions it was given.

Crimes -- Evidence -- Rape Shield Law

4. In a prosecution arising from allegations that defendant engaged in multiple acts of sexual misconduct against his minor daughter, County Court did not improvidently exercise its discretion under the Rape Shield Law (CPL 60.42) in precluding evidence concerning the victim's sexual history. Defendant's offer of proof that his daughter became pregnant by another man consisted of the fact that this man had lived next door to her, possibly at the time of conception, that the daughter had been seen texting people, and that, at an uncertain time, she had made a drawing with a picture of a heart, a baby, and the other man's name. The defendant's offer of proof was based on pure speculation and, therefore, was insufficient to overcome the presumption that such evidence should be precluded pursuant to the Rape Shield Law.

 

327 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Mangano v Silver


2012-09463, 2012-09991


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


107 A.D.3d 956; 968 N.Y.S.2d 147; 2013 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4699; 2013 NY Slip Op 4783



June 26, 2013, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at, Dismissed by Mangano v Silver, 22 NY3d 892, 997 NE2d 481, 974 NYS2d 908, 2013 N.Y. LEXIS 2762 (2013)
Leave to appeal denied by Edward P. Mangano v Sheldon Silver, 2014 N.Y. LEXIS 48 (N.Y., Jan. 14, 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, State, Department of Taxation and Finance, Metropolitan Transportation Authority (MTA) and officials, separately appealed an order and judgment by the Nassau County Supreme Court (New York) that granted a motion by plaintiffs, official, counties, and town, for summary judgment, denied the defendants' cross-motions for summary judgment, and thereafter declared that Tax Law art. 23 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: In 2009, the state legislature passed the Metropolitan Commuter Transportation Mobility Tax Law, codified at art. 23. The tax was imposed on employers and self-employed individuals for the sole purpose of providing an additional stable and reliable dedicated funding source for the MTA and its subsidiaries and affiliates to preserve, operate and improve essential transit and transportation services in the metropolitan commuter transportation district. The appellate court found, inter alia, that Law served a substantial state concern by providing a funding source for the preservation, operation, and improvement of essential transit and transportation services in the metropolitan commuter transportation district. Accordingly, it was not unconstitutionally passed without a home rule message under N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 2(b)(2).


OUTCOME: The appeals from the order were dismissed, the judgment was reversed, the plaintiffs' motions were denied, the cross-motions were granted, the order was modified accordingly, and art. 23 was declared constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations--Home Rule Powers--Special Law Serving Substantial State Concern--Metropolitan Transportation Authority Employer Tax Law--Home Rule Message Not Required

 

328 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Max v Ward


750.1 CAE 13-00619


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


107 A.D.3d 1597; 967 N.Y.S.2d 557; 2013 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4455; 2013 NY Slip Op 4523



June 14, 2013, Decided 
June 14, 2013, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner objectors challenged a decision from a New York Supreme Court, Erie County, which dismissed their action against respondent Erie County, New York, Democratic Committee. The action sought various forms of relief, including a judgment setting aside the election of officers at a September 29, 2012, reorganization meeting.


OVERVIEW: At a reorganization meeting, officers of the committee were elected. The objectors then filed this action alleging that a redistricting plan was arbitrary, capricious, and an abuse of discretion, that the redistricting plan was enacted in violation of open meeting laws, that Election Law § 2-104 was unconstitutional, and that they were entitled to damages as a result of various constitutional violations. The petition was dismissed, and this appeal followed. On review, it was determined that the first cause of action was time barred insofar as it sought relief under CPLR art. 78 since the proceeding was commenced more than 4 months after the redistricting plan was enacted. The first cause of action was timely to the extent that it sought review under the Election Law. The State Democratic Committee, the County Board of Elections, and the elected district committee members were not necessary parties. However, the Election Law could not have been used to advance an otherwise time-barred CPLR art. 78 claim. The objectors failed to show why the redistricting plan should have been voided as a sanction for an open meetings law violation. There were no constitutional violations shown.


OUTCOME: The judgment was modified in order to reflect a declaration, rather than a dismissal, of the third, fifth, and sixth causes of action. As modified, the judgment was affirmed without costs.


HEADNOTES

Limitation of Actions--Four-Month Statute of Limitations--Challenge to Political Party's County Committee Redistricting Plan

Parties--Necessary Parties--State Political Party Not Necessary Party to Proceeding Challenging Organization of County Committee

Elections--Political Parties--Challenge to County Committee's Weighted Voting Scheme

 

329 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Ford v New York State Racing & Wagering Bd.


514622


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


107 A.D.3d 1071; 967 N.Y.S.2d 453; 2013 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3982; 2013 NY Slip Op 4065



June 6, 2013, Decided 
June 6, 2013, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Leave to appeal denied by Matter of Ford v. N. Y. State Racing & Wagering Bd., 22 N.Y.3d 947, 999 N.E.2d 539, 2013 N.Y. LEXIS 2927, 977 N.Y.S.2d 176 (2013)
Affirmed by Matter of Ford v. New York State Racing & Wagering Bd., 2014 N.Y. LEXIS 3438 (N.Y., Dec. 18, 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, licensed owners and trainers of harness racehorses and a nonprofit organization of licensed owners, trainers and drivers of harness racehorses, filed an application in the Supreme Court, Schenectady County (New York), in a proceeding pursuant to CPLR article 78, to annul a certain regulation promulgated by respondent, the New York State Racing and Wagering Board. The court granted the application. The Board appealed.


OVERVIEW: The New York State Racing and Wagering Board, pursuant to Racing, Pari-Mutuel Wagering and Breeding Law § 301, adopted regulations, 9 NYCRR 4043.12 and 4120.17, prohibiting the use of certain performance enhancing substances in racehorses and permitting the Board to drug test all racehorses under the care or control of a Board licensed trainer anticipated to compete in a race at a New York racetrack within six months. On appeal, the court found that, under former Racing, Pari-Mutuel Wagering and Breeding Law § 101, as well as the Board's broad power to regulate the harness racing industry, and the State of New York's interest in assuring the integrity of racing, the Board did not exceed its statutory authority when it adopted the regulations. Further, 9 NYCRR 4120.17(i), the penalty provision, was not arbitrary, capricious, or unreasonable and the list of prohibited substances in 9 NYCRR 4120.17(e) was not so vague as to require annulment. However, 9 NYCRR 4120.17(e)(3), the portion of the regulation prohibiting all protein and peptide-based drugs, was properly annulled because it was inconsistent with, and could not be reconciled with 9 NYCRR 4120.2, the permitted-use regulation.


OUTCOME: The judgment was modified, on the law, without costs, by reversing so much thereof as annulled and enjoined the Board from enforcing all portions of a regulation, except for the portion of the regulation prohibiting all protein and peptide-based drugs, and, as so modified, was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Horse Racing--Harness Races--State's Statutory Authority to Adopt Regulation Permitting Out-of-Competition Drug Testing of Harness Racehorses

Horse Racing--Harness Races--Regulation Permitting Out-of-Competition Drug Testing of Harness Racehorses--180-Day Window Prior to Competition in Which Horses Anticipated to Race May be Drug Tested

Horse Racing--Harness Races--Regulation Permitting Out-of-Competition Drug Testing of Harness Racehorses--Requirement That Licensed Owners and Trainers Produce, upon Demand, Horse Stabled within 100-mile Radius of New York Track

Horse Racing--Harness Races--Regulation Permitting Out-of-Competition Drug Testing of Harness Racehorses--No Improper Infringement on Legal Rights of Private Horse Farm Owners

Horse Racing--Revocation or Suspension of License--Trainer--Minimum Penalty of 10-Year Suspension for Positive Drug Test

Horse Racing--Harness Races--Regulation Permitting Out-of-Competition Drug Testing of Harness Racehorses--List of Prohibited Performance-Enhancing Substances Not So Vague as to Require Annulment of Regulation

Horse Racing--Harness Races--Regulation Permitting Out-of-Competition Drug Testing of Harness Racehorses--Provision Prohibiting All "Protein and Peptide-Based Drugs" Properly Annulled Because Inconsistent with Permitted-Use Regulation

People v Parilla


1706/03, 9708


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


109 A.D.3d 20; 970 N.Y.S.2d 497; 2013 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3807; 2013 NY Slip Op 3931



May 30, 2013, Decided 
May 30, 2013, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Leave to appeal denied by, Motion dismissed by People etc. v Scott Parilla, 21 NY3d 865, 996 NE2d 501, 2013 N.Y. LEXIS 2456, 973 NYS2d 582 (N.Y., Sept. 10, 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the order of the Supreme Court, Bronx County (New York) that adjudicated him a level three sexually violent offender pursuant to the Sex Offender Registration Act (SORA), Correction Law art. 6-C.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court held that SORA, which was not punitive in nature, did not violate the Ex Post Facto Clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 10(1). Defendant argued, inter alia, that the trial court erred in adjudicating him a level three offender. The appellate court disagreed. Although the court should not have assessed 30 of the 170 points and defendant should have been scored 140, he was still correctly designated a level three sex offender. The court opted to rely on the risk assessment instrument (RAI) only as a starting point and only after hearing oral argument from defendant at a separate hearing regarding the reliability of risk assessment tools. Defendant was given ample opportunity to argue his case, and the court reviewed his extensive submissions. Regarding drug abuse, in addition to the case summary, a 2001 inmate status report confirmed that defendant admitted to abusing LSD and alcohol in the past; that amounted to clear and convincing evidence of his drug use. The select disciplinary records on which defendant relied did not conclusively establish that he was no longer abusing drugs.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Sex Offenders -- Sex Offender Registration Act -- Retroactive Application of Amendments to Act Not Violative of Ex Post Facto Clause -- Civil Nature of Act

1. Under the intents-effects test, amendments made to the Sex Offender Registration Act (Correction Law art 6-C [SORA]) since 1996 did not render the Act a punitive statute such that its retroactive application violates the Ex Post Facto Clause. The legislature intended the statute to be regulatory, and SORA is not now so punitive either in purpose or effect as to negate the State's intention to deem it civil. Instead of imposing punishment for a past crime, the Act is a remedial statute intended to prevent future crime. The effect of the amendments--which included lengthening the registration period, creation of additional categories of offenders, adjusting the petition for relief, a photographic requirement, Internet availability of registrants, changing the role of the People and sentencing court in proceedings under the Act, and changing the penalties for failure to report--were aimed at improving the strength, efficiency and effectiveness of the Act as a civil statute, not at punishing sex offenders. Accordingly, the effect of the amendments was not so punitive as to negate the legislative intent. Although subjecting sex offenders to lifetime registration and notification requirements, with their attendant obligations and restrictions, increases the difficulties and embarrassment a sex offender may endure, even where he has led a law-abiding life since his conviction, the fact that the restrictions are difficult and cumbersome is not enough to make them unconstitutional. 

Crimes -- Sex Offenders -- Sex Offender Registration Act -- Retroactive Application of Amendments to Act Not Violative of Constitutional Prohibitions against Double Jeopardy

2. The Sex Offender Registration Act (Correction Law art 6-C), as amended since 1996, is not penal in nature and does not violate constitutional prohibitions against double jeopardy.

Crimes -- Sex Offenders -- Sex Offender Registration Act -- Risk Level Adjudication

3. In an action challenging defendant's adjudication as a level three sex offender, the court sufficiently weighed the risk assessment instrument against the defense evidence and arguments, and correctly adjudicated defendant a level three offender. Defendant was given ample opportunity to argue his case at two separate hearings, and the court reviewed his extensive submissions. The People met their burden of establishing, by clear and convincing evidence, risk factors bearing a total score of 140 points, which supported a level three adjudication. While it was error to have assessed 20 points for the age of the victim, as there was a lack of clear and convincing evidence to support her age, and 10 points were doubly counted, defendant was initially assessed with 170 points, thereby still rendering him a level three offender. Moreover, defendant's admission of past drug use justified assessing points for that category.

Rodgers v City of New York


2012-02879


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


106 A.D.3d 1068; 966 N.Y.S.2d 466; 2013 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3750; 2013 NY Slip Op 3826



May 29, 2013, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Leave to appeal denied by Rodgers v City of New York, 21 NY3d 864, 995 NE2d 1159, 2013 N.Y. LEXIS 2156, 973 NYS2d 87 (N.Y., Sept. 3, 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff arrestee appealed an order by the Kings County Supreme Court (New York) that granted a motion by defendants, city, police department, and police detective, for summary judgment on his causes of action for false arrest, malicious prosecution, assault, battery, intentional/negligent infliction of emotional distress, and violations of 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 and U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OVERVIEW: The arrestee alleged that the detective failed to properly investigate the crime and disclose exculpatory evidence to him, the prosecutor, and the grand jury that indicted him. The trial court found probable cause for the arrest and prosecution based upon the identification of the arrestee by two witnesses, and that his assertion that disclosure of the exculpatory evidence to the grand jury would have materially influenced its investigation was speculative. The appellate court found, inter alia, that the arrestee failed to rebut the presumption of probable cause that was created by the grand jury's indictment. He also failed to raise triable issues of fact as to whether the prosecution was motivated by actual malice. His arrest, detention, and prosecution did not rise to the level of extreme or outrageous conduct necessary to sustain the intentional and negligent infliction of emotional distress. Finally, the arrestee failed to show a violation of § 1983 or amend. XIV. Accordingly, the trial court properly granted the defendants' motion for summary judgment.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed.

333 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Prince v City of New York


9525


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


108 A.D.3d 114; 966 N.Y.S.2d 16; 2013 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3539; 2013 NY Slip Op 3623



May 21, 2013, Decided 
May 21, 2013, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sculptor appealed a judgment by the New York County Supreme Court (New York) that denied his CPLR art. 78 petition to annul a decision by respondent city environmental control board that upheld a fine and the impoundment of his vehicle after he violated Administrative Code of the City of NY § 16-118(7)(f)(1)(i); the sculptor claimed that the penalty was excessive and violated the state and federal constitutions.


OVERVIEW: The sculptor removed a single television antenna from the top of some curbside garbage bags, placed it in his vehicle, and drove away. The sculptor believed that the antenna was garbage and intended to use it in his artwork. Shortly thereafter, a city sanitation police officer pulled the sculptor over, and issued him a summons for unauthorized removal of residential recyclable material using a motor vehicle. The summons carried with it a mandatory $2,000 fine for a first offense. The officer also impounded the sculptor's vehicle, which was not to be released until the fine and applicable storage fees were paid. The appellate court found, inter alia, that the legislative history of the statute made clear that the $2,000 fine was established to serve as a deterrent and was not remedial. Therefore, as the imposition of the $2,000 fine was grossly disproportional to the offense charged, it amounted to an excessive fine under amend. VIII and § 5. Accordingly, the sculptor was entitled to art. 78 relief.


OUTCOME: The judgment was modified to grant the petition to the extent of vacating the fine; and, as so modified, the judgment was otherwise affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Forfeitures and Penalties -- Unenforceable Penalty -- Excessive Fine -- Unlawfully Removing Recyclable Object from Trash Pile

1. The mandatory $2,000 fine imposed upon petitioner pursuant to Administrative Code of the City of New York §16-118 (7) (f) (1) (i) for his first offense of unlawful removal of a single television antenna from the top of some curbside garbage bags and placing the antenna in his vehicle and driving away, in violation of section 16-118 (7) (b) (1), was vacated as grossly disproportional to the gravity of the offense. Both the Eighth Amendment to the US Constitution and article I, § 5 of the NY Constitution prohibit "excessive fines." To be constitutional, the amount of the fine must bear some relationship to the gravity of the offense that it is designed to punish. Therefore, a court should consider the seriousness of the offense, the severity of the harm caused, the potential harm had the defendant not been apprehended, the maximum fine to which the defendant could have been subject, and the defendant's economic circumstances. Here, the seriousness of the offense was relatively minor, as it involved taking a single piece of metal, abandoned by its owner, which likely had little value to the City. There was no significant harm caused by petitioner's conduct, and no potential harm to the owner of the antenna, or anyone else in the area, had petitioner not been caught. Moreover, because the statute contained no discretion and mandated a $2,000 fine in all circumstances, petitioner was essentially subject to the maximum punishment for the offense, even though he took a minimal amount of material, and was neither a commercial dealer nor someone taking items in bulk, parties whom the statute was intended to deter. Finally, the record established that petitioner had limited financial resources.

Constitutional Law -- Cruel and Unusual Punishment -- Applicability of Constitutional Prohibitions against Excessive Fines to Civil Penalty

2. The constitutional prohibitions against excessive fines (US Const, 8th Amend; NY Const, art I, § 5) apply to a civil penalty imposed for the unlawful removal of a single television antenna from the top of some curbside garbage bags and placing the antenna in a vehicle and driving away, in violation of Administrative Code of the City of NY § 16-118 (7) (b) (1). The relevant inquiry is not whether the fine arose in the civil or criminal context, but whether the fine constituted punishment. Where a civil fine serves, at least in part, deterrent and retributive purposes, it is considered punitive and subject to the prohibitions against excessive fines. The requirement that a mandatory $2,000 fine be levied for a violation of section 16-118 (7) (b) (1) could not fairly be viewed as solely remedial. The sizeable sanction is assessed regardless of the amount or value of the recyclable materials taken and bears no relationship to the actual loss sustained by the City of New York as a result of the violation. Moreover, the legislative history of the statute made clear that the fine was established to serve as a deterrent.

Matter of Jayden QQ. (Christopher RR.)


513777


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


105 A.D.3d 1274; 964 N.Y.S.2d 280; 2013 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2722; 2013 NY Slip Op 2790



April 25, 2013, Decided 
April 25, 2013, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a Family Ct Act art. 10-A matter, appellant father appealed a judgment entered by the Family Court, St. Lawrence County, New York, which granted respondent child services agency's (CSA) motion to be relieved of its obligation to make further efforts to return the child to his father's care under Family Ct Act § 1039-b(b)(6) because the father's parental rights had been involuntarily terminated with regard to two of the child's half siblings.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court held that while the neglect petition was filed only against the mother, § 1039-b was not limited to the respondent named in the neglect petition. The father was on notice of the neglect case and participated in it. He was advised of his right to address custody issues and that a termination of parental rights petition could be brought against him regardless of whether he was a named respondent under Family Ct Act § 1035(d). The statute and its underlying justification supported the CSA's authority to make the motion. Section 1039-b(b)(6) did not unconstitutionally distinguish between individuals whose parental rights were involuntarily terminated and those who rights were voluntarily surrendered. An involuntary termination led to an adoption that relieved the biological parent of all parental duties and responsibilities for the child. A voluntary surrender might involve ongoing contacts between the biological parent and child. Section 1039-b(b)(6) was properly limited to individuals whose rights to further contact with their other children had been terminated. Section 1039-b's procedures did not offend due process. The procedures employed here were adequate.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Parent, Child and Family--Termination of Parental Rights--Termination of Agency's Reasonable Efforts to Reunite Child with Parent

Matter of Oakwood Prop. Mgt., LLC v Town of Brunswick


514922


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


103 A.D.3d 1067; 960 N.Y.S.2d 535; 2013 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1288; 2013 NY Slip Op 1310



February 28, 2013, Decided 
February 28, 2013, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Leave to appeal denied by Matter of Oakwood Prop. Mgt., LLC v Town of Brunswick, 21 NY3d 853, 2013 N.Y. LEXIS 1120 (N.Y., Apr. 30, 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner business brought a CPLR art. 78 proceeding and declaratory judgment action against respondent town, seeking to annul a determination of a zoning board of appeals (ZBA) and a declaration that a designation on a zoning map was unconstitutional. The Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), denied the landscaper's summary judgment motion, and granted the town's cross-motion for, inter alia, summary judgment. The business appealed.


OVERVIEW: The business asserted that the town was estopped from prohibiting it from conducting grinding and mulching operations on the subject parcels. The appellate court found, however, that estoppel could not have been invoked against a municipality to either (1) prevent it from discharging its statutory duties, (2) ratify administrative errors, or (3) preclude it from enforcing its zoning laws. The conduct alleged did not rise to the level of fraud, misrepresentation, or deception. The estoppel claim was properly rejected. Even if the ZBA violated the Open Meetings Law, Public Officers Law art 7, pursuant to Public Officers Law § 107(1), its actions with respect to the appeals were not void but, rather, voidable upon good cause shown. In light of the substantial public input at a prior hearing and the parties' extensive documentary submissions, the business failed to establish good cause to invalidate the ZBA's determination. The ZBA's interpretation of a designation on the town's zoning map was not irrational or unconstitutionally vague. The ZBA rationally and properly found that the mulching operation was not a permitted use on the parcel lying within the zone.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Estoppel--Estoppel against Government--Enforcement of Municipal Zoning Laws

Public Officers--Open Meetings Law--Executive Session of Zoning Board of Appeals--Failure to Establish Good Cause Warranting Invalidation of Board's Actions

Municipal Corporations--Zoning--Clarity of Designations Depicted on Zoning Map

9101, 2721/09


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT

January 29, 2013, Decided 
January 29, 2013, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Leave to appeal granted by People v. Golb, 20 N.Y.3d 1099, 988 N.E.2d 533, 2013 N.Y. LEXIS 1045, 965 N.Y.S.2d 795 (2013)
Motion granted by Maggese v. Stoia, 134 S. Ct. 908, 187 L. Ed. 2d 791, 2014 U.S. LEXIS 636 (U.S., 2014)
Affirmed in part and modified in part by, Dismissed by, in part, Remitted by People v. Golb, 23 N.Y.3d 455, 2014 N.Y. LEXIS 1093, 991 N.Y.S.2d 792, 15 N.E.3d 805 (2014)
Writ of habeas corpus granted, in part, Writ of habeas corpus denied, in part Golb v. AG of N.Y., 2016 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 7774 (S.D.N.Y., Jan. 21, 2016)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury in the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), convicted defendant of identity theft in the second degree, Penal Law § 190.79(3) (2 counts), criminal impersonation in the second degree (14 counts), forgery in the third degree (10 counts), aggravated harassment in the second degree (3 counts), and unauthorized use of a computer. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The convictions arose out of defendant's use of emails to impersonate actual persons. Defendant was the son of an expert on the Dead Sea Scrolls, and set up email accounts in which he pretended to be other scholars who disagreed with the father's opinion on the Scrolls. Among other things, defendant sent emails in which one of his father's rivals purportedly admitted to acts of plagiarism. The appellate court found that the evidence established that defendant never intended any kind of parody and that defendant intended harm that fell within the plain meaning of the term "injure." This was not protected by the First Amendment. Defendant also intended to create specific benefits for his father's career. However, the theory of the identity theft conviction under the first count was that in the commission of second degree identity theft, defendant attempted to commit the felony of scheme to defraud in the first degree, Penal Law § 190.65(1)(b). However, there was no evidence that defendant intended to defraud one or more persons of property in excess of $ 1,000 or that he attempted to do so.


OUTCOME: The judgment was modified to the extent of vacating the identity theft conviction under the first count of the indictment and dismissing that count. Otherwise, the judgment was affirmed. The case was remitted for further proceedings.


HEADNOTES

People v. Mateo


102751


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


101 A.D.3d 1458; 956 N.Y.S.2d 699; 2012 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9073; 2012 NY Slip Op 9095



December 27, 2012, Decided 
December 27, 2012, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Leave to appeal denied by People v Mateo, 21 NY3d 913, 988 NE2d 893, 966 NYS2d 364, 2013 N.Y. LEXIS 1284 (N.Y., 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the County Court of Ulster County (New York) that convicted him of second-degree assault.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's conviction resulted from an alleged home invasion. Defendant did not deny being present at the scene, but contended that he merely gave a friend a ride as a favor and waited outside the home while the friend had a conversation with the victim's wife. Defendant testified that he only became involved after the friend and the victim's wife started pushing each other and the victim charged the friend with a billy club. The victim and his wife testified that, upon arriving at their rural, secluded home, the friend and defendant immediately attacked them, restrained them with duct tape, ransacked parts of their house, and stole money they kept in their freezer. The appellate court found, inter alia, that the inconsistencies in the descriptions of the events were fully explored at trial. In any event, defendant admitted that he twice struck the victim in the head with the billy club. Because defendant argued that he was justified in doing so because the victim attacked him with it first, the trial court duly instructed the jury on the justification defense. Consequently there was no basis to disturb the verdict as against the weight of the evidence.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Crimes--Assault--Home Invasion--Weight of Evidence

Matter of VTR FV, LLC v Town of Guilderland


514507


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


101 A.D.3d 1532; 957 N.Y.S.2d 454; 2012 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9104; 2012 NY Slip Op 9123



December 27, 2012, Decided 
December 27, 2012, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner owners appeal a judgment by the Albany County Supreme Court (New York) that granted motions by respondents, town, town board, and competitors, to dismiss their combined petition for CPLR art. 78 relief and a declaratory judgment that an amended local law was unconstitutional, null, and void.


OVERVIEW: The amendment to the local law expanded the definition of "nursing home" to include an assisted living facility and/or memory care facility, thereby enabling the owners' competitors to build a second assisted living or memory care facility within a planned unit development (PUD). The appellate court found, inter alia, that, notwithstanding the proximity of the owners' property and assisted care facility to the site affected by the amendment, the economic harm they alleged was insufficient to confer standing on them. The amendment did not constitute illegal spot zoning. Although the amendment might have benefitted the competitors, it was enacted for the general welfare of the community. Moreover, it did not effect a regulatory taking of the owners' property without just compensation. Rather, the amendment served a legitimate governmental interest in expanding affordable housing options for seniors in the area, in addition to increasing the tax base and creating jobs. Finally, there was no evidence that the change brought about by the amendment interfered in any way with the returns on the owners' investments. Thus, they were not entitled to art. 78 relief or a declaratory judgment.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Parties--Standing--Challenge to Amendment to Zoning Law

Municipal Corporations--Zoning--Amendment to Zoning Law Did Not Constitute Spot Zoning--Enactment for General Welfare of Community

Constitutional Law--Taking of Property--Regulatory Taking--Amendment to Zoning Law

People v Smart


880 KA 09-01305


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


100 A.D.3d 1473; 954 N.Y.S.2d 322; 2012 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7782; 2012 NY Slip Op 7782



November 16, 2012, Decided 
November 16, 2012, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Leave to appeal granted by People v. Smart, 21 N.Y.3d 914, 988 N.E.2d 895, 2013 N.Y. LEXIS 1325, 966 N.Y.S.2d 366 (2013)
Motion granted by People v. Smart, 21 N.Y.3d 940, 990 N.E.2d 133, 2013 N.Y. LEXIS 1371, 967 N.Y.S.2d 894 (2013)
Affirmed by People v. Smart, 2014 N.Y. LEXIS 904 (N.Y., May 1, 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment entered by the Monroe County Court, New York, which convicted him of burglary in the second degree under Penal Law § 140.25(2) and sentenced him as a persistent felony offender to an indeterminate term of incarceration of 20 years to life.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court held that defendant did not preserve his claim relating to the trial court's response to a jury note and direction that the court reporter read back certain testimony. Defendant's claim of error in admitting the grand jury testimony of a witness after conducting a Sirois hearing was rejected. Defendant's challenge to the constitutionality of New York's discretionary persistent felony offender sentencing statute was unpreserved and meritless. While the court did not err in sentencing defendant as a persistent felony offender, the sentence was unduly harsh as although a burglary offense under § 140.25(2) was a violent felony offense under Penal Law § 70.02(1)(b), defendant did not employ actual violence in the offense, even though he was confronted by the resident. With the possible exception of two misdemeanor convictions of resisting arrest and criminal possession of a weapon in the 1980's, and a 2001 burglary conviction, defendant never used or threatened violence in the commission of a crime. The sentence was reduced to an indeterminate term of incarceration of 15 years to life under CPL 470.20(6).


OUTCOME: The judgment was modified to reduce the sentence to an indeterminate term of incarceration of 15 years to life. As modified, the judgment was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes--Appeal--Preservation of Issue for Review--Challenge to Court's Handling of Jury Note

Crimes--Witnesses--Unavailability of Witness Procured by Defendant--Admission of Grand Jury Testimony

Crimes--Sentence--Harsh and Severe Sentence--Lack of Violence in Defendant's Criminal History

Matter of County of Nassau v. State of New York


513309, 513446, 513649


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


100 A.D.3d 1052; 953 N.Y.S.2d 339; 2012 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7173; 2012 NY Slip Op 7236



November 1, 2012, Decided 
November 1, 2012, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected December 7, 2012.
Leave to appeal denied by, in part, Leave to appeal dismissed by, in part Matter of County of Nassau v State of New York, 20 NY3d 1092, 2013 N.Y. LEXIS 565 (N.Y., Mar. 28, 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Both sides appealed the judgment of the court with regard to an action with regard to laws that regulated accessibility to voting systems.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners sought a declaration that Election Reform and Modernization Act of 2005 (ERMA) was unconstitutional, claiming that electronic voting systems were susceptible to tampering and thus create the risk of disenfranchisement and subversion of the democratic process. The appellate court found that on the absence of any proof that approval had been given, the commissioner lacked the capacity to maintain the present appeal on behalf of the Nassau County Board of Elections (NCBOE). The county could not claim that, by complying with ERMA, it would be forced to violate a constitutional prohibition, because it was the NCBOE -- not the county -- that was responsible for the implementation of the requirements of ERMA. Inasmuch as the county had neither alleged nor demonstrated that it played any role in implementing the statute it sought to challenge, it could not be said that the county itself would be forced to violate a constitutional proscription if obliged to comply with the statute.


OUTCOME: The appeals were dismissed. The judgment was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Elections--Board of Elections--Capacity of Single Election Commissioner to Bring Suit--Claims Raising Issues Affecting Board of Elections as Whole

Parties--Capacity to Sue--Municipality's Challenge to Constitutionality of State Statute Requiring Use of Electronic Voting Systems--Municipality Not Forced to Violate a Constitutional Proscription

 

344 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of London Terrace Gardens, L.P. v City of New York


7917-7918


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


101 A.D.3d 27; 953 N.Y.S.2d 28; 2012 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7026; 2012 NY Slip Op 7078



October 23, 2012, Decided 
October 23, 2012, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Leave to appeal denied by Matter of London Terrace Gardens, L.P. v The City of New York, 2013 N.Y. LEXIS 1205 (N.Y., May 7, 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff/petitioner owner appealed an order by the New York County Supreme Court (New York) that granted motions by respondents/defendants, city and department of housing preservation and development (HPD), to dismiss the owner's CPLR art. 78 petition and the plenary action, which sought to rescind its participation in a city's J-51 tax incentive program.


OVERVIEW: After the Court of Appeals decision in Roberts v. Tishman Speyer Props., L.P., 13 NY3d 270 (2009), the owner sought a declaration that it was no longer subject to the provisions of the J-51 program, including but not limited to any rent regulatory provisions contained therein. The owner claimed that it would not have applied for the benefits if, as a result of receiving the benefits, it would be precluded from exercising luxury decontrol. The appellate court found, inter alia, that there was no provision in the program for unilateral withdrawal from the program or for repaying the tax benefits in exchange for rescission from the program. The owner, under the guise of recission, was seeking a waiver of tax benefits, which was not permitted by 28 RCNY 5-03(f)(3)(ii). Any mistake as to the law by HPD regarding whether units could be decontrolled while receiving J-51 benefits was immaterial to its decision to accept the owner into the program. Accordingly, as the owner failed to state a cause of action under the Due Process and Takings Clauses of the United States Constitution, or pursuant to 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983, its art. 78 petition was properly dismissed.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and Tenant -- Rent Regulation -- No Rescission of Participation in New York City Tax Incentive Program -- Mistake 

1. Petitioner/plaintiff landlord could not rescind its participation in the City of New York's J-51 tax incentive program following the Court of Appeals' decision that owners of rent-stabilized apartments in New York City would not be able to take advantage of the luxury decontrol provisions of the Rent Stabilization Law while simultaneously receiving tax incentive benefits under the J-51 program (see Roberts v Tishman Speyer Props., L.P., 13 NY3d 270, 918 NE2d 900, 890 NYS2d 388 [2009]). There is no provision in the J-51 program for unilateral withdrawal from the program or for repaying the tax benefits in exchange for rescission from the program nunc pro tunc; rather, the rules of the City of New York provide that rent regulation requirements shall not be terminated by the waiver or revocation of tax benefits (see 28 RCNY 5-03 [f] [3] [ii]). Although the landlord contended that its participation in the program was the result of a mutual mistake in that it would never have opted to participate in the program had it known what the Court of Appeals would decide, and that therefore it was not seeking a waiver of previously accepted benefits but a finding that those benefits were void ab initio, the practical effect of the landlord's request would be a waiver which is not permitted. Further, the remedy of rescission was not available since the J-51 program is a tax benefit program--there was no contract or agreement to rescind (see RPTL 489); moreover, there was no mutual mistake of fact.

Landlord and Tenant--Rent Regulation--No Rescission of Participation in New York City Tax Incentive Program--Effect of Judicial Decision

2. Petitioner/plaintiff landlord could not rescind its participation in the City of New York's J-51 tax incentive program on the ground that its participation was based on a mutual mistake of the law on its part and on the part of the City's Department of Housing and Preservation and Development (HPD), which mistake was exposed by the Court of Appeals' decision in Roberts v Tishman Speyer Props., L.P. (13 NY3d 270, 918 NE2d 900, 890 NYS2d 388 [2009]), which held that the owners of rent-stabilized apartments in New York City were not entitled to take advantage of the luxury decontrol provisions of the Rent Stabilization Law while simultaneously receiving tax incentive benefits under the City of New York's J-51 program. Even though the State Division of Housing and Community Renewal, and perhaps also HPD, were under the same mistaken interpretation of the law as was the landlord prior to the Roberts decision, that interpretation was entirely unrelated to HPD's confirmation of the landlord's eligibility for the J-51 program. Thus, any mistake as to the law by HPD regarding whether units could be decontrolled while receiving J-51 benefits was immaterial to its decision to accept the landlord into the program. Moreover, CPLR 3005 does not permit a misreading of the law by any party to cancel an agreement, nor was it a basis for granting the landlord the relief of rescission from the J-51 program.

Landlord and Tenant -- Rent Regulation -- No Rescission of Participation in New York City Tax Incentive Program -- Constitutional Issues 

3. In a proceeding and action in which petitioner/plaintiff landlord sought to rescind its participation in the City of New York's J-51 tax incentive program following the Court of Appeals' decision in Roberts v Tishman Speyer Props., L.P. (13 NY3d 270, 918 NE2d 900, 890 NYS2d 388 [2009]), which held that the owners of rent-stabilized apartments in New York City were not entitled to take advantage of the luxury decontrol provisions of the Rent Stabilization Law while simultaneously receiving tax incentive benefits under the City of New York's J-51 program, the landlord failed to state a cause of action under either the Due Process and Takings Clauses of the United States Constitution or pursuant to 42 USC § 1983. While the landlord alleged that without rescission, it will have surrendered a valuable statutory right to receive luxury decontrol without having had an opportunity to know what the law was and to conform conduct accordingly, the decision in Roberts construed a statute and did not create a new legal principle; judicial construction of a statute which invalidates a regulation promulgated by an agency does not create a new legal principle.
