Spinney at Pond View, LLC v. Town Bd. of The Town of Schodack


513860


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


99 A.D.3d 1088; 953 N.Y.S.2d 314; 2012 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6972; 2012 NY Slip Op 7020



October 18, 2012, Decided 
October 18, 2012, Entered


HEADNOTES

Limitation of Actions--Four-Month Statute of Limitations--Challenge to Municipal Water and Sewer Charges

 

346 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Aveni


2010-10110


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


100 A.D.3d 228; 953 N.Y.S.2d 55; 2012 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6922; 2012 NY Slip Op 6968



October 17, 2012, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected December 4, 2012
Leave to appeal granted by People v. Aveni, 20 N.Y.3d 1059, 985 N.E.2d 920, 2013 N.Y. LEXIS 300, 962 N.Y.S.2d 610 (2013)
Motion granted by People v. Thomas, 22 N.Y.3d 1007, 2013 N.Y. LEXIS 3272, 980 N.Y.S.2d 378, 3 N.E.3d 716 (2013)
Appeal dismissed by People v. Aveni, 22 N.Y.3d 1114, 2014 N.Y. LEXIS 207, 983 N.Y.S.2d 768, 6 N.E.3d 1124 (2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Supreme Court, Westchester County (New York), of burglary in the second degree, criminally negligent homicide, criminal injection of a narcotic drug, criminal contempt in the first degree, and criminal possession of a controlled substance in the seventh degree, after his motion to suppress statements to law enforcement officials and physical evidence was denied. He appealed his convictions.


OVERVIEW: Defendant entered the burglary victim's home, contrary to a protection order, and injected the homicide victim with heroin, whom police falsely told him was alive but could die if he didn't state what drug he gave her. The appellate court held his responses were inadmissible because (1) a threat of a homicide charge elicited them, so (2) he did not voluntarily waive his self-incrimination privilege, denying due process, under N.Y. Const. art. I, § 6 and CPL 60.45(1) and (2)(a). His convictions for criminally negligent homicide, criminal injection of a narcotic drug, and possession of a controlled substance had to be vacated because only the improper statements supported them. Sufficient evidence supported criminal contempt because (1) he knew of the protection order, which existed when he entered the home, (2) the victim's consent was no defense, under Penal Law § 140.00(5), and (3) he admitted a prior conviction. Insufficient evidence supported burglary because (1) he allegedly intended to commit criminal possession of drugs, (2) only his improper statement linked him to drugs in the home, (3) no drug possession was shown, and (4) no contemporaneous intent to possess was shown.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment was modified by vacating convictions of burglary in the second degree, criminally negligent homicide, criminal injection of a narcotic drug, and criminal possession of a controlled substance in the seventh degree, and, as modified, the judgment was affirmed, and defendant's motion to suppress statements to police was granted.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Confession -- Police Officer's Deception and Threatening of Defendant 

1. In a prosecution for various crimes arising out of the death of a woman defendant was accused of injecting with heroin, the police, while interrogating defendant, exceeded permissible deception, such that defendant's statements to the police should have been suppressed because they were unconstitutionally coerced. When interrogating a suspect, the police may, as part of their investigatory efforts, deceive a suspect, and any resulting statement will not be suppressed for that reason alone; however, the deception used to obtain a statement must not be so fundamentally unfair as to deny due process. Here, the detectives questioning defendant not only repeatedly deceived defendant by telling him that the victim was alive, but implicitly threatened him with a homicide charge by telling defendant that the consequences of his remaining silent would lead to the victim's death, since the physicians would be unable to treat her, which "could be a problem" for him. While arguably subtle, the import of the detectives' threat to the defendant was clear: his silence would lead to the victim's death, and then he could be charged with her homicide. The detectives coerced defendant's confession by deceiving him into believing that the victim was alive and implicitly threatening him with a homicide charge. Faced with no acceptable option, defendant had no alternative but to talk to the police. By lying to defendant and threatening him, the detectives eviscerated any sense defendant may have had that he could safely exercise his privilege against self-incrimination and put the People to their proof. Therefore, defendant did not voluntarily waive his Fifth Amendment privilege as the detectives' threat overcame defendant's will and was so fundamentally unfair as to deny due process.

Crimes -- Criminal Contempt -- Violation of Order of Protection 

2. The evidence, viewed in the light most favorable to the prosecution, was legally sufficient to support defendant's conviction of criminal contempt in the first degree based on defendant's violation of an order of protection requiring him to stay away from his mother's home. The order of protection was issued against the defendant, who was present in court when it was issued and, thus, he had actual knowledge of the order. At trial, the defendant admitted to a special information which charged that he had been previously convicted of criminal contempt in the second degree. Although the trial testimony of defendant's mother indicated that she attempted to have the order of protection modified or vacated, it was indisputably in effect when defendant entered her home. Thus, the fact that defendant's mother may have permitted defendant to enter her home did not render the defendant's entry lawful (see Penal Law § 140.00 [5]). To find otherwise would subvert the very purpose of orders of protection, which is to protect victims of domestic violence.

Crimes -- Burglary -- Unlawful Entry -- Violation of Order of Protection 

3. In a prosecution for burglary arising out of defendant's entry into his mother's home and committing certain crimes there, viewing the evidence in the light most favorable to the prosecution, there was legally sufficient evidence to establish that defendant entered his mother's home unlawfully where there was a valid temporary order of protection issued against defendant for the benefit of his mother, which order was indisputably in effect when defendant allegedly committed the underlying crimes, and of which defendant was unquestionably aware.

Crimes -- Burglary -- Intent to Commit Specific Crime 

4. In a prosecution for burglary, the People generally do not need to prove that a defendant intended to commit a particular crime, but where the People expressly limit their theory of the defendant's guilt of burglary to the intent to commit a specific crime, they are bound to prove the defendant's intent to commit that particular crime. Accordingly, where the indictment, as amplified by the bill of particulars, expressly charged defendant, with respect to burglary, with the intent to commit the crime of criminal possession of a controlled substance in the seventh degree, the People had the burden of proving that defendant, at the time he unlawfully entered his mother's home in violation of an order of protection, intended to commit that specific crime while inside.

Crimes -- Controlled Substances -- Possession of Heroin 

5. The evidence was legally insufficient to support defendant's conviction of criminal possession of a controlled substance in the seventh degree arising out of the discovery of a bag containing a trace amount of heroin found in his bedroom in his mother's house. Although the trial evidence established defendant's regular use of that bedroom, and his close proximity thereto when he was taken into custody, no evidence was presented to establish that defendant possessed heroin on his person at the time of his arrest. Moreover, since defendant's mother's home had multiple bedrooms and occupants, any of whom could have easily accessed the defendant's bedroom, even viewing the evidence in the light most favorable to the prosecution, it was legally insufficient to establish that defendant constructively possessed heroin.

Crimes -- Burglary -- Intent to Commit Criminal Possession of Controlled Substance 

6. In a prosecution for burglary in which the People alleged that defendant unlawfully entered his mother's home in violation of an order of protection with the intent to criminally possess heroin, even if the People had established defendant's constructive possession of heroin recovered from his bedroom, they nevertheless failed to present legally sufficient evidence establishing that defendant had intended to commit the offense of criminal possession of a controlled substance in the seventh degree at the time he entered his mother's home. Since criminal possession of a controlled substance in the seventh degree is a "continuing offense" (see Penal Law § 10.00 [8]), viewing the evidence in the light most favorable to the prosecution, the People could not establish, beyond a reasonable doubt, that the trace amount of heroin found in defendant's bedroom existed at the moment of the defendant's unlawful entry into his mother's home. The bag containing the heroin could have been there for days, or placed there immediately before or after defendant's entry. Therefore, the People did not establish that any intent on the defendant's part to commit the offense of criminal possession of a controlled substance in the seventh degree was contemporaneous with his unlawful entry.

 

347 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Williams v Beemiller, Inc.


938 CA 11-02092


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


100 A.D.3d 143; 952 N.Y.S.2d 333; 2012 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6683; 2012 NY Slip Op 6695



October 5, 2012, Decided 
October 5, 2012, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at, Appeal dismissed by Williams v. Beemiller, Inc., 100 A.D.3d 155, 951 N.Y.S.2d 444, 2012 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6684 (N.Y. App. Div. 4th Dep't, 2012)
Reargument granted by, Amended by Williams v. Beemiller, Inc., 103 A.D.3d 1191, 962 N.Y.S.2d 834, 2013 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 672 (N.Y. App. Div. 4th Dep't, 2013)
Leave to appeal denied by Williams v. Beemiller, Inc., 103 A.D.3d 1190, 959 N.Y.S.2d 85, 2013 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 636 (N.Y. App. Div. 4th Dep't, 2013)
Motion granted by, in part, Motion denied by, in part Williams v. Beemiller, Inc., 2013 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 5417 (N.Y. Sup. Ct., Nov. 25, 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, student and father, appealed orders by the Erie County Supreme Court (New York) that granted motions filed by defendants, manufacturer, licensee, and distributor, to dismiss and thereafter denied their motion to renew and reargue in their action for negligence and violation of federal, state, and local laws.


OVERVIEW: The student was shot in the abdomen by defendant shooter, who apparently misidentified him as a rival gang member. The plaintiffs alleged that the manufacturer and the distributor supplied handguns to the licensee even though they knew or should have known that he was distributing those guns to unlawful purchasers for trafficking into the criminal market. The appellate court found, inter alia, that the matter fell within the general definition of a "qualified civil liability action" in 15 U.S.C.S. § 7903(5)(A). In addition, the plaintiffs sufficiently alleged that defendants knowingly violated various federal and state statutes applicable to the sale or marketing of firearms within the meaning of the predicate exception in § 7903(5)(A)(iii). Although the complaint did not specify the statutes allegedly violated, it sufficiently alleged facts supporting a finding that the defendants knowingly violated federal gun laws. Accordingly, as the action was not precluded by 15 U.S.C.S. § 7903(5)(A)(iii), the court erred in dismissing the complaint.


OUTCOME: The orders were unanimously reversed, the motions were denied, and the complaint was reinstated.


HEADNOTES

Products Liability -- Misuse of Product -- Firearms -- Protection of Lawful Commerce in Arms Act -- Predicate Exception

1. The Protection of Lawful Commerce in Arms Act (PLCAA) (15 USC § 7901 et seq.) did not preclude an action to recover damages for injuries sustained when plaintiff student was shot by a gang member, inasmuch as plaintiffs sufficiently alleged that defendants, the manufacturer, distributor, and dealer of the subject gun, knowingly violated various federal and state statutes applicable to the sale or marketing of firearms within the meaning of the PLCAA's predicate exception (15 USC § 7903 [5] [A] [iii]). While the PLCAA generally shields manufacturers and sellers of firearms from liability for harm caused by the criminal or unlawful misuse of their non-defective products (see 15 USC §§ 7901 [b] [1]; 7903 [5] [A]), a "qualified civil liability action" prohibited under the PLCAA does not include "an action in which a manufacturer or seller of a qualified product knowingly violated a State or Federal statute applicable to the sale or marketing of the product, and the violation was a proximate cause of the harm for which relief is sought" (15 USC § 7903 [5] [A] [iii]). Although the complaint did not specify the statutes allegedly violated, it sufficiently alleged facts supporting a finding that defendants knowingly violated federal gun laws. Plaintiffs alleged that, on at least four occasions, the dealer sold guns to a convicted felon who was prohibited from purchasing firearms (see 18 USC § 922 [d] [1]), via illegal straw purchases, and that one of those straw purchases included the gun used to shoot plaintiff. With respect to the manufacturer and distributor, the complaint sufficiently alleged that they were accomplices to the dealer's statutory violations in that they supplied guns to the dealer even though they knew or should have known that he was distributing those guns to unlawful purchasers for trafficking in the criminal market.

Courts -- Jurisdiction -- Long-Arm Jurisdiction -- Discovery 

2. In an action seeking damages for injuries sustained in a shooting, in which plaintiffs alleged that defendant firearms dealer unlawfully sold the subject gun in Ohio to a New York resident via a straw purchase and that the gun was later used to shoot and injure plaintiff in New York, the court erred in dismissing the action against the dealer for lack of personal jurisdiction. The complaint sufficiently alleged under CPLR 302 (a) (3) that defendant committed a tortious act outside New York that caused injury to a person inside New York and expected or reasonably should have expected that his sale of guns to a gun trafficking ring would have consequences in New York; plaintiffs were otherwise entitled to discovery on that issue. In opposing a motion to dismiss pursuant to CPLR 3211 (a) (8) on the ground that discovery on the issue of personal jurisdiction is necessary, plaintiffs need not make a prima facie showing of jurisdiction, but instead must only set forth a "sufficient start," and show their position not to be frivolous. Although plaintiffs' allegations that the dealer garnered significant revenue from gun sales to a New York resident did not establish that he "derive[d] substantial revenue from goods used or consumed" in New York (CPLR 302 [a] [3] [i]), plaintiffs made the requisite sufficient start by alleging that the purchaser operated a gun trafficking ring in New York, and that the dealer supplied over 140 guns to the purchaser and his associates, including the gun used to shoot plaintiff, within a period of months. Thus, plaintiffs were entitled to discovery to determine how many of the guns the dealer sold to the purchaser were trafficked into New York and whether that amount was sufficient to conclude that the dealer derived substantial revenue from guns used or consumed in New York. Further, jurisdictional discovery was necessary to determine the nature of the dealer's relationship with defendant distributor. If the distributor and dealer were a single enterprise or shared an agency relationship, as plaintiffs alleged, then the admittedly interstate character of the distributor could render the dealer amenable to jurisdiction in New York.

 

348 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Capital Dist. Regional Off-Track Betting Corp. v. New York State Racing & Wagering Bd.


512595


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


97 A.D.3d 1044; 949 N.Y.S.2d 523; 2012 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5694; 2012 NY Slip Op 5766



July 26, 2012, Decided 
July 26, 2012, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner corporations brought a combined proceeding pursuant to CPLR art. 78 and action for declaratory judgment, to, among other things, review a policy of respondent wagering board with regard to the granting of certain secondary pari-mutuel operator approvals. The Supreme Court, Schenectady County (New York), dismissed the application. The corporations appealed.


OVERVIEW: The corporations challenged the board's change in policy with regard to approval of simulcast agreements between New York tracks and out-of-state secondary pari-mutuel operators (SPMOs) that did not include a New York resident restriction. The appellate court found that the board's change in policy occurred in January 2008 and the record demonstrated that the corporations participated in a conference call with the board's counsel at the end of that month during which they were informed of the change and that agreements with out-of-state SPMOs had been approved without the resident restriction. As the gravamen of the corporations' claim was that the policy change put them at a competitive disadvantage, an actual and concrete injury occurred, if at all, in January 2008. Further administrative action as of that time would have been unavailing in light of the fact that certain agreements had already been approved. Thus, this proceeding commenced in February 2010 was untimely, as it was initiated well after the CPLR 217(1) four-month statute of limitations had expired.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Limitation of Actions--Four-Month Statute of Limitations--Challenge to Change in Policy by State Racing and Wagering Board

Matter of Association for A Better Long Is., Inc. v. New York State Dept. of Envtl. Conservation


514121


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


97 A.D.3d 1085; 949 N.Y.S.2d 291; 2012 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5700; 2012 NY Slip Op 5781



July 26, 2012, Decided 
July 26, 2012, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Leave to appeal granted by Matter of Association for a Better Long Is., Inc. v. New York State Dept. of Envtl. Conservation, 20 N.Y.3d 852, 980 N.E.2d 535, 2012 N.Y. LEXIS 3506, 956 N.Y.S.2d 485 (2012)
Affirmed in part and modified in part by Matter of Association for a Better Long Is., Inc. v. New York State Dept. of Envtl. Conservation, 23 N.Y.3d 1, 2014 N.Y. LEXIS 591, 988 N.Y.S.2d 115, 11 N.E.3d 188 (N.Y., Apr. 1, 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner town, limited liability companies (LLCs), and others, brought combined CPLR art. 78 proceedings and declaratory judgment actions challenging regulations issued by respondent department that required "incidental take" permits for activity likely to result in the "taking" of an endangered or threatened species. The Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), granted the department's motion to dismiss. The town and the LLCs appealed.


OVERVIEW: The town and the LLCs based their challenges to the regulations on their ownership of property that contained or was in proximity to endangered and threatened species. The town and the LLCs had no pending applications for a permit under the regulations, but claimed that their properties would have been affected by the amended regulations or that they could have been compelled to comply with them. The appellate court found, however, unlike owners affected by a zoning reclassification, these allegations were potential, speculative harm that was insufficient to confer standing. Nor could the LLCs have claimed standing pursuant to State Finance Law § 123-b. The LLCs did not identify any expenditure by the state that they sought to challenge, and the claims raised in their petition did not have a sufficient nexus to fiscal activities so as to allow for § 123-b standing. The LLCs did not qualify for common-law taxpayer standing either, as property owners subjected to the requirements of the regulations would have been able to challenge them.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Parties--Standing--Challenge to Environmental Regulations--Speculative Harm--Taxpayer Standing--Lack of Ripeness

351 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Empire State Ch. of Associated Bldrs. & Contrs., Inc. v M. Patricia Smith


647 CA 11-01813


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


98 A.D.3d 335; 949 N.Y.S.2d 549; 2012 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5375; 2012 NY Slip Op 5456



July 6, 2012, Decided 
July 6, 2012, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed in part and modified in part by Empire State Ch. of Associated Bldrs. & Contrs., Inc. v. Smith, 2013 N.Y. LEXIS 1432 (N.Y., June 6, 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, builders and builders associations, sued defendant state officials challenging the 2008 amendments to the Wicks Law, claiming the amendments violated the New York State and Federal Constitutions, and seeking, inter alia, judgment declaring the 2008 amendments to be unconstitutional and enjoining their enforcement. The Erie County, New York Supreme Court granted defendants' motion to dismiss. Plaintiffs appealed.


OVERVIEW: The amendments increased and created a three-tiered monetary threshold and provided a means for governmental entities to opt out of wicks law separate bidding requirements. Inter alia, the appellate court held that the Legislature acted within its province in determining, as a matter of statewide concern, that it was necessary to provide relief to all counties by easing the burdens of Wicks Law compliance. As to the three-tiered classification, nothing in the N.Y. Const. art. IX home rule provisions required classifications that would extend the benefits of the 2008 amendments equally. All that was required was that the classification be defined by conditions common to the class and related to the subject of the statute. Neither the wisdom behind the creation of the classification nor specific monetary thresholds was an appropriate subject of judicial fact-finding. Since plaintiffs sought declaratory relief, however, the proper course was not to dismiss the complaint but rather to issue a judgment in favor of defendants declaring that the 2008 amendments to the Wicks Law, to the extent they were challenged by plaintiffs, were valid and constitutional.


OUTCOME: The judgment was modified on the law by denying defendants' motion to the extent that it sought dismissal of the complaint, reinstating the complaint insofar as declaratory relief was sought, and granting judgment in favor of defendants.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Home Rule Powers -- Standing to Challenge State Legislation 

1. Plaintiff County of Erie had standing to challenge the 2008 amendments to the Wicks Law (L 2008, ch 57, part MM), insofar as they established a three-tiered monetary threshold for the cost of public construction projects subject to the Wicks Law's separate bidding requirements which took into consideration geographically-based differences in the costs of construction, as an allegedly unconstitutional impairment of its home rule powers protected under article IX of the New York State Constitution. The general rule that a local government may not challenge the constitutionality of an act of the legislature affecting its powers was not applicable. Undiscriminating application of the general rule would undermine the home rule protection afforded local governments in article IX of the Constitution by subverting the very purpose of giving the local governments powers which the legislature is forbidden by the Constitution to impair or annul except as provided in the Constitution.

Municipal Corporations -- Home Rule Powers -- Special Law Serving Substantial State Concern -- Monetary Thresholds for Public Construction Projects Subject to Wicks Law 

2. The 2008 amendments to the Wicks Law (L 2008, ch 57, part MM), insofar as they established a three-tiered monetary threshold for the cost of public construction projects subject to the Wicks Law's separate bidding requirements which took into consideration geographically-based differences in the costs of construction, were valid and constitutional under the home rule provisions of article IX of the New York State Constitution, notwithstanding that the monetary threshold constituted a special law and the enactment of the 2008 amendments was unaccompanied by a home rule message. Although the 2008 amendments constitute a special law inasmuch as the new monetary thresholds apply differently "in terms and in effect" to the counties classified within each tier (NY Const, art IX, § 3 [d] [4]), the procedural requirement of a home rule message (see NY Const, art IX, § 2 [b]) was not triggered because the subject matter of the amendments bears a direct and reasonable relationship to the substantial state concern underlying the Wicks Law, i.e., "assur[ing] the prudent and economical use of public moneys for the benefit of all the inhabitants of the state and . . . facilitat[ing] the acquisition of facilities and commodities of maximum quality at the lowest possible cost" (General Municipal Law § 100-a). Thus, the legislature acted by virtue of the powers reserved to it under article IX of the Constitution in enacting the amendments without a home rule message. The classification created by the 2008 amendments, distinguishing between the counties comprising New York City, its immediate suburbs, and the remainder of the State, is reasonable and related to the purpose of the amendments, which was to ease the burden that the Wicks Law imposes on local governments by eliminating smaller projects from the Wicks Law mandates. The requirement that the classification be defined by conditions common to the class and related to the subject of the statute was met, and neither the wisdom behind the creation of the classification nor the amount of the specific monetary thresholds chosen by the legislature was an appropriate subject of judicial fact-finding.

Contracts -- Public Works Contracts -- Project Labor Agreements -- Apprenticeship Training Program Requirement 

3. The apprenticeship training program requirement of Labor Law §222 (2) (e), which states that, "[w]ith respect to any contract for construction" meeting the Wicks Law monetary thresholds, the contracting governmental entity "shall . . . require that each contractor and subcontractor shall participate in apprentice training programs . . . that have been approved by the [D]epartment [of Labor]," does not apply to all contracts subject to the Wicks Law, but applies only to those contracts where the government entity has elected to utilize a project labor agreement (PLA) and thereby to opt out of the separate bidding mandate of the Wicks Law. Labor Law § 222, an integral part of the comprehensive Wicks Law reforms enacted in 2008, provides a means for governmental entities to opt out of the Wicks Law's separate bidding requirements altogether provided that there is a PLA complying with the terms of that section in place (see Labor Law § 222 [2] [b]). While Labor Law § 222 (2) (e) states that it applies to "any contract for construction," the quoted language does not refer to any contract subject to the Wicks Law but, rather, it refers to any contract subject to a PLA. That interpretation follows from the language of subdivision (2) (e), which refers to "[a]ny contract . . . with respect to each project undertaken pursuant to this section," i.e., pursuant to Labor Law § 222, "Project labor agreements." The heading of Labor Law § 222 resolves the ambiguity created by the language "any contract" used therein.

Administrative Law -- Administrative Interpretation -- Project Labor Agreements -- Apprenticeship Training Program Requirement 

4. The apprenticeship training program requirement of Labor Law § 222 (2) (e), which applies to contracts where the governmental entity has elected to utilize a project labor agreement (PLA) and thereby to opt out of the separate bidding mandate of the Wicks Law, does not require a contractor or subcontractor to maintain an apprenticeship training program of its own. The Department of Labor, which is charged with the enforcement of the Wicks Law, including the PLA provisions enacted in 2008 (see Labor Law §§ 2 [2]; 224 [1]), has concluded that, if a contractor or subcontractor enters into a PLA that meets the requirements of Labor Law § 222, those contractors and subcontractors who perform work under the PLA are deemed to be participating in apprenticeship programs within the meaning of that section. That interpretation, viewed in the light of the language and purpose of the statute, is reasonable.

Parties -- Standing -- Taxpayer's Action Challenging Wicks Law Amendments 

5. Plaintiffs did not have standing to bring a citizen taxpayer cause of action alleging that the 2008 amendments to the Wicks Law (L 2008, ch 57, part MM) waste taxpayer funds by excluding out-of-state contractors and minority-owned and women-owned businesses from qualifying to obtain work on public construction projects, and by inflating the cost of those projects. Plaintiffs' allegations amounted to no more than a claim that state funds were not being spent wisely, which was patently insufficient to satisfy the minimum threshold for standing under State Finance Law § 123-b (1). Moreover, plaintiffs failed to allege some specific threat of an imminent expenditure, and thus lacked standing to bring a citizen taxpayer action on that ground as well.

Constitutional Law -- Equal Protection of Laws -- Wicks Law Amendments 

6. With regard to causes of action alleging that the 2008 amendments to the Wicks Law (L 2008, ch 57, part MM) violate the State and Federal guarantees of equal protection of the laws, insofar as they established a three-tiered monetary threshold for the cost of public construction projects subject to the Wicks Law's separate bidding requirements which took into consideration geographically-based differences in the costs of construction, plaintiffs failed to allege sufficient facts to meet their burden to negate every conceivable basis which might support the 2008 amendments, whether or not the basis had a foundation in the record. Since the 2008 amendments neither interfered with the exercise of a fundamental right nor involved a suspect class, review was governed by the rational basis standard. The three-tiered monetary threshold met the more exacting standard of the home rule provisions of article IX of the New York State Constitution in that it bears a reasonable relationship to an accompanying substantial state concern. Further, plaintiffs failed to establish that those sections of the Labor Law sanctioning the use of project labor agreements unconstitutionally favor union contractors over nonunion contractors.

 

352 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Stickler


104734


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


97 A.D.3d 854; 948 N.Y.S.2d 696; 2012 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5259; 2012 NY Slip Op 5348



July 5, 2012, Decided 
July 5, 2012, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Leave to appeal denied by People v. Stickler, 20 N.Y.3d 989, 2012 N.Y. LEXIS 3911 (2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant's motion to dismiss an indictment charging him with vehicular manslaughter and driving while intoxicated alleging that Penal Law § 125.12 was unconstitutionally vague was denied by the County Court of Chemung County (New York). Defendant waived his right to a jury trial, and agreed to a bench trial conducted on stipulated facts. He was convicted and sentenced to five years of probation, with six months to be served in the county jail.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed. The appellate court held that the rebuttable presumption of § 125.12 did not render § 125.12 vague as it contained sufficient standards to afford a reasonable degree of certainty so that a person of ordinary intelligence was not forced to guess at its meaning, and to safeguard against arbitrary enforcement. The New York legislature had found that there was a rational connection between the fact that a defendant caused death or serious physical injury by operating a vehicle while impaired by alcohol and the presumption that it was the alcohol impairment that caused the defendant to operate the vehicle dangerously. Defendant stipulated that he operated a vehicle while unlawfully intoxicated, that the victim fell out of the vehicle as defendant corrected a deviation from the vehicle's course, that the victim died due to injuries sustained in the fall, and that defendant caused the victim's death while unlawfully intoxicated. However, the trial court erred by treating the statutory presumption as mandatory, rather than permissive, and did not determine whether defendant operated the vehicle so as to cause the victim's death and did so due to unlawful intoxication.


OUTCOME: The judgment was modified, by reversing defendant's conviction of vehicular manslaughter. The matter was remitted to the trial court for further proceedings not inconsistent with the appellate court's decision. As modified, the judgment was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes--Vehicular Manslaughter--Presumption of Intoxication--Burden Shifting--Statute Not Unconstitutionally Vague--Permissive Presumption

Roman Catholic Diocese of Albany, N.Y. v New York State Workers' Compensation Bd.


513936


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


96 A.D.3d 1288; 947 N.Y.S.2d 653; 2012 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5001; 2012 NY Slip Op 5062



June 21, 2012, Decided 
June 21, 2012, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, a self-insured employer, sought review of an order of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), which granted motions by defendants, the Special Disability Fund (SDF) and the Special Funds Conservation Committee, to dismiss the complaint, seeking reimbursement of workers' compensation benefits under assorted theories. A reimbursement request was denied as untimely under Workers' Compensation Law §§ 15(8)(h)(2) and 2(19), (20).


OVERVIEW: The employer requested reimbursement from the SDF for benefits paid to three claimants who were injured prior to 1994. The Workers' Compensation Board previously determined that the employer was entitled to reimbursement, and it was paid numerous times for benefits paid to those claimants. However, the particular requests were rejected as untimely by the Committee, based on a §§ 15(8)(h)(2) and 2(19), (20). Thereafter, the employer brought an action, asserting claims of actual and constructive trust, conversion, and unjust enrichment, and seeking various relief and a declaration that § 15(8)(h)(2)(B) was unconstitutional. The trial court granted defendants' dismissal motion. On appeal, the court agreed, noting that there was no constitutionally protected property interest in the assessments or in the SDF. There was no statutory language that created private rights of a contractual nature that were enforceable against the State. Further, the employer received constitutionally adequate process with respect to receipt of the benefits. Lastly, a takings claim failed, as it was the employer's own failure to file on time that caused the reimbursement right to become extinguished.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court.

Workers' Compensation--Special Funds--Special Disability Fund--Change in Limitations Period for Reimbursement Claims

Matter of State of New York v. Spencer D.


2011-06364


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


96 A.D.3d 768; 946 N.Y.S.2d 180; 2012 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4298; 2012 NY Slip Op 4350



June 6, 2012, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent State brought a Mental Hygiene Law art. 10 proceeding for the civil management of appellant patient, a sex offender allegedly suffering from a mental abnormality and requiring civil management. The Supreme Court, Westchester County (New York), among other things, granted the petition and directed that the patient be subject to a regimen of strict and intensive supervision and treatment. The patient appealed.


OVERVIEW: The patient argued that the term "mental abnormality" as defined in Mental Hygiene Law § 10.03(i) was unconstitutionally vague both on its face and as applied to him. However, the appellate court found that the terms "condition," "disease," and "disorder" in § 10.03(i) were sufficiently definite to give a person of ordinary intelligence a reasonable opportunity to make the determination. The State's expert psychiatrist concluded that the patient suffered from paraphilia NOS, a disorder of sexual arousal, based upon hebephilia, an attraction to post-pubescent teenage boys, which predisposed him to committing sexual offenses, and that the patient had serious difficulty controlling his conduct. The State's expert explained that while hebephilia was not a specifically defined subcategory of paraphilia listed in the DSM-IV, paraphilia NOS, which was a specifically defined subcategory of paraphilia, encompassed hebephilia. Under the circumstances of this case, the term "mental abnormality" as defined in § 10.03(i) was not unconstitutionally vague as applied. Thus, since there existed at least one constitutional application of the statute, it was not invalid on its face.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes--Sex Offenders--Civil Commitment or Supervision--Constitutional Challenge to Statutory Definition of "Mental Abnormality"

Matter of Chenango Forks Cent. School Dist. v. New York State Pub. Empl. Relations Bd.


512983


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


95 A.D.3d 1479; 944 N.Y.S.2d 665; 2012 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3718; 2012 NY Slip Op 3700; 193 L.R.R.M. 3511



May 10, 2012, Decided 
May 10, 2012, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed by Matter of Chenango Forks Cent. Sch. Dist. v. New York State Pub. Empl. Relations Bd., 2013 N.Y. LEXIS 1429 (N.Y., June 6, 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent Public Employment Relations Board (PERB) ruled that petitioner, a school district, violated Civil Service Law § 209-a (1)(d) by failing to negotiate the discontinuance of reimbursement of Medicare Part B premiums. Petitioner appealed.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court rejected petitioner's assertion that reimbursement of Medicare Part B premiums was not a "term and condition of employment" subject to mandatory negotiation. Petitioner had a duty to negotiate with the bargaining representative of current employees regarding any change in a past practice affecting their own retirement health benefits. On its past practice determination, PERB found a reasonable expectation by unit employees that they would receive reimbursement of Medicare Part B premiums upon their retirement. This finding was supported by the stipulated facts that petitioner reimbursed Medicare Part B premiums to active employees and retirees since at least 1980 and that, despite the fact that the health insurance coverage provided under the collective bargaining agreements subsequent to 1990 did not require such reimbursement, petitioner continued to do so until July 2003. Furthermore, numerous witnesses testified that they were made aware of petitioner's practice prior to the June 2003 announcement. PERB's conclusion that the employee union did not waive its right to negotiate was rational and supported by substantial evidence.


OUTCOME: The decision of PERB was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Labor Unions--Collective Bargaining--Improper Practice--Failure to Negotiate--Retirees' Health Benefits--Past Practice

Civil Service--Public Employment Relations Board--Improper Practice--School District--Reimbursement of Retirees' Health Benefits--Past Practice

Matter of Morris Bldrs., LP v. Empire Zone Designation Bd.


511897


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


95 A.D.3d 1381; 943 N.Y.S.2d 678; 2012 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3494; 2012 NY Slip Op 3486



May 3, 2012, Decided 
May 3, 2012, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Leave to appeal denied by Matter of Morris Bldrs., LP v. Empire Zone Designation Bd., 19 N.Y.3d 812, 976 N.E.2d 251, 2012 N.Y. LEXIS 2147, 951 N.Y.S.2d 722 (2012)
Motion to strike granted by Morris Builders, LP v. Empire Zone Designation Bd., 2012 N.Y. LEXIS 3583 (N.Y., Dec. 11, 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant builder appealed the revocation by appellee board of its certification as an empire zone business.


OVERVIEW: The court agreed that the Board's decision to uphold the revocation of the builder's empire zone certification was entirely rational, and that such revocation could not be made retroactive to January 1, 2008. The builder could not have anticipated that the program rules governing certification would be changed and had every reason to assume that it would continue to enjoy the benefits of certification so long as it continued to comply with the provisions in the then existing statutory enactments. Accordingly, having given due consideration to all of the relevant factors, the retroactive application of the amendments constituted an unlawful taking of the builder's property and, as such, violated its right to due process. Accordingly, the revocation of the builder's certification as an empire zone business could not be made retroactive to January 1, 2008, and the amendments could only be prospectively applied.


OUTCOME: The judgment was modified.


HEADNOTES

Counties--Industrial Development Agency--Revocation of Empire Zone Designation--Retroactive Application of Standards

Administrative Law--Administrative Review--Revocation of Empire Zone Designation--Due Process

 

357 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of WL, LLC v. Department of Economic Dev.


513318


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


97 A.D.3d 24; 943 N.Y.S.2d 661; 2012 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3504; 2012 NY Slip Op 3497



May 3, 2012, Decided 
May 3, 2012, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected June 15, 2012.
Appeal dismissed by Matter of WL, LLC v. Department of Economic Dev., 19 N.Y.3d 1021, 976 N.E.2d 245, 2012 N.Y. LEXIS 2163, 951 N.Y.S.2d 717 (2012)
Leave to appeal granted by Matter of WL, LLC v. Department of Economic Dev., 20 N.Y.3d 853, 2012 N.Y. LEXIS 3551, 957 N.Y.S.2d 689 (2012)
Affirmed by James Sq. Assoc. LP v. Mullen, 2013 N.Y. LEXIS 1401 (N.Y., June 4, 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), dismissed an application brought by petitioner limited liability company (LLC) which, in a combined proceeding pursuant to CPLR art. 78 and action for declaratory judgment, sought review of a determination of respondent Department of Economic Development (DED) revoking the LLC's certification as an empire zone business enterprise pursuant to General Municipal Law § 959. The LLC appealed.


OVERVIEW: The LLC argued that the DED should have taken into account the investment it made in May 2000 when it bought a building in a city empire zone, when it was a participant in the Empire Zones Development Program (EZDP), which was the predecessor to the Empire Zones Program (EZP). However, the appellate court held that the April 2009 amendments to the Empire Zones Act, General Municipal Law § 955 et seq., did not require that the DED examine every business annual report (BAR) filed by an entity, whether for the EZP or the EZDP. Instead, the amendments required only that the review include at least three BARs and, as such, implicitly authorized a limited review of the BARs filed by a program participant. § 959(a)(v)(6), (w). Since 2001 was the first tax year to occur after the EZP came into existence, it represented a logical starting point to review an entity's performance, and the determination not to include the LLC's 2000 BAR was not arbitrary or capricious. However, making the decertification retroactive to 2008 and, as a result, depriving the LLC of tax benefits and credits earned during the 2008 tax year, constituted an unlawful taking of its property without due process.


OUTCOME: The judgment was modified by reversing so much thereof as dismissed that part of the petition/complaint seeking a declaration that the April 2009 amendments to General Municipal Law § 959 may not have been applied retroactively to January 1, 2008. The petition/complaint was granted to that extent and it was declared that said amendments shall be applied prospectively. As so modified, the judgment was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- Exemptions -- Decertification of Empire Zone Business Enterprise -- Time Frame of Review

1. In reviewing petitioner's business annual reports (BARs) to determine if petitioner met the "1:1 benefit-cost test" (General Municipal Law §959 [a] [v] [6]) required to remain eligible for tax benefits as a participant in the Empire Zones Program (EZP), the Department of Economic Development's (DED) decision to limit its review to the 2001-2007 time period (see 5 NYCRR 11.9 [c] [2]) and to exclude petitioner's 2000 BAR filed with the predecessor Economic Development Zones Program (EDZP) was not arbitrary, capricious, or an unlawful exercise of its regulatory authority. The 2009 amendments to the Empire Zones Act do not require that the DED examine every BAR filed by a participating business enterprise, whether for the EZP or its predecessor, the EDZP. The amendments require only that the review include at least three BARs and, as such, implicitly authorize a limited review of the BARs filed by a program participant (see General Municipal Law §959 [a] [v] [6]; [w]). Moreover, since 2001 is the first tax year to occur after the EZP came into existence, it represents a logical starting point to review a business enterprise's performance while participating in that program. Furthermore, DED uniformly applied the 2001-2007 time frame to more than 8,000 companies, including those, like petitioner, that had been certified prior to 2001 as participants in the EDZP.

Taxation -- Exemptions -- Decertification of Empire Zone Business Enterprise -- Statutory Procedures -- Notice 

2. Petitioner was provided adequate notice of the Commissioner of Economic Development's determination to revoke its certification as a participating business enterprise in the Empire Zones Program (EZP), as well as the reasons for its decertification, as required by General Municipal Law §959 (w). By letter, the Commissioner informed petitioner that an examination of its business records had been conducted pursuant to the 2009 amendments to the Empire Zones Act. One month later, in another letter, the Commissioner informed petitioner that its certification was being revoked because it failed to meet the requirements of the "1:1 benefit-cost test" (General Municipal Law §959 [a] [v] [6]), and that petitioner had the right to appeal its decertification in writing to the Empire Zone Designation Board (see 5 NYCRR 14.2 [a], [b]). In addition, petitioner was on notice that its decertification review would be based on at least three business annual reports that it had filed with the EZP and that the Department of Economic Development had recently promulgated regulations limiting that review to the 2001-2007 time period (see 5 NYCRR 11.9 [c] [2]).

Constitutional Law -- Due Process of Law -- Decertification of Empire Zone Business Enterprise -- Hearing Not Required

3. The lack of a hearing before the Empire Zone Designation Board during petitioner's appeal of the Commissioner of Economic Development's determination to decertify it as a participating business enterprise in the Empire Zones Program did not deprive petitioner of due process. The Empire Zones Act (General Municipal Law §955 et seq.) does not require the Board to hold such a hearing, and petitioner was offered an opportunity to submit documents to the Board to support its claim that it was entitled to continued certification. The decision to decertify petitioner was based upon a mathematical computation performed by the Department of Economic Development on figures that petitioner provided in its business annual reports, and petitioner had not challenged the accuracy of that calculation. Given the nature and scope of petitioner's principal complaint, which was directed at the propriety of the Commissioner's decision to limit its review of petitioner to the 2001-2007 time period, a hearing was not required for petitioner to be able to fully address that issue in its submissions to the Board.

Constitutional Law -- Taking of Property -- Retroactive Application of Empire Zones Act Amendments -- Decertification of Empire Zone Business Enterprise 

4. The application of the 2009 amendments to the Empire Zones Act, making petitioner's decertification as a participating business enterprise in the Empire Zones Program (EZP) retroactive to 2008 and, as a result, depriving it of tax benefits and credits earned during the 2008 tax year, constituted an unlawful taking of its property without due process of law. Petitioner's certification as a participant in the EZP represented a property interest that was entitled to due process protection. Given that petitioner had fully complied with all the laws in place before the 2009 amendments were enacted and that none of the EZP participants were on notice that the rules governing certification would change, petitioner had every reason to expect that it could make investments in its business and rely upon the EZP's promise that it would receive commensurate tax relief. The additional state revenue generated by the retroactive application of the 2009 amendments, standing alone, could not justify governmental appropriation of private property. Moreover, respondents' objective of addressing alleged abuses in the EZP did not outweigh the prejudice resulting from severely penalizing an entity that had faithfully complied with the requirements of the EZP and legitimately earned its tax credits.

 

358 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Homestead Funding Corp. v. State of N.Y. Banking Dept.


513553


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


95 A.D.3d 1410; 944 N.Y.S.2d 649; 2012 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3506; 2012 NY Slip Op 3499



May 3, 2012, Decided 
May 3, 2012, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner mortgage bank appealed a judgment by the Albany County Supreme Court (New York) that dismissed its combined CPLR art. 78 and declaratory judgment application to review a determination by respondent Banking Department, imposing an annual general assessment upon it.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court found, inter alia, that the annual general assessments were a fee, not a tax, because the purpose of the assessments was to recover the Department's expenses related to regulating banks from the banks that were regulated not to raise revenue for the support of government generally. The same formula was applied to all mortgage banks. Although the Department had not previously included income from mortgage servicing and secondary market activities for purposes of calculating the annual general assessment, it was reasonable to include that income because it was indirectly related to a mortgage bank's license, and failing to include such income could allow banks to structure transactions so as to avoid paying fees to the Department. Nevertheless, as no rule was properly promulgated pursuant to State Administrative Procedure Act §§ 202, 203, N.Y. Const. art. IV, § 8, the assessment could not be enforced and had to be annulled. The Department was to determine the bank's annual general assessment based on a properly promulgated rule or upon the bank's individual facts and circumstances. Accordingly, the bank was entitled to art. 78 and declaratory relief.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed, the petition granted to the extent that the annual general assessment was annulled, the Department's methodology for determining the annual general assessment for mortgage banks was declared not arbitrary or capricious and did not result in an unconstitutional tax, and matter was remitted for further proceedings.


HEADNOTES

Banks and Banking--Mortgage Bank--Imposition of Annual General Assessment

Administrative Law--Rule Making--Lack of Formal Promulgation--Imposition of Annual General Assessment on Mortgage Bank by Banking Department

Matter of Chautauqua County Dept. of Social Servs. v. Rita M.S.


414 CAF 11-02254


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


94 A.D.3d 1509; 943 N.Y.S.2d 332; 2012 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3285; 2012 NY Slip Op 3306



April 27, 2012, Decided 
April 27, 2012, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Chautauqua County Department of Social Services filed suit against respondents, a father and a stepmother (parents), under Family Ct Act art. 4 seeking retroactive child support. A judgment was entered against the parents upon their default. The parents' motion to vacate the support orders was denied. The Chautauqua County Family Court, New York, denied the parents' objections and the affirmed the judgment. The parents appealed.


OVERVIEW: The motion to vacate only raised the issue of personal jurisdiction, not the underlying merits of the support orders. The court had personal jurisdiction based on the claim in the support petitions that jurisdiction was proper under the long-arm statute, Family Ct Act § 580-201(5). The parents failed to answer the petitions, move to dismiss them under CPLR 3211(a)(8), or appear in court in opposition to them. The parents waived any right to a hearing by submitting their motions on papers. The parents failed to preserve their jurisdiction claim. The court had jurisdiction over the parents under § 580-201(5) since the parents' voluntary decision to place the children with the aunt in New York after they were arrested for child abuse and their formal acts in effectuating that decision constituted more than mere acquiescence. The parents purposefully availed themselves of the privilege of conducting activities within New York, and the assertion of jurisdiction over the parents complied with due process. The claim that § 580-201(5) was unconstitutionally void was not preserved. The magistrate's error in refusing to consider the parents' reply papers on the motion to vacate was harmless.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Parent and Child--Support--Uniform Interstate Family Support Act--Jurisdiction over Nonresident--Children Residing in New York as Result of Acts or Directives of Nonresident

Village of Pomona v Town of Ramapo


2010-11976


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


94 A.D.3d 1103; 943 N.Y.S.2d 146; 2012 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3162; 2012 NY Slip Op 3181



April 24, 2012, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Decision reached on appeal by Matter of Village of Pomona v. Town of Ramapo, 2017 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7833 (N.Y. App. Div. 2d Dep't, Nov. 8, 2017)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff village filed an action against defendants, a developer and a town, seeking to annul the town's local law, which amended the town's zoning map. The developer and town moved to dismiss the petition under CPLR 3211 and 7804(f), arguing that the village lacked capacity to sue and lacked standing. The village challenged orders of the Supreme Court, Rockland County (New York), which granted the motions.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court held that pursuant to CPLR 3211(a)(3), the supreme court properly dismissed for lack of standing the cause of action seeking a declaration that the rezoning constituted improper and unconstitutional spot zoning inconsistent with the town's comprehensive plan. Villages had no interest in a town board's compliance with its comprehensive plan. The supreme court properly dismissed pursuant to CPLR 3211(a)(7) and 7804(f) the cause of action alleging that the rezoning violated General Municipal Law § 239-nn and thus, was affected by error of law. Notice of the town's public hearings was provided, and the village did participate in the public hearings relating to the subject zoning action. The supreme court should have denied those branches of the motions that were pursuant to CPLR 3211(a)(3), 3211(a)(7), and 7804(f) to dismiss the causes of action alleging violations of the State Environmental Quality Review Act (SEQRA), ECL art 8. The village established a demonstrated interest in the potential environmental impacts of the town's rezoning law and related resolutions sufficient to give it SEQRA standing.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the judgment in part and reversed the judgment in part.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations--Zoning--Proposed Amendments--Notice to Adjacent Municipality

Parties--Standing--Challenge by Adjacent Municipality to Rezoning Adopted by Town--Compliance with State Environmental Quality Review Act--Potential Threat to Community Character

 

362 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Putney v People


513001


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


94 A.D.3d 1193; 942 N.Y.S.2d 252; 2012 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2565; 2012 NY Slip Op 2544



April 5, 2012, Decided 
April 5, 2012, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal dismissed by Putney v. People, 19 N.Y.3d 1020, 976 N.E.2d 244, 2012 N.Y. LEXIS 2154, 951 N.Y.S.2d 716 (2012)
Leave to appeal denied by, in part, Appeal dismissed by, in part Alice Putney v. People of State of New York, Acting By & Through Power Auth. of State of New York, 2013 N.Y. LEXIS 1126 (N.Y., Apr. 30, 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff owners appealed orders by the St. Lawrence County Supreme Court (New York) that granted a motion by defendant New York State Power Authority (NYPA) to dismiss their complaint challenging an eminent domain appropriation as time barred, and thereafter denied their motions to amend and for reargument.


OVERVIEW: When NYPA determined that it no longer needed a fee interest in the parcels at issue, it offered the owners the opportunity to purchase them. Unhappy with the appraised value, the owners commenced the underlying declaratory action alleging that, among other things, the original takings in 1955 and 1964 were excessive, illegal, and unconstitutional. The appellate court found, inter alia, that the owners offered no authority upon which to conclude that the statute of limitations for their EDPL 207(A) challenge to the extent of the appropriation commenced at any time other than when title vested in NYPA. Nor did they advance any persuasive reason why they or their predecessors in interest could not have challenged the extent of the appropriations in a timely manner. Consequently, dismissal pursuant to the doctrine of laches and CPLR 217(1) was appropriate. The owners' motions to amend and for reargument were properly denied as they were based on their claim that the appropriations were excessive, which claim had no merit.


OUTCOME: The order dismissing the action was affirmed, and the appeal from the order denying the motions to amend and for reargument was dismissed.


HEADNOTES

Limitation of Actions--When Cause of Action Accrues--Condemnation of Land--50-Year-Old Development Project

Public Authorities--Power Authority of State of New York--Fraud and Continuing Trespass in Connection with 50-Year-Old Condemnation Proceedings

Brightonian Nursing Home v Daines


399 CA 11-01861


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


93 A.D.3d 1355; 941 N.Y.S.2d 396; 2012 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2213; 2012 NY Slip Op 2245



March 23, 2012, Decided 
March 23, 2012, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reversed by, Judgment entered by Brightonian Nursing Home v. Daines, 2013 N.Y. LEXIS 2805 (N.Y., Oct. 15, 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, health commissioner and governor, appealed a judgment by the Monroe County Supreme Court (New York) that denied their cross-motion to dismiss plaintiff nursing homes' amended complaint/petition, and declared that Public Health Law § 2808(5)(c) was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The court found, inter alia, that § 2808(5)(c) as written was unconstitutionally vague and improperly delegated legislative authority to the commissioner. The catchall provision granted the commissioner unfettered discretion in assessing equity withdrawal requests, and provided no standards to guide the commissioner in determining what factors were "appropriate" in reviewing such requests. The statute did not adequately apprise nursing home owners and operators of the standards used to assess their equity withdrawal requests and precluded meaningful judicial review. Therefore, as § 2808(5)(c) swept so broadly as to be irrational and arbitrary in view of the objective to be accomplished, i.e., ensuring the financial viability of nursing homes for the protection of their residents, it violated due process.


OUTCOME: The judgment was unanimously affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law--Validity of Statute--Review of Equity Withdrawal Requests by Nursing Homes--Improper Delegation of Legislative Authority to Commissioner of Health

Constitutional Law--Validity of Statute--Review of Equity Withdrawal Requests by Nursing Homes--Unconstitutionally Vague Catchall Provisions

364 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v Smith


202 KA 09-02653


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


95 A.D.3d 21; 940 N.Y.S.2d 373; 2012 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1983; 2012 NY Slip Op 1896



March 16, 2012, Decided 
March 16, 2012, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment by the Niagara County Court (New York) that convicted him, among other things, of first-degree burglary and first-degree robbery; defendant claimed that the trial court erred in denying his motion to suppress DNA evidence because he lacked notice of the application seeking to compel him to provide a buccal swab and because the police used excessive force to obtain the swab.


OVERVIEW: After defendant's DNA matched evidence collected in two prior crimes, the People obtained a warrant to compel him to provide a buccal swab. When the swab that was obtained was "compromised," a second order was issued. However, defendant was not provided with notice of the People's application for the second swab or served with the second order. Thereafter, when defendant refused to open his mouth, police officers applied a taser to his bare skin for several seconds in order to obtain the sample. The appellate court found, inter alia, that defendant's due process rights in N.Y. Const. art. I, § 6 were violated when he was not afforded an opportunity to appear before the court and contest the second order compelling him to submit to the swab. Defendant's failure to object to the first order compelling him to provide a buccal swab did not constitute a waiver to any subsequent such orders. In addition, it was objectively unreasonable for the police to tase defendant in order to force him into submission. Finally, as there were less-intrusive alternatives, the DNA evidence should have been suppressed as an unconstitutional search and seizure.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed, the motion was granted, and a new trial was granted.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Evidence -- DNA Identification Tests -- Due Process 

1. The People's failure to provide defendant with notice of their second application for a court order compelling him to submit to a second buccal swab after the DNA sample obtained from him pursuant to the first court order was compromised violated defendant's due process rights since he was not afforded an opportunity to appear before the court and contest the second order (US Const Amend XIV; NY Const, art I, §6). Due process requires the People's application for an order to compel a suspect to provide a DNA sample to be made upon notice to the suspect where, as here, there are no exigencies that might frustrate the purpose of the application. Defendant's failure to appear in opposition to the People's first application for a buccal swab, even though he received notice of that application, did not constitute a waiver of his due process rights with respect to any subsequent such orders inasmuch as each order constituted a bodily intrusion warranting notice and an opportunity to be heard. While the People may not need to make a showing of probable cause upon each successive application, defendant could contest, among other things, the need for further buccal swabs and the availability of less intrusive means of obtaining a DNA sample. Accordingly, defendant was entitled to suppression of the DNA evidence obtained pursuant to the second order.

Crimes -- Unlawful Search and Seizure -- Use of Taser to Obtain Buccal Swab for DNA Testing 

2. The use of a taser by police to obtain a buccal swab from defendant was objectively unreasonable under the circumstances, and, therefore, constituted excessive force under the Fourth Amendment, warranting suppression of the DNA evidence obtained. The record established that defendant posed no immediate threat to the safety of himself or the officers, willingly accompanied the officers to the police station, where he was placed in a secure room and handcuffed, and did not threaten, fight with, or physically resist the officers at any time. Defendant refused to open his mouth for, at most, 10 to 15 minutes after the police had asked him to comply with a court-ordered buccal swab of which he had no prior knowledge. Furthermore, there were no exigent circumstances to justify the failure to employ a less-intrusive alternative to obtain defendant's DNA, which, unlike blood-alcohol content or other types of evanescent evidence, was not susceptible to alteration, destruction or loss if not obtained in a timely manner. As a reasonable alternative to the use of the taser, the police could have arrested defendant for criminal contempt and then sought judicial approval to use physical force if necessary to extract the DNA sample.

Crimes -- Consolidation and Severance -- Offenses Defined by Same or Similar Statutory Provisions 

3. The trial court did not abuse its discretion in denying that part of defendant's omnibus motion seeking to sever the charges against him relating to two home invasions from the charges relating to a gas station robbery. Even though the home invasions and the gas station robbery were based upon different criminal transactions, the charges were properly joinable pursuant to CPL 200.20 (2) (c) because the offenses were defined by the same or similar statutory provisions. The record did not support defendant's contention that there was substantially more proof of his involvement in the home invasions than the gas station robbery (CPL 200.20 [3] [a]), where defendant was connected to both crimes by the presence of his DNA at or near the crime scenes, and no witnesses to either incident were able to identify defendant.

SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT

Matter of Association for Community Living, Inc. v. New York State Off. of Mental Health


512744


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


92 A.D.3d 1066; 937 N.Y.S.2d 743; 2012 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 880; 2012 NY Slip Op 888



February 9, 2012, Decided 
February 9, 2012, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal dismissed by Assn. for Community Living, Inc. v. N. Y. State Off. of Mental Health, 19 N.Y.3d 874, 969 N.E.2d 1162, 2012 N.Y. LEXIS 1251, 947 N.Y.S.2d 47 (2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner providers brought a CPLR art. 78 proceeding, to, among other things, review implementation of Medicaid exempt income requirements by respondent office of mental health (OMH). The Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), dismissed the application. The providers appealed.


OVERVIEW: The OMH informed the providers that it would seek to recoup Medicaid exempt income that they had received for the years 2003 through 2009. The providers argued that the OMH's Medicaid exempt income recoupment policy lacked statutory or regulatory authority and that the OMH's reliance on guidelines, rather than a rule or regulation, violated State Administrative Procedures Act. However, the appellate court found that the enactment of L 2010, ch. 111, pt. D, § 1, which expressly confirmed the OMH's existing authority to recoup Medicaid exempt income rendered these claims moot. Because the purpose of L 2010, ch. 111, pt. D was specific and the recoupment policy effected the intent of that statute, it was not necessary for the OMH to promulgate any formal rules or regulations before enforcing the recoupment policy. The providers also argued that the Medicaid exempt income that the OMH sought to recoup was an "overpayment" and, as such, that they were entitled to a hearing pursuant to 18 NYCRR 519.4 to assess the validity of the recoupment and to determine the amount each provider owed. However, when a mass change was effected, the providers had no right to a formal hearing.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Terminello v Village of Piermont


2010-10364


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


92 A.D.3d 673; 938 N.Y.S.2d 162; 2012 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1014; 2012 NY Slip Op 953

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, residential condominium owners and homeowner's associations (jointly the owners), and defendant village cross-appealed an order by the Rockland County Supreme Court (New York) that denied their respective motions for summary judgment in the owners' action to declare RPTL art. 19, as applied by Village of Piermont, N.Y., Loc. Laws No. 7 (2005), unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The owners alleged, among other things, that their tax assessments increased disproportionately to other similarly situated properties after the village's adoption of Local Law No. 7 and the 2005 revaluation figures, and that such disparate treatment was unconstitutional. The village was also entitled to and a declaration that art. 19 was not unconstitutional. The parties' remaining contentions were without merit.


OUTCOME: The order was reversed by granting the village's motion, and the matter was remitted for a declaration that art. 19 was not unconstitutional; the order was otherwise affirmed.

369 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of State of New York v. Enrique T.


6570


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


93 A.D.3d 158; 937 N.Y.S.2d 203; 2012 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 456; 2012 NY Slip Op 476



January 26, 2012, Decided 
January 26, 2012, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Leave to appeal dismissed by Matter of State of New York v. Enrique T., 18 N.Y.3d 976, 967 N.E.2d 703, 2012 N.Y. LEXIS 616, 944 N.Y.S.2d 478 (2012)
Appeal after remand at, Appeal dismissed by, in part Matter of State of New York v. Enrique T., 2014 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1362 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, Feb. 27, 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner State appealed an order of the Supreme Court, Bronx County (New York) finding the pretrial detention provisions of Mental Hygiene Law § 10.06(k) facially unconstitutional and ordering respondent sex offender's immediate release.


OVERVIEW: The Attorney General asserted that because Mental Hygiene Law § 10.06(k) incorporated a finding of dangerousness at the probable cause stage, release under strict and intensive supervision and treatment (SIST) represented a discretionary policy and was not constitutionally mandated. Thus, the Attorney General argued that committing the sex offender to a secure treatment facility pending trial did not violate any substantive or procedural due process guarantees, even if it was ultimately found that the sex offender required civil management under SIST. The appellate court held that a finding of probable cause to believe that a sex offender under Article 10 of the New York Mental Hygiene Law required civil management because of mental abnormality incorporated the necessary finding of dangerousness. The appellate court also noted that while the private interest was weighty, the risk of erroneous deprivation of such interest was limited by the statutory requirement of review prior to commencing a proceeding under Article 10, and by the statutory requirement of a judicial finding of probable cause to believe that a respondent is a sex offender requiring civil management.


OUTCOME: The order of the supreme court was reversed, and the order for sex offender's conditional release was vacated. The sex offender was ordered returned to detention pending completion of disposition pursuant to the Sex Offender Management and Treatment Act.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Premature Finding of Facial Unconstitutionality 

1. In a proceeding arising out of Mental Hygiene Law article 10 pertaining to sex offenders requiring civil commitment or supervision, Supreme Court erred in attempting to determine, sua sponte, the facial constitutional validity of Mental Hygiene Law § 10.06 (k), which provides for the pretrial detention of certain offenders. The issue of facial unconstitutionality was not before the court. Respondent did not argue it, and in any such argument, it would be the challenger's burden to so argue and establish unconstitutionality beyond a reasonable doubt. Facial invalidation is an extraordinary remedy which is generally disfavored, and courts must avoid, if possible, interpreting a presumptively valid statute in a way that will needlessly render it unconstitutional.

Crimes -- Sex Offenders -- Civil Commitment or Supervision -- Pretrial Detention 

2. Mental Hygiene Law § 10.06 (k), which provides for the pretrial detention of certain sex offenders, is not unconstitutional as applied to respondent, a convicted sex offender, as well as to that category of Mental Hygiene Law article 10 sex offenders who, at disposition, may be approved for release into the community under strict and intensive supervision and treatment (SIST). Article 10's definition of mental abnormality (Mental Hygiene Law § 10.03 [i]) incorporates a finding of a respondent's dangerousness, even if the word "dangerous" is not used in the statutory definition. Consequently, whether ultimately confined or released conditionally under SIST, all article 10 sex offenders are "detained sex offenders" where there is probable cause to believe they suffer a mental abnormality and where that is found at trial. The fact that a sex offender requiring SIST is defined as "a detained sex offender who suffers from a mental abnormality but is not a dangerous sex offender requiring confinement" (Mental Hygiene Law § 10.03 [r]), does not mean a sex offender who is not dangerous at all; rather, the statute should be interpreted to mean a sex offender who is not dangerous to the extent where his dangerousness must be managed by confinement. Significantly, the requirements of due process are met in providing for the civil commitment of sexual predators based on a finding of dangerousness resulting from a mental illness or mental abnormality that results in a serious lack of ability to control behavior, and article 10's scope encompasses only sex offenders who are dangerous enough to be confined without the need to consider less restrictive alternatives. Accordingly, the State may impose pretrial detention without showing that there were no lesser conditions than confinement to assure the safety of the community. The Legislature's decision to provide, as a discretionary policy, the less restrictive option of SIST to certain dangerous respondents after trial depends on implementing a carefully tailored and individualized program. (Mental Hygiene Law § 10.11 [a].) This does not create a mandatory constitutional obligation to offer such release before trial when the same safeguards and protections are absent.

Crimes -- Sex Offenders -- Civil Commitment or Supervision -- Pretrial Detention 

3. Mental Hygiene Law article 10, particularly Mental Hygiene Law § 10.06 (k), which provides for the pretrial detention of certain sex offenders, does not violate the New York State Constitution's mandate that mental health patients be treated in the "least restrictive setting." The statute states clearly that sex offenders in need of civil management are a different population from traditional mental health patients (Mental Hygiene Law § 10.01 [g]). "Mental abnormality" as used in the statute has no medical validity; it is a legal term containing an inherent meaning of dangerousness. Thus, while article 10 aims to treat the sex offender, it was nevertheless primarily enacted to protect the community from high risk sex offenders and, as in other statutes designed for the protection of the community, to remove the offender from the arena of possible action.

Appeal -- Academic and Moot Questions -- Mootness Exception -- Constitutionality of Pretrial Detention of Certain Sex Offenders Requiring Civil Management 

4. The issue of the constitutionality of Mental Hygiene Law § 10.06 (k), which provides for the pretrial detention of certain sex offenders requiring civil management, as applied to respondent against whom a civil management petition was filed, will not be moot upon his dispositional hearing based on the view that once a disposition is made, the appellate courts would no longer need to decide whether the Attorney General sustained his burden of establishing that lesser conditions than confinement would not suffice to protect the public in the respondent's case. The issue raised by the respondent's as-applied constitutional challenge, and determined against him, was whether the Attorney General had that burden at all with regard to article 10 sex offenders. Accordingly, it is a challenge the Attorney General will be certain to face again from sex offenders in the same category as respondent, or those where the propriety of strict and intensive supervision and treatment would be immediately apparent, and the temporary nature of pretrial detention makes it unlikely that the constitutional issue will be decided before a respondent's dispositional hearing. Therefore, as an issue likely to arise in other cases, but likely to evade review, it meets the exception to mootness.

SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT
