Roulan v County of Onondaga


1275 CA 11-00086


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


90 A.D.3d 1617; 936 N.Y.S.2d 417; 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9492; 2011 NY Slip Op 9696



December 30, 2011, Decided 
December 30, 2011, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion granted by Roulan v. County of Onondaga, 20 N.Y.3d 889, 980 N.E.2d 522, 2012 N.Y. LEXIS 3463, 956 N.Y.S.2d 474 (2012)
Motion granted by Roulan v. County of Onondaga, 20 N.Y.3d 1049, 985 N.E.2d 142, 2013 N.Y. LEXIS 239, 961 N.Y.S.2d 377 (2013)
Affirmed in part and modified in part by Roulan v. County of Onondaga, 2013 N.Y. LEXIS 838 (N.Y., Apr. 30, 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff challenger appealed the order of the Supreme Court, Onondaga County (New York), which denied plaintiff's motion for partial summary judgment and granted summary judgment dismissing the action to defendants, a county and its assigned counsel program. Plaintiff brought a declaratory judgment action seeking to invalidate the assigned counsel program, which was established pursuant to County Law art. 18-B.


OVERVIEW: The assigned counsel program plan was established by the county through a contract with the county bar association. Plaintiff contended that the contract and handbook containing the assigned counsel plan was illegal, ultra vires and/or a nullity and, as written, were unconstitutional. Plaintiff primarily asserted that the plan deprived criminal defendants of their right to counsel and violated County Law art. 18-B in several respects. First, the court found that the trial court erred by dismissing the declaratory judgment action rather than declaring the rights of the parties. In examining the plan, however, the court found that it was valid but for the provision regarding assignment by a court and client eligibility, which prohibited attorneys from representing nonincarcerated criminal defendants until there was a determination of their eligibility, and thus it required attorneys to violate the indelible right to counsel that attached at arraignment. Otherwise, the court found the plan statutorily authorized and properly approved as it existed.


OUTCOME: The court modified the order by invalidating the provision of the assigned counsel plan that prohibited attorneys from representing nonincarcerated criminal defendants. As modified, the order was otherwise affirmed.

372 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


A.J. Baynes Frgt. Contrs., Ltd. v Polanski


1296 CA 11-01428


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


90 A.D.3d 1630; 936 N.Y.S.2d 428; 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9499; 2011 NY Slip Op 9703



December 30, 2011, Decided 
December 30, 2011, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reargument denied by, Leave to appeal denied by A.J. Baynes Frgt. Contrs., Ltd v. Polanski, 940 NYS2d 517, 2012 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1974 (N.Y. App. Div. 4th Dep't, Mar. 16, 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs trucking companies sued defendants, a city, and its mayor, city council, and police chief, in the Supreme Court, Erie County (New York) for a declaration that City of Lackawanna, N.Y., Municipal Code § 215.53, restricting truck traffic in the city, was invalid and unconstitutional. The trial court granted the relief requested, and defendants appealed.


OVERVIEW: The ordinance barred trucks from a state route. The appellate court held the ordinance was invalid under Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1640(a)(10) because its truck route system provided no connection between the state route and the system. Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1640(a)(5) and (20), letting cities regulate trucks on their highways, did not permit the ordinance because they had to be harmonized with Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1640(a)(10). The ordinance was invalid under the Department of Transportation's (DOT) "access highway" regulations, 17 NYCRR 8000.7(a)(2), 8114.00(q), (ae), allowing truck traffic on access highways, because (1) it barred trucks from roads DOT designated as "access roads," (2) the city's authority in Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1640 did not trump DOT's authority to designate access highways in Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1627(b), limiting cities' authority to regulate truck traffic, and (3) § 1627(b) did not limit access roads to highways that were part of the state highway system. It was error to declare the ordinance unconstitutional because, having found it invalid on statutory grounds, it was unnecessary to reach the constitutional claim.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment was modified by vacating its provision declaring the ordinance unconstitutional, and, as modified, the judgment was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Local Laws--Validity--Ordinance Excluding Trucks from Municipal Highway Invalid--Failure to Provide Connection with State Route as Required under Vehicle and Traffic Law

Holland v City of Poughkeepsie


2010-05610, 2010-09699


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


90 A.D.3d 841; 935 N.Y.S.2d 583; 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9102; 2011 NY Slip Op 9277



December 20, 2011, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff patient sued defendants city, police department, and officer, seeking to recover damages for personal injuries and for civil rights violations pursuant to 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983. The Supreme Court, Dutchess County (New York), denied a motion filed by the city, the police department, and the officer which was for summary judgment dismissing six of the claims. The city, the police department, and the officer appealed.


OVERVIEW: The case arose from the officer's actions in an incident in which the patient, an epileptic, refused transport to the hospital after having suffered seizures. The appellate court found that the city, the police department, and the officer failed to eliminate a triable issue as to whether the officer's use of a taser to restrain the patient, either to arrest him or to restrain him for his own safety, was excessive. The trial court thus properly denied the motion as to the common law excessive force and assault and battery claims. With respect to the patient's disorderly conduct arrest, there was evidence that the patient was engaged in tumultuous behavior, but a triable fact issue remained as to whether a reasonable officer could have interpreted the patient's behavior as being motivated by an intent to cause public inconvenience, annoyance or alarm for purposes of Penal Law § 240.20(1). In addition, although the patient might nevertheless have recklessly caused public inconvenience, there was a triable fact issue as to whether the "public" was inconvenienced. These fact issues precluded summary judgment for the officer on the ground of qualified immunity.


OUTCOME: The order was modified by granting the summary judgment motion seeking to dismiss the first, third, and fourth causes of action alleging violations of § 1983 against the city, the seventh cause of action against the city, and the second and thirteenth causes of action insofar as asserted against the city and the officer. As so modified, the order was affirmed.

Sunrise Check Cashing & Payroll Servs., Inc. v Town of Hempstead


2010-05381


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


91 A.D.3d 126; 933 N.Y.S.2d 388; 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8565; 2011 NY Slip Op 8745



November 29, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Leave to appeal denied by, Leave to appeal dismissed by, Appeal dismissed by, Motion denied by Sunrise Check Cashing & Payroll Servs. v. Town of Hempstead, 19 N.Y.3d 848, 969 N.E.2d 220, 2012 N.Y. LEXIS 972 (2012)
Affirmed by Sunrise Check Cashing & Payroll Servs., Inc. v. Town of Hempstead, 2013 N.Y. LEXIS 125 (N.Y., Feb. 14, 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, operators of check cashing establishments, sued defendant town, seeking, inter alia, a judgment declaring that Hempstead, N.Y., Building Zone Ordinance art. XXXI, § 302(K), was void. The Nassau County Supreme Court (New York) granted the town's motion for summary judgment; plaintiffs appealed.


OVERVIEW: Section 302(K) banned check-cashing establishments within the town in any districts other than industrial and light manufacturing districts. Preexisting check-cashing establishments like plaintiffs' located in districts where such establishments would be banned under § 302(K) were required to terminate by amortization no later than five years after the effective date of § 302(K). In support of their claim that § 302(K) was void, plaintiffs argued that because the field was preempted by the State of New York, the town was precluded from enacting legislation in the same area. The appellate court agreed. Section 302(K) was invalid pursuant to the doctrine of conflict preemption. In adopting § 302(K), the town determined that check-cashing establishments were appropriate only in the town's industrial and light manufacturing districts. However, Banking Law § 369 delegated to the New York Superintendent of Banks the task of determining whether particular locations were appropriate for check-cashing establishments. Therefore, the town's attempt to control the determination of the appropriate locations of these establishments by enactment of § 302(K) was in conflict with state law.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the matter was remitted to the trial court for the entry of a judgment declaring that § 302(K) was void and of no effect.


HEADNOTES

Local Laws -- Preemption by State -- Town Regulation of Check-Cashing Establishments

A town ordinance that restricted check-cashing establishments to certain areas of the town, requiring preexisting establishments to terminate or relocate to industrial or light manufacturing districts in the town within five years, was invalid pursuant to the doctrine of conflict preemption. Through the enactment and amendment of Banking Law § 369, the Legislature specifically delegated to the Superintendent of Banks the task of determining whether particular locations were appropriate for check-cashing establishments. The ordinance purported to accomplish the same function delegated by the Legislature to the Superintendent of Banks by making a determination as to the appropriate location for check-cashing establishments. By permitting such establishments to be located only within the town's industrial and light manufacturing districts, the ordinance purported to divest the Superintendent of the authority to "determine whether there is a community need for a new licensee in the proposed area to be served" (Banking Law § 369 [1]). In adopting the ordinance, the town necessarily determined that, in its estimation, check-cashing establishments were only appropriate in certain districts. As a direct consequence of the ordinance, existing check-cashing establishments at locations in the town's business district, each of which was necessarily determined by the Superintendent to be appropriately located to serve a community need, would find themselves in violation of a provision of the town's ordinance. Because that violation did not exist under state law, and because the Legislature vested the Superintendent with the authority to determine appropriate locations for check-cashing establishments, the ordinance was preempted by state law.

 

376 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


James Sq. Assoc. LP v Mullen


1211 CA 11-00675


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


91 A.D.3d 164; 933 N.Y.S.2d 485; 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8279; 2011 NY Slip Op 8423



November 18, 2011, Decided 
November 18, 2011, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at James Square Assocs. LP v. Mullen, 89 A.D.3d 1533, 932 N.Y.S.2d 755, 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8246 (N.Y. App. Div. 4th Dep't, 2011)
Affirmed by James Sq. Assoc. LP v. Mullen, 2013 N.Y. LEXIS 1401 (N.Y., June 4, 2013)


HEADNOTES

Statutes -- Retroactive Application of Statute -- Empire Zones Act Amendments 

1. Under the 2009 amendments to the Empire Zones Act that revised certain eligibility criteria for business enterprises and directed the Commissioner of the Department of Economic Development to conduct a review of all businesses receiving tax credits under the Empire Zones Program to determine whether they should be decertified pursuant to the new eligibility criteria (General Municipal Law § 959 [a] [v] [5], [6]; [w]), the revocations of plaintiffs' certifications of eligibility were intended to be effective for the taxable year commencing January 1, 2008. Although the 2009 amendments were to "take effect immediately," when a statute is to take effect and whether the statute applies retroactively are distinct issues. Furthermore, while the Legislature has no power to declare, retroactively, that an existing statute shall receive a construction contrary to that which the statute would ordinarily have received, the Legislature's enactment of legislation in 2010 to clarify the effective date of decertifications was consistent with the 2009 amendments. The legislative history suggested that the 2009 amendments were intended, in part, to generate revenue during 2009-2010, revenue that would not be generated if those amendments were to be applied prospectively. In addition, each of the contemporaneous amendments to the Tax Law affecting carryover tax credits referred to the retention certificate issued to business enterprises that satisfied the new eligibility criteria set forth in General Municipal Law § 959 (a) (v) (5) and (6), and those Tax Law amendments were expressly effective retroactive to January 1, 2008. The Legislature's subsequent statement of its earlier intent was entitled to great weight.

Constitutional Law -- Due Process of Law -- Retroactive Application of Empire Zones Act Amendments -- Revocation of Tax Credits 

2. The retroactive application of the 2009 amendments to the Empire Zones Act that revised certain eligibility criteria for business enterprises receiving tax credits under the Empire Zones Program deprived plaintiff businesses of their property interests in violation of due process of law to the extent that the revocations of plaintiffs' certificates of eligibility were made effective for the taxable year commencing January 1, 2008. Plaintiffs had no warning that the criteria for certification were going to change, prospectively or retroactively, prior to April 2009, and had maintained their eligibility for the tax credits throughout the taxable year beginning January 1, 2008 pursuant to the criteria then in effect. They did not merely rely on the continuing tax credits, but were induced to conduct their businesses in a particular way in specified disadvantaged areas in reliance upon their availability. Under such circumstances, the tax credits induced action in reliance thereon and thus could not be invalidated by subsequent legislation. Finally, defendants failed to explain what legitimate public purpose was served by the retroactive application of the 2009 amendments. The only justification offered, that the retroactive elimination of the tax credits would generate additional revenue for the State, was insufficient as balanced against the inequity to plaintiffs.

378 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Shernise C. (Rhonda R.)


2010-08309, 2010-10076


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


91 A.D.3d 26; 934 N.Y.S.2d 171; 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8199; 2011 NY Slip Op 8355



November 15, 2011, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent administration for children's services (ACS) filed petitions against respondents, a child's stepfather and mother, alleging that the child was abused. Pursuant to Family Ct Act § 1027(g), the Kings County Family Court (New York) ordered ACS to arrange for a forensic medical examination of the child. Appellant, the child's attorney, filed a motion to vacate this order. The trial court denied the motion; the attorney appealed.


OVERVIEW: Just before she turned 14, the child gave birth to a baby; DNA testing established a 99.97 percent chance that the child's stepfather was the father. The appellate court noted that a victim such as the child was entitled to no less protection under the Fourth Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. IV, than her stepfather would enjoy as an accused. Adolescent vulnerability intensified the intrusiveness of the exposure and could result in serious emotional damage. Given the conclusive evidence of the child's sexual abuse by her stepfather provided by the DNA test results, the State's need to subject the child to a highly intrusive physical examination was so diminished as to render the search unreasonable under the Fourth Amendment, U.S. Const. amend IV. Therefore, the mandated application of 1027(g) to the child under the particular facts of this case was unreasonable and violated her Fourth Amendment rights.


OUTCOME: The order was reversed and the attorney's motion to vacate was granted.


HEADNOTES

Statutes -- Construction -- Avoidance of Constitutional Issue

1. In a child protective proceeding in which the allegedly abused child contended that Family Court Act § 1027 (g), which requires forensic medical examination in all cases of alleged child abuse, was violative of her Fourth Amendment rights, the statute should not be read as affording the Family Court discretion to dispose of exams where they are unnecessary or unwarranted. Under established principles of judicial restraint, courts should not address constitutional issues when a decision can be reached on other grounds; however, a court may not avoid a constitutional issue by interpreting a challenged statute in a manner which contravenes its plain wording and legislative purpose. A statute will not be read to dictate an absurd or unreasonable result, and when interpreting a statute, the judiciary must first look to its plain language, which here provides that the court shall order examinations in all abuse cases. Moreover, given the mandatory nature of an order directing an examination, the order authorizing and directing a medical examination of the subject child should not be vacated on the basis that the court ordered the child protective agency, rather than a court-appointed professional, to arrange for the examination, so as to avoid reaching the constitutional issue.

Parent and Child -- Abused or Neglected Child -- Physical Examination of Abused Child

2. The mandated application of Family Court Act §1027 (g), which requires forensic medical examination in all cases of alleged child abuse, was unreasonable and violative of an abused child's Fourth Amendment rights, where the child gave birth to a daughter when she was just shy of her 14th birthday, a DNA test established a 99.97% probability that the father was the subject child's stepfather, and more than two years had elapsed before the medical examination was ordered. Strip searches and visual body cavity searches implicate fundamental Fourth Amendment rights, and the alleged victim was entitled to no less protection under the Fourth Amendment than her stepfather would enjoy as an accused. The effect of a search on the child must be balanced against the state's "extraordinarily weighty" interest in protecting children, as well as its interest in protecting the rights of those accused of child abuse. The statute's examination policy is generally effective in discovering and preserving evidence of abuse; however, the state's interests did not require a medical examination of the subject child since DNA evidence of paternity already conclusively established that the stepfather sexually abused the child and indelibly preserved unequivocal evidence of the abuse. While a physical examination and colored photographs taken of the victim more than two years after the birth of her daughter have the possibility, albeit remote, of revealing additional evidence of abuse, the examination, as a tool of early and accurate diagnoses, had been rendered, at best, cumulative and, at worst, superfluous.

State of New York v Getty Petroleum Corp.


512283


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


89 A.D.3d 262; 933 N.Y.S.2d 114; 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7604; 2011 NY Slip Op 7781



November 3, 2011, Decided 
November 3, 2011, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As corrected December 23, 2011


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent State sued appellant owner, seeking to recover costs allegedly incurred in an environmental clean-up of the owner's property. The owner counterclaimed, challenging the lien procedures. The Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), among other things, denied the owner's motion for summary judgment declaring the lien provisions of the Navigation Law to be unconstitutional. The owner appealed.


OVERVIEW: The owner argued that Navigation Law § 181-a required that, before a lien was filed, there had to be a judicial determination that the person who owned the property was liable to the fund. The owner asserted that the absence of the word "potentially" in § 181-a(1)(a) connoted a legislative intent to require a judicial determination of liability before an environmental lien could have been filed. The appellate court disagreed. The statute made no specific mention of a judicial determination as a prerequisite to filing a lien. The absence of the word "potentially" in § 181-a(1)(a) was insufficient to provide a sound basis for reading such a requirement into the statute. While the risk of erroneous deprivation due to an environmental lien was not reduced to the same level as matters supported by documentary proof, nevertheless the risk was low given that liability under the Oil Spill Act, Navigation Law art. 12, was strict, joint, and several, and the statute had been broadly and liberally applied to owners where a petroleum discharge was discovered. The owner failed to establish that the Oil Spill Act violated due process because it lacked a pre-filing hearing procedure.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Gas and Oil -- Oil Spill Cleanup -- Environmental Lien - Statutory Procedure

1. Navigation Law § 181-a did not require a judicial determination of liability before an environmental lien could be filed by the State upon real property owned by defendant for costs incurred by the Environmental Protection and Spill Compensation Fund (Fund) for the cleanup of a petroleum discharge. The statute makes no specific mention of a judicial determination as a prerequisite to filing a lien upon real property "owned by a person liable" (Navigation Law §181-a [1] [a]) to the Fund. Although the phrase "potentially liable" is used elsewhere in the Oil Spill Act (Navigation Law art 12), the absence of the word "potentially" in Navigation Law § 181-a (1) (a) is insufficient to read the requirement of a judicial determination of liability into the statute when read in the context of the entire statutory article. The Oil Spill Act was enacted to accomplish speedy, effective cleanups when spills occur and its provisions are to be "liberally construed to effect its purposes" (Navigation Law § 195). The environmental lien is tied to a sense of urgency to act, as the legislative history reveals a concern about a party frustrating Fund reimbursement by quickly disposing of the property. Thus, if the Legislature had intended to require a judicial determination of liability prior to the filing of an environmental lien, it would have affirmatively so stated and provided guidance as to the procedural issues pertinent to such a determination.

Gas and Oil -- Oil Spill Cleanup -- Environmental Lien -- Due Process

2. Defendant landowner failed to establish as a matter of law that the Oil Spill Act's failure to require a judicial determination of liability before the State filed an environmental lien upon its property pursuant to Navigation Law § 181-a for costs incurred by the Environmental Protection and Spill Compensation Fund for the cleanup of a petroleum discharge violated due process. While the environmental lien clouded title and affected defendant's ability to transfer the property, the risk of erroneous deprivation was low given that liability under the Oil Spill Act is strict, joint and several, and the statute has been broadly and liberally applied to owners of real property where a discharge is discovered. Defendant was given prior notice that the lien would be filed, the amount of the lien was established, and the lien was not filed until the underlying action was commenced. Although a prefiling hearing was not provided, defendant could challenge the lien within the context of the pending action or seek a vacatur under Lien Law § 59 (see Navigation Law § 181-d). Defendant failed to show that those procedures did not provide the expeditious postdeprivation review required by due process.

Eckardt v. City of White Plains


2010-09150


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


87 A.D.3d 1049; 930 N.Y.S.2d 22; 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6427; 2011 NY Slip Op 6548



September 20, 2011, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff arrestee sued defendants city and officer, seeking to recover damages for assault and battery, intentional infliction of emotional distress, negligent hiring and supervision, and for civil rights violations pursuant to 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983. The Supreme Court, Westchester County (New York), among other things, denied a motion which was for summary judgment filed by the city and the officer. The city and the officer appealed.


OVERVIEW: The arrestee alleged that, after his arrest, police officers unnecessarily used a taser on him several times while he was handcuffed. The appellate court found that the city and the officer failed to make a prima facie showing of their entitlement to judgment dismissing the assault and battery claim. A municipality may have been vicariously liable on such a state law claim under a respondeat superior theory. The city and the officer did, however, establish the city's entitlement to summary judgment on the intentional infliction of emotional distress claim, since such a claim could not have been brought against a governmental entity. However, the officer was not entitled to summary judgment on this claim. The actions complained of occurred during the arrest by several police officers in the scope of their employment with the city. Since the city was subject to vicarious liability, the arrestee was not allowed to proceed with a negligent hiring and supervision claim. The city and the officer made a prima facie showing that the police officers were adequately trained by the city with regard to the use of tasers. In opposition, the arrestee failed to raise a triable issue of fact.


OUTCOME: The order was modified by granting the motion of the city and the officer for summary judgment dismissing the second, third, and fourth causes of action insofar as asserted against the city and the third cause of action insofar as asserted against the officer. As so modified, the order was affirmed insofar as appealed from.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations--Tort Liability--Police--Assault and Battery

Torts--Intentional Infliction of Emotional Distress--Claim against Governmental Entity Barred

Employment Relationships--Respondeat Superior--Negligent Hiring and Supervision

Civil Rights--Federal Civil Rights Claim--Action against Municipality--Use of Tasers--Inadequate Training of Police Officers

Matter of Graves v Doar


2009-04567, 2010-02295


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


87 A.D.3d 740; 928 N.Y.S.2d 774; 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6256; 2011 NY Slip Op 6379



August 30, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at Matter of Graves v. Doar, 87 A.D.3d 744, 928 N.Y.S.2d 771, 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6264 (N.Y. App. Div. 2d Dep't, 2011)
Related proceeding at Davis v. Proud, 2014 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 30014 (E.D.N.Y., Mar. 5, 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant recipients brought an action challenging a decision of respondent commissioner which reduced the food stamp benefits received by the recipients. The Supreme Court, Nassau County (New York), granted the recipients' motion for summary judgment declaring that the New York State Group Home Standardized Benefits Program (GHSBP) was unconstitutional, and awarding the recipients retroactive benefits. All parties appealed.


OVERVIEW: The trial court declared that the implementation of the GHSBP violated the Equal Protection Clauses of the Fourteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 11. The appellate court found that, even under the rational basis test, the trial court properly awarded summary judgment for the recipients on the equal protection claim since the recipients established that there was no rational basis for providing higher allotments of food stamps to similarly-situated recipients of public assistance than to recipients of Supplemental Security Income. The trial court also properly limited the recipients' recovery to the restoration of monthly food stamp benefits which they would have received had the GHSBP not been implemented, as they were already entitled to this relief pursuant to their successful contention that the GHSBP was invalid by virtue of its implementation in the absence of a proper and formal rulemaking procedure. The trial court also properly, in effect, declared that the GHSBP did not violate N.Y. Const. art. XVII, § 1. The recipients failed to prove that additional proposed intervenors fit within the class definition.


OUTCOME: The order and interlocutory judgment was modified by deleting the provision thereof certifying the proposed intervenors as members of the class. As so modified, the order and interlocutory judgment was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Social Services--Food Stamp Allowance--Constitutionality of Group Home Standardized Benefits Program--Equal Protection

Civil Rights--Federal Civil Rights Claim

Actions--Class Actions

 

387 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of State of New York v Daniel Oo.


510835


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


88 A.D.3d 212; 928 N.Y.S.2d 787; 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6083; 2011 NY Slip Op 6196



August 11, 2011, Decided 
August 11, 2011, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Amended October 3, 2011.
Leave to appeal denied by Matter of State of New York v. Daniel OO., 2013 N.Y. LEXIS 2117 (N.Y., Aug. 29, 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner State brought a sex offender civil management petition pursuant to Mental Hygiene Law art. 10 against respondent patient. The Supreme Court, Franklin County (New York), granted the patient's motion to dismiss the petition. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: The patient moved to dismiss on the ground that due process precluded the State from pursuing civil management of a sex offender when the commission of a sex offense had not yet been proven and the patient lacked the capacity to stand trial with respect to that charge. The appellate court held initially that the proceeding was not rendered moot by a federal court proceeding. The State conceded that it had to prove, beyond a reasonable doubt, that the patient engaged in the conduct constituting the charged offense. Overriding the patient's significant liberty interest was the State's strong interest in both protecting the public from those who were dangerously mentally ill and providing treatment for those individuals if they were unable to care for themselves. The State had long had authority under its police power to protect the community from the dangerous tendencies of some who were mentally ill and this interest was of the highest order. The battery of protections in an art. 10 proceeding reduced the risk that the patient would have been erroneously deprived of liberty. Due process did not preclude the State from pursuing sex offender civil management of the patient.


OUTCOME: The order was reversed, the motion was denied, and the case was remitted to the trial court.


HEADNOTES

Appeal -- Academic and Moot Questions -- Federal Injunction Severing Unconstitutional Provision of State Statute 

1. A proceeding pursuant to Mental Hygiene Law article 10 seeking sex offender civil management of respondent, who lacked the mental capacity to stand trial for the underlying sexual assault and reached the maximum period of commitment possible under CPL article 730, was not rendered moot by a permanent injunction issued by a federal district court which mandated that the State, in order to comply with constitutional due process, prove beyond a reasonable doubt that an incapacitated sex offender committed the underlying sex offense before proceeding under Mental Hygiene Law article 10. On its face, the federal injunction did not prohibit the civil management of all sex offenders found incapacitated to stand trial under CPL article 730. Rather, the injunction severed the phrase "by clear and convincing evidence" from Mental Hygiene Law § 10.07 (d) and required the higher reasonable doubt standard, leaving the rest of the statute effective. Since a vacatur of the federal injunction on appeal would cause the statute to go back in full force and effect, the dispute over its validity was still live. In any event, the subject matter jurisdiction of the state court was not affected, since there is parallelism, but not paramountcy, between the state courts and lower federal courts with respect to passing on federal constitutional questions.

Constitutional Law -- Due Process of Law -- Civil Management of Dangerous Sex Offender Lacking Mental Capacity to Stand Trial 

2. Mental Hygiene Law § 10.07 (d), which permits the State to seek sex offender civil management of a respondent who lacked the mental capacity to stand trial and was never convicted of the underlying sex offense, does not deprive such a respondent of his or her right to constitutional due process, given the State's strong interest in providing treatment to sex offenders with mental abnormalities and protecting the public from their recidivistic conduct. A Mental Hygiene Law article 10 proceeding is civil in nature in which the primary inquiry is whether a respondent suffers from a mental abnormality requiring civil management or commitment, and, accordingly, does not run afoul of the blanket due process prohibition on trying incapacitated criminal defendants. Nevertheless, civil commitment for any purpose constitutes a significant deprivation of liberty for which due process requires the State to minimize the risk of an erroneous deprivation. A battery of procedural protections in Mental Hygiene Law article 10 ameliorates the risk that an incapacitated respondent will be erroneously deprived of liberty. A respondent is extended the right to counsel, extensive discovery, the right to be assessed by a psychiatric evaluator of his or her choosing, the right to call witnesses, introduce evidence and testify on his or her own behalf, and a unanimous verdict of 12 jurors is required to find him or her to be a detained sex offender suffering from a mental abnormality. Moreover, the State must demonstrate that respondent committed acts constituting a sex offense beyond a reasonable doubt. If those protections do not prevent a respondent's erroneous confinement, further remedies are available, including the right to apply for release at any time and to be evaluated annually, with the burden on the State to demonstrate that continued confinement is appropriate.

 

388 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


T.V. (Anonymous) v New York State Dept. of Health


2010-03689


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


88 A.D.3d 290; 929 N.Y.S.2d 139; 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6120; 2011 NY Slip Op 6229



August 9, 2011, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Before the birth of a child via surrogate, plaintiffs, the genetic and gestational parents, sought a judgment declaring the genetic parents to be the child's legal parents and enjoining the hospital and the department of health (DOH) from issuing a birth certificate identifying the gestational parents as the legal parents. The Supreme Court, Nassau County (New York), granted DOH's motion to dismiss under CPLR 3211(a)(7). The parents appealed.


OVERVIEW: The amended complaint sought to declare Domestic Relations Law art. 8, which was enacted to prohibit commercial surrogacy and to preclude a gestational carrier from being contractually compelled to relinquish her parental rights, and Family Ct Act §§ 517 and 542, allowing a genetic father, but not a genetic mother, to obtain an order of filiation, to be unconstitutional. The trial court erred in finding that it could not make a declaration as to whether the genetic mother was the legal mother of the subject child, and in dismissing the complaint for failure to state a cause of action. The trial court had the inherent authority under Family Ct Act art. 4 to determine whether a female was, in fact, a child's legal parent. An order relating to adoption was not necessary for the issuance of a new birth certificate pursuant to Public Health Law § 4138(1)(b). Furthermore, whether there was an impermissible gender-based classification between parents after the birth of the child was a valid question. Accordingly, the trial court erred in dismissing the complaint, finding that no cause of action was stated.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment, and denied the motion to dismiss the amended complaint.


HEADNOTES

Courts -- Supreme Court -- Jurisdiction to Issue Order of Maternity 

1. In an action to amend the birth certificate of a child born to a surrogate mother, who became pregnant after the egg of the genetic mother was fertilized with the sperm of the genetic father through in vitro fertilization and transferred into her uterus, the fact that article 5 of the Family Court Act addresses only "Paternity Proceedings" rather than maternity proceedings deprived neither the Family Court nor the Supreme Court of jurisdiction to issue an order of maternity. Such an approach is consistent with General Construction Law § 22 which mandates that the construction of statutes containing "gender indicative words" should be deemed to refer to "both male or female persons." Although there is no statutory provision providing for a declaration of maternity, there is also no provision prohibiting such declaration. Moreover, the Family Court has the inherent authority under article 4 of the Family Court Act to determine whether a female is, in fact, a child's legal parent. Consequently, the Supreme Court, as a court of general jurisdiction, has the authority to determine whether a child's genetic mother is indeed the child's legal mother.

Records -- Birth Certificate -- Amendment to Reflect Genetic Mother as Legal Mother -- Adoption Proceeding Not Required 

2. In a proceeding to amend the birth certificate of a child born to a surrogate mother, who became pregnant after the egg of the genetic mother was fertilized with the sperm of the genetic father through in vitro fertilization and transferred into her uterus, the Supreme Court had the authority to issue an order of maternity to the genetic mother absent her recourse to a formal adoption proceeding. Based on the plain reading of Public Health Law § 4138 (1) (b), an order relating to adoption is not necessary for the issuance of a new birth certificate. Once a court of competent jurisdiction issues an order or judgment "relating to the parentage," the Commissioner of the Department of Health, upon notification, "shall" issue a new certificate of birth pursuant to the statute. Although adoption was an alternate means to amend the birth certificate, adoptions are complicated and may be costly. The biological link between the child and the genetic mother provided a preexisting relationship that could not be ignored. The relief requested did not interfere with the reporting requirements of article 41 of the Public Health Law as plaintiffs only requested that a hearing be held shortly after birth as part of a declaratory judgment action to adjudicate the child's legal parentage, not an order that a gestational carrier's name never appear on a child's birth certificate. Moreover, a declaratory judgment action was the most appropriate mechanism to adjudicate legal parentage.

Records -- Birth Certificate -- Amendment to Reflect Genetic Mother as Legal Mother -- Equal Protection 

3. In an action to amend the birth certificate of a child born to a surrogate mother, who became pregnant after the egg of the genetic mother was fertilized with the sperm of the genetic father through in vitro fertilization and transferred into her uterus, plaintiffs stated a valid cause of action alleging that Family Court Act §§ 517 and 542 violated the genetic mother's rights to equal protection since the genetic father was able to obtain an order of filiation from the court while denying that same protection and enforcement of the same fundamental liberty interest to the similarly situated genetic mother, and that article 8 of the Domestic Relations Law, which was enacted to prohibit commercial surrogacy and to preclude a gestational carrier from being contractually compelled to relinquish her parental rights, violated due process if it were read to presume that the gestational mother was the legal mother of the child and to preclude the genetic mother from establishing her parental relationship to the child. The issue was not whether there is a distinction between males and females in the birth process, as there most assuredly is; rather, the issue as framed in the amended complaint was whether there was an impermissible gender-based classification between parents after the birth of the child.

 

389 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Yatauro v Mangano


2011-06696


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


87 A.D.3d 582; 928 N.Y.S.2d 561; 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6118; 2011 NY Slip Op 6230



August 9, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Amended August 17, 2011. As Amended August 24, 2011.
Leave to appeal denied by Yatauro v. Mangano, 17 NY3d 812, 954 NE2d 77, 2011 N.Y. LEXIS 2036, 929 NYS2d 788 (N.Y., Aug. 24, 2011)
Reversed by Yatauro v. Mangano, 17 NY3d 420, 955 NE2d 343, 2011 N.Y. LEXIS 2038, 931 NYS2d 36 (N.Y., Aug. 30, 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner legislators brought an action, inter alia, pursuant to CPLR art. 78 against respondent county, and others, challenging Nassau County, N.Y. Local Law No. 3 (2011). The Supreme Court, Nassau County (New York), granted summary judgment declaring that Local Law No. 3 was null and void, but ruled that the adoption of Local Law No. 3 was in accord with Nassau County, N.Y. Charter § 112. All parties appealed.


OVERVIEW: The trial court declared Local Law No. 3 null and void for lack of compliance with Nassau County, N.Y., Charter §§ 113 114. The legislators argued Local Law No. 3-2011, which redrew the metes and bounds of the county's 19 legislative districts based on the results of the 2010 decennial federal census, failed to comply with the requirements of §§ 112, 113, 114. The appellate court found that the trial court properly declared that the adoption of Local Law No. 3-2011 was in accordance with the requirements of § 112. The legislature, as required, adopted a local law amending Nassau County, N.Y., Charter Annex A to describe the 19 legislative districts, based upon the new census data, and did so within six months of the announcement of the census data. However, the trial court erred in concluding that the resultant metes and bounds only constituted new proposed legislative districts. In reaching this conclusion, the trial court overlooked a crucial term used in § 112. Local Law No. 3 was not inconsistent with, and did not purport to supersede, the Election Law in contravention of N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 2(c)(ii)(1) and Municipal Home Rule Law § 34(3)(g).


OUTCOME: The order was reversed insofar as appealed from, the legislators' motion for summary judgment on their first cause of action was denied, and it was declared that the legislative boundaries described in Nassau County, N.Y., Charter Annex A, as amended by Local Law No. 3, had to be implemented in connection with the general election to be held on November 8, 2011. The judgment was affirmed insofar as cross-appealed from.


HEADNOTES

Elections--Redistricting Plan--Validity of Local Law

Matter of CRP Sanitation, Inc. v Solid Waste Commn. of County of Westchester


2009-10430


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


86 A.D.3d 608; 927 N.Y.S.2d 384; 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5898; 2011 NY Slip Op 6029



July 19, 2011, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner/plaintiff solid waste hauler appealed a judgment by the Westchester County Supreme Court (New York) that denied its CPLR art. 78 petition, and, in effect, determined that Westchester County, N.Y., Laws § 826-a.700 was not unconstitutional, and dismissed its proceeding against defendants, County and County Solid Waste Commission (the SWC); the hauler claimed that § 826-a.700 was unconstitutionally vague and overbroad.


OVERVIEW: After the hauler's general manager was indicted on two counts-racketeering and racketeering conspiracy in connection with his alleged participation in an illegal "property rights" system in the carting industry, the SWC required the hauler to enter into a contract with an independent auditor pursuant to § 826-a.700 if it wished to continue its operations. The appellate court found, inter a alia that contrary to the hauler's contention, § 826-a.700 was not unconstitutionally vague or overbroad. The SWC had broad discretion to impose an independent auditor as a condition of the issuance, reinstatement, and/or renewal of a license. The conduct of the hauler's former general manager provided a rational basis for the SWC's determination that the hauler must enter into a contract with an independent auditor as a condition of renewal of its license. The hauler's remaining challenges to the constitutionality of § 826-a.700 were without merit. Since the hauler's action, was in part, a declaratory judgment action, the judgment should have included a provision declaring that § 826-a.700 was not unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The judgment was modified by adding thereto a provision declaring that § 826-a.700 was not unconstitutional; and, as so modified, the judgment was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law--Validity of Statute--County Law Requiring Commercial Carter to Enter Into Contract with Independent Auditor as Condition of License

Licenses--Commercial Carting License--Requirement That Commercial Carter Enter Into Contract with Independent Auditor as Condition of License--Rational Basis

Proceeding against Body or Officer--Mandamus--Clear Legal Right to Relief Required

Roberts v Health & Hosps. Corp.


4974, 112247/10, 112249/10, 112294/10


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


87 A.D.3d 311; 928 N.Y.S.2d 236; 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5733; 2011 NY Slip Op 5882



July 7, 2011, Decided 
July 7, 2011, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Leave to appeal denied by Roberts v. Health & Hosps. Corp., 17 N.Y.3d 717, 958 N.E.2d 1202, 2011 N.Y. LEXIS 3260, 935 N.Y.S.2d 287 (2011)
Motion granted by Roberts v. Health & Hosps. Corp., 17 N.Y.3d 935, 959 N.E.2d 514, 2011 N.Y. LEXIS 3269, 935 N.Y.S.2d 576 (2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated appeals from three CPLR art. 78 proceedings, respondent public benefit corporation appealed from orders of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), granting petitions to annul the public benefit corporation's decision to layoff carpenters, electricians, and laborers. Petitioners consist of elected officials, labor union representatives and union members (collectively, the unions).


OVERVIEW: The public benefit corporation sought to restructure its organization to make it more cost efficient. As part of this effort, it made the layoffs in dispute. The unions argued that the layoffs could have potentially created conditions that would have violated unspecified health laws and regulations. The trial court should have dismissed the petitions because the unions lacked standing and failed to state a claim for injunctive relief under Public Health Law § 2801-c where they relied upon threatened, not actual, violations of the public health laws. Section 2801-c authorized injunctive relief only for violations of specified provisions of the Public Health Law or regulations adopted thereunder, not for claimed violations of N.Y. Unconsolidated Laws or for unspecified violations. Further, the question of whether the scheduled layoffs would have left the public benefit corporation with a sufficient staff to satisfy its statutory obligations presented a nonjusticiable controversy. Finally, even assuming the unions had standing to challenge the layoffs, the layoff decision was not arbitrary and capricious and was founded on a rational basis pursuant to CPLR 7803(3).


OUTCOME: The court reversed the orders which granted the petitions to annul layoff determination, vacated the injunctions, denied the petitions, and dismissed the CPLR art. 78 proceedings.


HEADNOTES

Parties -- Standing -- Challenge to Decision by Public Hospital Corporation to Lay Off Certain Workers

1. Petitioners, elected public officials and members and officials of trade worker unions, failed to satisfy both the "injury in fact" and "zone of interest" prongs of the test to establish standing to challenge the decision of a public hospital corporation to lay off certain maintenance staff as violative of McKinney's Unconsolidated Laws of NY §§7382 and 7385 (7) (New York City Health and Hospitals Corporation Act [L 1969, ch 1016, § 1, as amended (HHC Act)] §§ 2, 5 [7]), which require the corporation to operate, manage, superintend, control, repair, maintain and otherwise keep up its health facilities, and Public Health Law §§ 2800 and 2803, as well as specified Department of Health Regulations promulgated thereunder (10 NYCRR 405.24, 702.1, 702.2, 702.3, 711.2, 711.4), which require the corporation to maintain its health facilities in a manner so as to assure a safe and suitable environment for patients. Petitioners' claim that the scheduled layoffs would leave the hospitals in question so short-staffed that the mandate of the Public Health Law to provide high quality health services would be violated was speculative. Petitioners' claims asserted only threatened, not actual, violations of the law, and pointed to no specific violation of any building code provision which would, as a result of the impending layoffs, actually occur and cause injury to them. Additionally, the asserted "injury" was neither separate nor distinct from that of the public at large. Nor were petitioners within the "zone of interest" sought to be promoted or protected by the statutory provisions under which the corporation acted: the regulations cited by petitioners that the corporation allegedly violated mostly provided for the benefit and protection of patients, with any benefits derived by hospital staff being incidental. Moreover, to the extent that certain regulations do mention staff and personnel, any claimed violations thereof remained wholly theoretical and unsubstantiated. Further, neither section 7382 nor section 7385 (7) of the Unconsolidated Laws of NY (HHC Act §§ 2, 5 [7]) imposes enforceable legal duties upon the corporation.

Courts -- Justiciable Questions -- Challenge to Decision by Public Hospital Corporation to Lay Off Certain Workers

2. The question of whether respondent New York City Health and Hospitals Corporation's scheduled layoffs of certain maintenance staff positions would leave the corporation without sufficient staff to satisfy its statutory obligations to operate, manage, superintend, control, repair, maintain and otherwise keep up its health facilities, and to maintain its health facilities in a manner so as to assure a safe and suitable environment for patients presented a nonjusticiable controversy. Inasmuch as the Legislature saw fit to give the corporation the discretion to determine the number of nonmanagerial employees necessary to carry out its mission (McKinney's Uncons Laws of NY § 7385 [12]; § 7382 [New York City Health and Hospitals Corporation Act (L 1969, ch 1016, § 1, as amended) § 5 (12); § 2]), its decisions regarding staffing levels were beyond judicial review. Petitioners failed to identify any law or regulations requiring the corporation to employ maintenance staff at a specific level or to determine maintenance staff levels in accordance with a particular standard or formula. Statutory requirements that public agencies maintain their facilities in a safe and sanitary condition do not give rise to judicially enforceable rights to employment of maintenance staff at any given level. Moreover, the statutory right to a safe workplace may not be enforced by means of a remedy at law which would require the judiciary to preempt the exercise of discretion by the executive branch of government.

Health -- New York City Health and Hospitals Corporation -- Judicial Review of Decision to Lay Off Certain Workers

3. The decision of a public hospital corporation to lay off certain maintenance staff in response to city budget cuts and other financial issues affecting its operations was not arbitrary and capricious, and was founded on a rational basis. There was no reason to apply the "substantial evidence" test to the corporation's determination since there was no administrative hearing held or required prior to the determination. The record showed that the layoff decision was rational in light of the imperative to reduce costs in conjunction with the corporation's statutory mandate to provide medical services to all. The undisputed facts showed that the corporation took its massive restructuring effort seriously, as evidenced by the creation of a high level steering committee and retention of the services of an outside consultant to assist in a review of all of its current operations. After a nine-month review, the consultant provided the steering committee with a voluminous report detailing 100 cost-cutting options as well as presenting the risks and mission impact of each. The committee thoughtfully reviewed those recommendations and selected 39. There was nothing that mandated the corporation to utilize any particular methodology in making its staffing determination. Moreover, there was nothing in the record to show that the layoff decision was arrived at in bad faith or without adequate facts or deliberation.

Brookhaven Baymen's Assn., Inc. v. Town of Southampton


2009-08735


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


85 A.D.3d 1074; 926 N.Y.S.2d 594; 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5515; 2011 NY Slip Op 5646; 2011 AMC 1668



June 28, 2011, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, commercial fishermen, women, and association (jointly, the fishermen), appealed from an order by the Suffolk County Supreme Court (New York) that denied their motion for a preliminary injunction enjoining the enforcement of certain local laws and granted a cross-motion by defendants, town, its board and trustees (jointly, the town), to dismiss the fishermen's complaint for failure to state a cause of action.


OVERVIEW: The local laws in question regulated who could take, and the manner of taking, "shellfish" from waters within the town. The fishermen alleged that the town impermissibly prohibited anyone but freeholders, residents, temporary residents, or taxpayers who possessed a permit issued by the town from taking crabs, conchs, and shrimp from the navigable waters of the town. The appellate court found, inter alia, that the original town patent did not grant the Trustees the exclusive rights to use the navigable waters in the town or to take migratory fish from them. The town board was empowered by Town Law § 130(19) to pass an ordinance prohibiting a trespass on both public and private property. However, the record was not sufficiently developed to determine whether the fishermen's methods of taking migratory fish constituted a trespass or if they could remove crabs and conchs without disturbing a town's land under the waters. Consequently, the fishermen were not entitled to a preliminary injunction to enjoin the enforcement of the local laws.


OUTCOME: The order was modified by deleting the provision thereof granting the cross-motion to dismiss, and by substituting therefor a provision denying the cross-motion; and, as so modified, the order was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Fish and Fisheries--Local Regulation--Validity of Local Law Prohibiting Taking Migratory Fish from Town Waters

Injunctions--Preliminary Injunction

People v. Foster


2009-04490


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


87 A.D.3d 299; 927 N.Y.S.2d 92; 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5602; 2011 NY Slip Op 5734



June 28, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Amended September 9, 2011.
Leave to appeal denied by People v. Foster (Wesley), 2011 N.Y. LEXIS 3847 (N.Y., Dec. 6, 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Upon defendant's guilty plea, the Supreme Court, Kings County (New York), convicted defendant of burglary in the second degree, criminal contempt in the first degree, and criminal contempt in the second degree. The trial court also entered a final order of protection. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the trial court lacked the authority to issue an order of protection which would remain in effect for eight years beyond the expiration of his five-year determinate term of imprisonment because he committed the offenses to which he pled guilty before CPL 530.12(5) was amended to increase the maximum duration of final orders of protection. However, the appellate court found that the legislative history made clear that the 2006 amendment increasing the maximum permissible duration of orders of protection issued pursuant to CPL 530.12(5), 530.13(4) was intended to provide greater protection to victims and witnesses, rather than to punish defendants. A statute which was enacted for nonpunitive purposes, and was not so punitive in effect as to negate that nonpunitive intent, may have been retroactively applied without violating the Ex Post Facto Clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 10(1). Application of the 2006 amendment extending the maximum duration of final orders of protection issued pursuant to CPL 530.12(5) to crimes committed before its effective date did not violate the Ex Post Facto Clause.


OUTCOME: The request to vacate the final order of protection was denied.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Appeal -- Waiver of Right to Appeal

1. Defendant's waiver of his right to appeal was unenforceable where the trial court's statement during the plea allocution that if defendant were to be "tried and convicted" he would have the right to appeal but that he was now giving up that right was misleading because it suggested that only defendants who are convicted after trial have a right to appeal. Furthermore, instead of eliciting a specific acknowledgment that defendant was waiving his right to appeal, the court followed up its explanation by asking defendant whether he was willing to "give up all those rights and any other rights" he possessed and now plead guilty. Without any indication in the record that defendant understood the distinction between the right to appeal and other trial rights which are automatically forfeited incident to a plea of guilty, it cannot be said that he knowingly, voluntarily, and intelligently waived his right to appeal.

Crimes -- Appeal -- Preservation of Issue for Review -- Discretionary Review of Unpreserved Issue

2. In a prosecution in which defendant's challenge to the duration of an order of protection was unpreserved for appellate review because he did not raise the issue at sentencing or move to amend the order of protection on that ground, the Appellate Division exercised the discretion it possesses to review unpreserved claims in the interest of justice (see CPL 470.15 [6] [a]) and reviewed defendant's challenge in order to clarify whether it was permissible to retroactively apply the 2006 amendment to CPL 530.12 (5), which extended the maximum duration of orders of protection, to crimes committed prior to its effective date.

Crimes -- Order of Protection -- Duration

3. The issuance of an order of protection is not a form of punishment and there is no constitutional impediment to the application of the 2006 amendment to CPL 530.12 (5), which extended the maximum duration of a final order of protection issued in favor of the victim of a felony family offense from five years to eight years, to crimes committed before the effective date of the amendment. It was entirely proper for a sentencing court to issue a final order of protection in accordance with the version of the statute in effect at sentencing, rather than the version in effect when the subject offenses were committed. The legislative history of the amendment made it clear that its intent was to provide greater protection to victims and witnesses, rather than to punish defendants. The amendment, which merely extended the permissible duration of orders of protection, was not so punitive in its consequences as to transform an order of protection into punishment. Moreover, there was no violation of the Ex Post Facto Clause of the United States Constitution (US Const, art I, § 10 [1]), which only applies to penal statutes which disadvantage the offender affected by them and does not prohibit retroactive application of a statute which is enacted for nonpunitive purposes, and is not so punitive in effect as to negate that nonpunitive intent.

Matter of Griffiss Local Dev. Corp. v. State of N.Y. Auth. Budget Off.


510005


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


85 A.D.3d 1402; 925 N.Y.S.2d 712; 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5229; 2011 NY Slip Op 5150



June 16, 2011, Decided 
June 16, 2011, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Leave to appeal denied by Griffiss Local Dev. Corp. v. State Auth. Budget Office, 17 NY3d 714, 957 NE2d 1159, 2011 N.Y. LEXIS 3149, 933 NYS2d 655 (N.Y., Oct. 25, 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner developer appealed a judgment by the Albany County Supreme Court (New York) that dismissed its combined CPLR art. 78 proceeding and declaratory judgment action, seeking to annul respondent budge office's determination that it was required to comply with the Public Authorities Accountability Act of 2005 (PAAA), L 2005, ch. 766; the developer claimed that the trial court erred in finding that it was a public authority.


OVERVIEW: The developer was a not-for-profit local development corporation whose sole purpose was the redevelopment of a former air force base. The appellate court found, inter alia, that the developer's stated public/quasi-public purposes included lessening the burdens of government and acting in the public interest. It was required to cooperate and coordinate with local governments and other state and local economic development organizations that may be appropriate. In addition, a majority of the developer's board was comprised of local officials. The close relationship between the developer and local governments persuaded the appellate court that the developer was the type of entity that the Legislature intended to subject to the provisions of the PAAA. As the developer was created by, sponsored by and/or affiliated with the local government within the meaning of the PAAA, it qualified as a "local authority" for purposes of Public Authorities Law § 2(2)(b). The developer's remaining contentions were considered and found to be unavailing.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Public Authorities--Claims against Public Authorities--Whether Not-for-Profit Local Development Corporation was Public Authority

Matter of Feminists Choosing Life of N.Y., Inc. v Empire State Stem Cell Bd.


511202


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


87 A.D.3d 47; 926 N.Y.S.2d 671; 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5047; 2011 NY Slip Op 5160



June 16, 2011, Decided 
June 16, 2011, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected June 20, 2011.
Leave to appeal denied by Matter of Feminists Choosing Life of N. Y., Inc. v. Empire State Stem Cell Bd., 2011 N.Y. LEXIS 3588 (N.Y., Dec. 13, 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a citizens group and members of its board of directors, filed a combined CPLR art. 78 proceeding and declaratory judgment action against respondents, the Empire State Stem Cell Board (ESSCB) and the New York Commissioner of Health, seeking to annul ESSCB's resolution permitting certain researchers to compensate oocyte donors. The Albany County Supreme Court (New York) dismissed the action; appellants sought review.


OVERVIEW: The members, but not the group, appealed the trial court's finding that petitioners lacked standing. The appellant court held that the members lacked standing under State Finance Law § 123-b as citizen taxpayers to challenge the resolution on the ground the standards developed by ESSCB's ethics committee were inadequate to ensure that the consent of compensated donors would be truly voluntary, as this was a challenge to the manner in which the donor compensation program was conducted, rather than to ESSCB's authority to conduct it. The members lacked common law standing as taxpayers on the informed consent issue, as there was no showing that an oocyte donor would be unable to challenge the procedures used to obtain her informed consent. The use of somatic cell nuclear transfer to create stem cells did not constitute "human reproductive cloning" within the meaning of Public Health Law § 265-a(2), and ESSCB's donor compensation program did not, as petitioners argued, improperly make grant funds available for such cloning.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Parties -- Standing -- Citizen Taxpayer Standing -- Challenge to Administration of Stem Cell Research Grants -- Egg Donor Compensation Program

1. The individual petitioners, members of the board of directors of the organizational petitioner, lacked standing as citizen taxpayers to challenge a resolution adopted by respondent Empire State Stem Cell Board (ESSCB), an agency created by the Legislature to administer the Empire State Stem Cell Trust Fund, to include in future grants to stem cell researchers a clause permitting researchers to compensate oocyte donors on the ground that the resolution failed to provide for fully informed consent. State Finance Law § 123-b, under which a citizen taxpayer may seek equitable or declaratory relief based on a wrongful expenditure of state funds, is narrowly construed and does not cover challenges to an agency's alleged mismanagement of state funds or the arbitrary and capricious distribution of state funds. Here, petitioners asserted that the informed consent standards developed byESSCB's ethics committee were inadequate to ensure that the consent of compensated donors would be truly voluntary. This was a challenge to the manner in which the donor compensation program was conducted, rather than to the ESSCB's authority to conduct it. As such, it was not the kind of challenge for which State Finance Law § 123-b confers standing. Further, petitioners did not have common-law taxpayer standing, which permits taxpayers to challenge certain governmental actions when the failure to accord such standing would erect an impenetrable barrier to judicial scrutiny. No such impenetrable barrier existed here, since no showing was made that an oocyte donor would be unable to challenge the procedures used to obtain her informed consent.

Statutes -- Construction -- Stem Cell Research Grants - Definition of "Human Reproductive Cloning"

2. Respondent Empire State Stem Cell Board's (ESSCB) interpretation of the term "human reproductive cloning," which is prohibited by Public Health Law § 265-a (2), as meaning an attempt to establish a pregnancy or the birth of a human child by transferring a human embryo created by somatic cell nuclear transfer (SCNT) into a woman's uterus, and not including "therapeutic cloning" in which SCNT is used to produce stem cells for research or therapeutic purposes, was not unreasonable or irrational. Since the Legislature did not provide a statutory definition of the phrase "human reproductive cloning" and its meaning is not commonly understood, ESSCB's administrative expertise in interpreting that technical term was entitled to deference.ESSCB's interpretation was well-established in the scientific community and in scientific literature, and consistent with stem cell research legislation defining "human reproductive cloning" in other states. Moreover, nothing in the Stem Cell Act or the record indicated that the Legislature intended the phrase "human reproductive cloning" to prohibit funding any stem cell research involving SCNT.

Statutes -- Construction -- Stem Cell Research Grants -- Scope of Prohibition against Human Reproductive Cloning Research

3. In authorizing the Empire State Stem Cell Trust Fund to compensate donors of oocytes that might be used to create stem cells using somatic cell nuclear transfer (SCNT), respondent Empire State Stem Cell Board (ESSCB) did not violate Public Health Law § 265-a (2), which provides that "[n]o grants made available in the fund from any source shall be directly or indirectly utilized for research involving human reproductive cloning," because knowledge derived from SCNT research could be indirectly utilized for research involving human reproductive cloning. As the statute is written, the adverb "indirectly" applies to the utilization of grants, not to the utilization of research results. Thus, the indirect use of grants for research involving human reproductive cloning does not equate with the use of state-funded research results by subsequent researchers who themselves receive no ESSCB funding. Further, a prohibition against funding any research that might indirectly advance human reproductive cloning would effectively include a broad range of stem cell research, contravening the legislative authorization to ESSCB "to make grants to ... activities that will advance scientific discoveries in fields related to stem cell biology" (Public Health Law § 265-a [1]).

 

398 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Karlsberg v. Tax Appeals Trib. of the State of N.Y.


509668


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


85 A.D.3d 1347; 925 N.Y.S.2d 237; 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4696; 2011 NY Slip Op 4788

399 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Frank v State of New York, Off. of Mental Retardation & Dev. Disabilities


511590


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


86 A.D.3d 183; 924 N.Y.S.2d 634; 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4504; 2011 NY Slip Op 4588



June 2, 2011, Decided 
June 2, 2011, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employee filed a whistleblower's action under Civil Service Law § 75-b and 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 after he was terminated from his job with defendant state agency. The Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), granted in part a motion to dismiss by the agency and defendant directors as time-barred under Labor Law § 740(4)(a) and barred by the election of remedies provision of § 740(7).


OVERVIEW: First, Civil Service Law § 75-b did not apply separately to individual public employees where the pertinent governmental entity was also sued. Therefore, the trial court did not err in dismissing the individuals named in the employee's claim because he sued them in their official capacities. However, the trial court erred in dismissing the employee's claim under § 75-b and 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983, alleging that his U.S. Const. amend. I rights were violated because he was terminated for speaking with the police and other investigators regarding a patient's death. The trial court erred in holding that Labor Law § 740(7), the election of remedies provision, barred the claim. Section 740(7), which was incorporated into actions against public employers under Civil Service Law § 75-b(3)(c), did not extend to exclude a potential 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 cause of action.


OUTCOME: The court modified the order by reversing that portion that dismissed the 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 claim, and affirmed the judgment as modified.


HEADNOTES

Labor -- Whistleblower Law -- Election of Remedies Exception 

1. In an action commenced pursuant to the public employee whistleblower statute (Civil Service Law § 75-b), plaintiff employee's 42 USC § 983 cause of action based on his claim that his employment with defendant Office of Mental Retardation and Developmental Disabilities was terminated because he spoke with police, the Office of the Inspector General and a patient's family regarding the patient's death, which allegedly resulted from improper actions by said defendant's employees, was improperly dismissed as being barred by the election of remedies provision of Labor Law § 740 (7). Section 740 (7), which addresses private employers, provides that an action instituted in accordance with section 740 is deemed a waiver of the rights and remedies available under any other contract, collective bargaining agreement, law, rule or regulation or under the common law, and is incorporated into actions against public employers under Civil Service Law § 75-b (3) (c). However, private employers are not susceptible to section 1983 claims as they do not typically engage in state action. Thus, a statutory exception for potential actions against a private employer would not include section 1983, and a statute applicable to public employers that borrows the exception for private employers with no expansion or clarification should not be extended to encompass a section 1983 action absent clear legislative guidance.

Labor -- Whistleblower Law -- Individual Liability 

2. Supreme Court properly dismissed individual defendants sued in their official capacity as public employees of defendant Office of Mental Retardation and Developmental Disabilities from plaintiff's cause of action alleging a violation of the public employee whistleblower statute (Civil Service Law § 75-b). Civil Service Law § 75-b does not apply separately to individual employees where, as here, the pertinent governmental entity is being sued directly.
