People v Morales


1142 & 2210/04, 6039


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


86 A.D.3d 147; 924 N.Y.S.2d 62; 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4459; 2011 NY Slip Op 4537



May 31, 2011, Decided 
May 31, 2011, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Leave to appeal granted by People v. Morales, 17 N.Y.3d 904, 957 N.E.2d 1163, 2011 N.Y. LEXIS 3457, 933 N.Y.S.2d 659 (2011)
Affirmed in part and reversed in part by People v. Morales, 2012 N.Y. LEXIS 3596 (N.Y., Dec. 11, 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Supreme Court, Bronx County (New York), convicting him, after a jury trial of first-degree manslaughter as a crime of terrorism, attempted second-degree murder as a crime of terrorism, second-degree criminal possession of a weapon as a crime of terrorism, and second-degree conspiracy, and imposing an enhanced sentence, pursuant to Penal Law § 490.25.


OVERVIEW: In the first prosecution for a crime of terrorism under § 490.25, a provision of the Anti-Terrorism Act of 2001, L 2001, ch. 300, defendant was charged with specified offenses as crimes of terrorism following a fight among rival gang members. The State alleged that defendant acted with intent to further the alleged purpose of the gang to intimidate or coerce a civilian population. It further claimed that individuals of a particular ethnicity living in a particular urban neighborhood constituted a "civilian population" within the meaning of § 490.25(1). However, the evidence was not legally sufficient to establish that defendant acted with the requisite intent to render his offenses crimes of terrorism. Even assuming in the State's favor that the Mexican-American residents of the area could have constituted a "civilian population," which it did not, the evidence was insufficient to support a finding that defendant committed the crimes with the intent to intimidate or coerce that civilian population generally, as opposed to the much more limited category of members of rival gangs. Accordingly, defendant's crime of terror convictions were reduced to the lesser included offenses.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment, reducing the convictions, and affirmed the judgment as modified. It remitted the case to the trial court with directions to resentence defendant on the reduced counts.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Terrorism -- Intimidation of "Civilian Population" 

1. In a prosecution arising out of defendant's shooting of a supposed rival gang member and a 10-year-old girl, the evidence was not legally sufficient to establish that defendant acted with the requisite intent to render his offenses crimes of terrorism. Even assuming that residents of a certain ethnicity in a particular area constituted "a civilian population" under Penal Law § 490.25 (1), the evidence was insufficient to support a finding that defendant committed his crimes with the intent to intimidate or coerce that "civilian population" generally, as opposed to the much more limited category of members of rival gangs. The only purposes of defendant's actions were those of asserting his gang's dominance over rival gangs in general and pursuing a vendetta against a particular rival gang. While defendant's gang sometimes preyed on area residents who were not gang members, there was nothing to suggest that defendant's actions were motivated by the desire to intimidate a particular ethnic community in a certain geographic area. The crime of terrorism is not established unless the alleged terroristic intent is connected to the particular specified offense underlying the charge, and the "civilian population" to be intimidated must be some group other than the direct victims of the crime.

Crimes -- Terrorism -- Intimidation of "Civilian Population" 

2. In a prosecution arising out of defendant's shooting of a supposed rival gang member and a 10-year-old girl, the evidence was not legally sufficient to establish that defendant acted with the requisite intent to render his offenses crimes of terrorism. Members of other rival gangs in the area in which defendant's gang operated did not qualify as "a civilian population" within the meaning of Penal Law § 490.25 (1). The context of the Anti-Terrorism Act weighs against stretching the meaning of its language to cover such a narrowly defined subcategory of individuals. Rather, the Legislature intended to address extraordinary criminal acts perpetrated for the purpose of intimidating a broad range of people, not a narrowly defined group of particular individuals whom the criminal actor happens to regard as adversaries. A minimum size of "a civilian population" need not be defined where the legislative findings implied an intent to create a pervasively terrorizing effect on people living in a given area, directed either to all of those residents or those residents who are members of some broadly defined class, such as a gender, race, nationality, ethnicity or religion.

Crimes -- Corroboration of Accomplice Testimony -- Evidence Tending to Connect Defendant with Crime 

3. In the prosecution of defendant arising from his shooting of a supposed rival gang member and a 10-year-old girl, the trial evidence was sufficient to support the judgment convicting him of attempted murder, manslaughter and weapon possession, and conspiracy to commit first-degree assault as lesser included offenses underlying terrorism charges based on the testimony of an accomplice who was a leader of defendant's gang, because that testimony found support in "corroborative evidence tending to connect the defendant with the commission of [the] offense[s]" (CPL 60.22 [1]). The chief corroborating evidence was the statements defendant gave to the police in which he admitted his membership in the gang, placed himself at the scene of the crime and admitted having held a gun there.

Crimes -- Fair Trial -- Judge's Remarks at Voir Dire 

4. In a prosecution for terrorism arising out of defendant's shooting of a supposed rival gang member and a 10-year-old girl, defendant was not deprived of a fair trial by the manner in which the court referred to the terrorist attack of September 11, 2001 (9/11). While it would have been preferable for the court to forgo the reference to jury service as a way to "speak back" to the 9/11 terrorists, it was highly unlikely that the jurors misinterpreted that hortatory rhetoric as an invitation to vindicate their own rage at the 9/11 terrorists by their treatment of defendant's case, thereby undermining the impartiality of the proceedings. Nothing in the court's remarks likened defendant to the 9/11 terrorists; on the contrary, the court specifically explained that defendant was not being charged with politically motivated terrorism. Significantly, the trial took place a full six years after 9/11, and defendant did not claim that anything the jurors learned of his background might have caused them to connect him to the 9/11 terrorists. Further, given the vast scale of the 9/11 catastrophe, the distinction between those attacks and the crimes charged was unmistakable.

Crimes -- Appeal -- Preservation of Issue for Review -- Strategic Waiver 

5. Defendant was not entitled to a new trial in the interest of justice based on the introduction of numerous hearsay statements at his trial on charges stemming from his shooting of a supposed rival gang member and a 10-year-old girl. Defendant not only failed to raise any objections, but also affirmatively waived them and sought to use the evidence in question for his own strategic ends. Accordingly, defendant, through counsel, intelligently and knowingly waived his Confrontation Clause claims.

Crimes -- Right to Counsel -- Effective Representation 

6. In a prosecution for terrorism arising out of defendant's shooting of a supposed rival gang member and a 10-year-old girl, the performance of defendant's lead trial counsel was not so deficient as to deny him effective assistance of counsel. In the absence of a complete record developed by a motion to vacate the judgment pursuant to CPL 440.10, it may be presumed that counsel exercised professional judgment and strategic discretion in determining how to conduct the defense. The existing record reflected that counsel followed a coherent strategy that sought to show that defendant committed no crime beyond weapon possession, a charge that he was unlikely to defeat given the denial of his suppression motion. Further, counsel competently attacked the credibility of the People's main witness, and brought out the inconsistencies between his testimony and that of other witnesses. Ultimately, counsel obtained an acquittal on the second-degree murder charge, the most serious count of the indictment.

Crimes -- Appeal -- Preservation of Issue for Review -- Inconsistent Verdict 

7. In a prosecution for terrorism arising out of defendant's shooting of a supposed rival gang member and a 10-year-old girl, defendant's claim that the verdict was irreconcilably inconsistent insofar as he was convicted of attempted second-degree murder with respect to the supposed rival gang member who was paralyzed, while he was acquitted of the second-degree murder charge with respect to the girl who was killed, was unpreserved as defendant failed to raise it before the jury was discharged. The failure to object to the verdict as inconsistent at the appropriate time may well have been a conscious tactical choice by defense counsel, since resubmitting the case to the jury to cure the inconsistency could have resulted in the acquittal on the murder charge being changed to a conviction. Accordingly, the claim was not reviewed in the interest of justice.

Crimes -- Confession -- Noncustodial Statements 

8. In a criminal prosecution, defendant's motion to suppress statements he made to the police was correctly denied. The police did not act improperly in entering defendant's apartment when they first approached him for questioning, since they entered the apartment with the implicit consent of an elderly man (apparently, defendant's stepfather) who met them at the door. The police did not have to give defendant Miranda warnings when they began to interview him after he voluntarily accompanied them to the precinct, since a reasonable innocent person in defendant's situation would have believed, at the inception of the interview, that the police, who never displayed their weapons, were still in the process of gathering information about the alleged incident. Accordingly, the suppression court properly concluded that defendant was not in custody when the interview began and that the police were not required to read the Miranda warnings at that point.
